Buyu — Sales and Trade

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Flour (Daqiq) vs Parched Grain (Sawiq)
Intra-Hanafi dispute on flour-sawiq exchange

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding exchanging flour for sawiq and identify the tarjih (preferred view).

Core Idea
Flour and sawiq: Abu Hanifa says same genus, Sahibayn say different; fatwa on weight but not count? Actually the dispute is on flour-sawiq.

The text presents a dispute within the Hanafi school: Abu Hanifa prohibits flour-sawiq exchange, analogizing to parched vs. unparched wheat, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow it, arguing they are different genera due to different names and purposes. The author provides a rebuttal favoring Abu Hanifa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa
Find 'ولا يجوز بيع الدقيق بالسويق متفاضلاً أو متساوياً، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة له'.
He prohibits both excess and equal.
2
Identify the ruling of Sahibayn
Find 'وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد الله : يجوز بيع الدقيق بالسويق كيف ما كان'.
They allow unconditionally.
3
Understand the reasoning for each
Abu Hanifa: both are from wheat; parched vs unparched analogy. Sahibayn: different names and purposes; one suits bread, other suits mixing with ghee.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The author refutes Sahibayn: nutrition is the main purpose, which is common; loss of minor purposes does not change genus.
The response cites Kamal al-Dirayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبسر بالتمر إلا متساوياً ، والبُرِّ بالدقيق أو بالسويق، أو الدقيق بالسويق متفاضلاً أو متساوياً، والزّيتون بالزيت، والسمسم بالخل حتى يكونَ الزَّيتُ والخل أكثر مما في الزيتون والسمسم والبسر) بالتمر إلا متساوياً ، والبُرِّ بالدقيق أو بالسويق، أو الدقيق بالسويق متفاضلاً أو متساوياً، والزيتون بالزيت، والسمسم بالخل حتى يكونَ الزَّيَتُ والخل أكثر مما في الزيتون والسمسم الأموال الربوية ؛ ولهذا قيد المصنف الله لقوله : من الربوي، وأما الثاني : فلإطلاق التمر على البسر فيكون من جنسه ، والتَّفاضلُ لا يجوز في المتجانسين ، فلا يجوزُ البيع في هاتين الصورتين إلا متساوياً. قال في منتهى الأرب: بسر : بالضم غوره خرما، ويضم السين، وانجداز شکوفه رخر ما اول طاهر شود انر اطلع خوانند وجون بسته گردد سیاب گویند و هرگاه سبنردگرد گردد جدال وجون اندکي کلان گردد آنر ابغو خوانند وجون ازان کلان شود بسرست و بعد ازان مخطم بعد ازان موکت بعد از ان بذنوب بعد از ان جمسه بعد ازان خالع وجون يحتكي آن بانتهار سدر طب نامند و بعد ازان تمر. انتهى. [١] قوله : والبر...الخ ؛ يعني لا يجوز بيع البر بالدقيق : أي بدقيق البر أو بالسويق ، أي بسويق البر متفاضلاً أو متساوياً. أما متفاضلاً ؛ فلان كل واحدٍ من الدقيق والسويق بر من وجه وإن اختص باسم ؛ لأن كل واحد منهما من أجزاء البُرِّ ، فإنَّ الطحن لا يوجد إلا تفريق الأجزاء، والمجتمع لا يصير بالتفريق جنساً آخر. وأما متساوية ؛ فلان المعيار فيه الكيل، وهو غير مستو بينهما وبين البر؛ لاكتنازهما فيه، وتخلخل البر. ويجوز عند مالك وأحمد الله في رواية بيع البر بالدقيق وبالسويق كيلاً ووزناً. ولا يجوز بيع الدقيق بالسويق متفاضلاً أو متساوياً، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة له ؛ لبقاء المجانسة من وجه، إذ السويقُ أجزاء حنطة مقلية، والدقيق أجزاء حنطة غير مقلية، وبيع الحنطة المقليَّةِ بغيرِ المقلية لا يصح بحال، فكذا بيع الدقيق بالسويق. (۱) البسر : التمر قبل أن إرطابه لخضاضته ، وذلك إذا لون ولم ينضج، وإذا نضج فقد أرطب. ينظر: (تاج العروس(١٠ : ١٧٤)، وغيره. ويستقرضُ الخبز وزناً لا عدداً عند أبي يوسف له وبه يُفْتَى (١ ليكون بعضُ الزَّيتِ بالزيت الذي في الزيتون ، والباقي بالنَّجير" [۲] ( ويستقرض الخبز وزناً لا عدداً عند أبي يوسف له، وبه يُفْتَى) ، أما عند أبي حنيفة له لا يجوز لا وزناً ولا عدداً وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد الله : يجوز بيع الدقيق بالسويق كيف ما كان؛ لأنهما جنسان مختلفان ؛ لاختلاف اسمهما والمقصود منهما ؛ لأنَّ أحدهما يصلح لما لا يصلح الآخر، فإنَّ الدقيق مثلاً يصلح للخبز، والسويق لا يصلح له، بل يلت بالسمن أو العسل ويؤكل، والدقيق ليس كذلك. وأجيب : بأن التغذي معظم المقصود، وهو يشملها، ولا يضـر فـواتُ بعض المقاصدِ كالحنطة المقلية مع غير المقلية، فإنَّ المقلية لا يصلح للزراعة مثلاً، وغير المقلية تصلح لها، وذا لا يوجب اختلاف الجنس، فكذا الدقيق مع السويق، كذا في «كمال الدراية وغيره من الكتب المبسوطة. (١) قوله : ليكون ...الخ ؛ واعلم أنَّ بيع الزيتون بالزيت على أربعة أوجه : . إن علم أن الزيت الذي في الزيتون أكثر من الزيت المنفصل ، لم يصح لتحققِ الفضل الخالي عن العوض من حيث زيادة الدهن والتفل. . وكذا إن علم أنه مثله ؛ لأنَّ التفل يكون فضلاً خالياً عن العوض. . وإن علم أن الزيت المنفصل أكثر جازَ والفضلُ بالتفل، وهذه الثلاثة بالإجماع. ٤. وإن لم يعلم أنه مثله ، أو أكثر منه، أو أقلّ منه صح عند زفره ؛ لأنَّ الجواز هو الأصل، والفساد لوجودِ الفضل الخالي عن العوض، فما لم يعلم لا يفسد البيع، وعندنا : لا يصح؛ لأنَّ الفضل الذي هو متوهّم الوجودِ كالمتحقق في بـاب الـربا احتياطاً ، كذا في «كمال الدراية»(۲) نقلا عن شرح «الوافي». [٢] قوله : بالثجير؛ تجير كامير کنجاره که تفل باشد كذا في منتهى الأرب»، وكنجاره بمعنی کنجارا ست که نخاله کنجد و هر تخم که روغن آنرا کشیده باشند ، كذا في «البرهان القاطع».
And unripe dates with ripe dates except equal, and wheat with flour or with sawiq, or flour with sawiq (whether) excess or equal, and olives with oil, and sesame with vinegar such that the oil and vinegar are more than what is in the olives and sesame. ... (Unripe dates with ripe dates except equal). The author conditioned by his saying 'from the ribawi'. And the second: because the term 'tamr' applies to busr, so it is of its genus, and excess is not allowed among homogeneous items. So sale in these two forms is not allowed except equally. He said in Muntaha al-Arab: Busr: with damma of ba, it is raw dates ... end. [1] His statement: 'And wheat...' meaning: It is not permissible to sell wheat for flour, i.e., wheat flour, or for sawiq, i.e., wheat sawiq, whether excess or equal. As for excess: because each of flour and sawiq is wheat in a sense, even if it has a specific name; because each of them is from parts of the wheat grain, for grinding only separates parts, and the aggregate does not become another genus by separation. And as for equal: because the standard is by measure, and it is not equal between them and wheat due to their density in it and the loosening of wheat. It is permissible according to Malik and Ahmad in one narration to sell wheat for flour and sawiq by measure and weight. And selling flour for sawiq is not permissible, whether excess or equal, according to Abu Hanifa; due to genus remaining similar in some respect, for sawiq is parts of parched wheat, and flour is parts of unparched wheat, and selling parched wheat for unparched is not valid at all, so too selling flour for sawiq. (1) Busr: dates before they become ripe due to freshness, when they color but do not ripen; when they ripen they become rutab. See Taj al-Arus (10:174) etc. And bread is borrowed by weight, not by count, according to Abu Yusuf, and with it is given fatwa. [1] So that some of the oil (is exchanged) for the oil in the olives, and the remainder for the marc. [2] (And bread is borrowed by weight, not by count, according to Abu Yusuf, and with it is given fatwa). As for Abu Hanifa, it is not permissible neither by weight nor by count. And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: Selling flour for sawiq is permissible in any way because they are different genera; due to difference in their names and the intended purpose; because one is suitable for what the other is not, for flour is suitable for bread, and sawiq is not suitable for it, rather it is mixed with ghee or honey and eaten, and flour is not like that. And it was answered: that nutrition is the main purpose, and it includes them, and the loss of some purposes does not matter, like parched wheat with unparchd, for parched is not suitable for cultivation, etc., and the other is suitable for it, and that does not necessitate difference in genus, so too flour with sawiq, as in Kamal al-Dirayah and other detailed books. (1) His statement: 'so that...' Know that selling olives for oil has four aspects: 1. If it is known that the oil in the olives is more than the separate oil, it is not valid due to the existence of excess without compensation in terms of increase of oil and pulp. 2. Similarly if it is known that it is equal, because the pulp is excess without compensation. 3. If it is known that the separate oil is more, it is valid, and the excess is for the pulp; these three are by consensus. 4. If it is not known whether it is equal, or more, or less, it is valid according to Zufar because permissibility is the original, and invalidity is due to existence of excess without compensation, and as long as it is not known, the sale is not invalidated. According to us: it is not valid because the excess which is possibly existing is like actual in the chapter of riba as a precaution, as in Kamal al-Dirayah quoting from Sharh al-Wafi. [2] His statement: 'with marc': Tajir like amir, is the marc, as in Muntaha al-Arab, and 'kunjara' means the husk of sesame and any seed whose oil has been extracted, as in al-Burhan al-Qati.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the rulings on ribawi transactions, specifically dealing with the exchange of similar food items. The author, commenting on the matn of Al-Wiqayah, begins with the case of unripe dates (busr) for ripe dates (tamr): they are of the same genus, so exchange must be equal in measure. He then defines 'busr' in detail from Muntaha al-Arab, showing the stages of date development. Next, he addresses wheat (burr) exchanged for flour (daqiq) or parched grain (sawiq). The Hanafi position holds that these are all from the same genus despite name changes, so excess is prohibited. He notes that Malik and Ahmad allow such exchange. For flour with sawiq, Abu Hanifa forbids it, analogizing to parched versus unparched wheat, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider them different genera and allow it. The author then discusses olives with oil: if the oil in the olives is equal or more, sale is invalid due to ambiguous excess; if the separate oil is known to be more, it is valid; if unknown, Zufar allows but the Hanafis do not, as precaution against riba. Finally, the ruling on borrowing bread: Abu Yusuf says it is permissible by weight (not count), and this is the fatwa; Abu Hanifa forbids it entirely. The commentary maintains the Hanafi framework, presenting the relied-upon positions.

Key Terms
حنطة مقلية (hanṭa maqliyya): Parched (roasted) wheat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider flour and sawiq different genera?
A) Because they have different names
B) Because they are used for different purposes
C) Because one is ground, the other is parched
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
The intra-Hanafi dispute on flour vs sawiq hinges on whether processing and purpose affect genus; the school sides with Abu Hanifa that they are the same genus.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Selling Olives for Oil: Four Scenarios and the Ruling When Unknown
Ruling on olives-oil exchange with four scenarios and dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four scenarios of exchanging olives for oil and state the Hanafi ruling for each, including the precautionary approach when the oil content is unknown.

Core Idea
Olives for oil: three scenarios by consensus, fourth disputed; Hanafis declare invalid if unknown due to precaution against riba.

This passage details the ruling on exchanging olives for their extracted oil. It outlines four possible cases based on whether the oil content of the olives is known to be more, equal, or less than the oil being exchanged, and when it is unknown. The author explains the consensus on the first three and the dispute with Zufar on the fourth.

Guided Path
1
List the four scenarios
Identify the four cases: 1) oil in olives more, 2) equal, 3) separate oil more, 4) unknown.
The text numbers them as 'على أربعة أوجه'.
2
Learn the ruling for each by consensus
Cases 1 and 2: invalid due to excess/pulp without compensation. Case 3: valid; excess oil compensated by pulp.
These are agreed upon.
3
Understand the dispute on unknown case
Zufar allows because permissibility is the default; Hanafis do not because the possible excess is treated as actual in riba matters (precaution).
The author cites Kamal al-Dirayah.
4
Note the definition of marc (thujīr)
The marc is the residue after oil extraction; it acts as countervalue for the excess oil in case 3.
Defined in Muntaha al-Arab.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبسر بالتمر إلا متساوياً ، والبُرِّ بالدقيق أو بالسويق، أو الدقيق بالسويق متفاضلاً أو متساوياً، والزّيتون بالزيت، والسمسم بالخل حتى يكونَ الزَّيتُ والخل أكثر مما في الزيتون والسمسم والبسر) بالتمر إلا متساوياً ، والبُرِّ بالدقيق أو بالسويق، أو الدقيق بالسويق متفاضلاً أو متساوياً، والزيتون بالزيت، والسمسم بالخل حتى يكونَ الزَّيَتُ والخل أكثر مما في الزيتون والسمسم الأموال الربوية ؛ ولهذا قيد المصنف الله لقوله : من الربوي، وأما الثاني : فلإطلاق التمر على البسر فيكون من جنسه ، والتَّفاضلُ لا يجوز في المتجانسين ، فلا يجوزُ البيع في هاتين الصورتين إلا متساوياً. قال في منتهى الأرب: بسر : بالضم غوره خرما، ويضم السين، وانجداز شکوفه رخر ما اول طاهر شود انر اطلع خوانند وجون بسته گردد سیاب گویند و هرگاه سبنردگرد گردد جدال وجون اندکي کلان گردد آنر ابغو خوانند وجون ازان کلان شود بسرست و بعد ازان مخطم بعد ازان موکت بعد از ان بذنوب بعد از ان جمسه بعد ازان خالع وجون يحتكي آن بانتهار سدر طب نامند و بعد ازان تمر. انتهى. [١] قوله : والبر...الخ ؛ يعني لا يجوز بيع البر بالدقيق : أي بدقيق البر أو بالسويق ، أي بسويق البر متفاضلاً أو متساوياً. أما متفاضلاً ؛ فلان كل واحدٍ من الدقيق والسويق بر من وجه وإن اختص باسم ؛ لأن كل واحد منهما من أجزاء البُرِّ ، فإنَّ الطحن لا يوجد إلا تفريق الأجزاء، والمجتمع لا يصير بالتفريق جنساً آخر. وأما متساوية ؛ فلان المعيار فيه الكيل، وهو غير مستو بينهما وبين البر؛ لاكتنازهما فيه، وتخلخل البر. ويجوز عند مالك وأحمد الله في رواية بيع البر بالدقيق وبالسويق كيلاً ووزناً. ولا يجوز بيع الدقيق بالسويق متفاضلاً أو متساوياً، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة له ؛ لبقاء المجانسة من وجه، إذ السويقُ أجزاء حنطة مقلية، والدقيق أجزاء حنطة غير مقلية، وبيع الحنطة المقليَّةِ بغيرِ المقلية لا يصح بحال، فكذا بيع الدقيق بالسويق. (۱) البسر : التمر قبل أن إرطابه لخضاضته ، وذلك إذا لون ولم ينضج، وإذا نضج فقد أرطب. ينظر: (تاج العروس(١٠ : ١٧٤)، وغيره. ويستقرضُ الخبز وزناً لا عدداً عند أبي يوسف له وبه يُفْتَى (١ ليكون بعضُ الزَّيتِ بالزيت الذي في الزيتون ، والباقي بالنَّجير" [۲] ( ويستقرض الخبز وزناً لا عدداً عند أبي يوسف له، وبه يُفْتَى) ، أما عند أبي حنيفة له لا يجوز لا وزناً ولا عدداً وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد الله : يجوز بيع الدقيق بالسويق كيف ما كان؛ لأنهما جنسان مختلفان ؛ لاختلاف اسمهما والمقصود منهما ؛ لأنَّ أحدهما يصلح لما لا يصلح الآخر، فإنَّ الدقيق مثلاً يصلح للخبز، والسويق لا يصلح له، بل يلت بالسمن أو العسل ويؤكل، والدقيق ليس كذلك. وأجيب : بأن التغذي معظم المقصود، وهو يشملها، ولا يضـر فـواتُ بعض المقاصدِ كالحنطة المقلية مع غير المقلية، فإنَّ المقلية لا يصلح للزراعة مثلاً، وغير المقلية تصلح لها، وذا لا يوجب اختلاف الجنس، فكذا الدقيق مع السويق، كذا في «كمال الدراية وغيره من الكتب المبسوطة. (١) قوله : ليكون ...الخ ؛ واعلم أنَّ بيع الزيتون بالزيت على أربعة أوجه : . إن علم أن الزيت الذي في الزيتون أكثر من الزيت المنفصل ، لم يصح لتحققِ الفضل الخالي عن العوض من حيث زيادة الدهن والتفل. . وكذا إن علم أنه مثله ؛ لأنَّ التفل يكون فضلاً خالياً عن العوض. . وإن علم أن الزيت المنفصل أكثر جازَ والفضلُ بالتفل، وهذه الثلاثة بالإجماع. ٤. وإن لم يعلم أنه مثله ، أو أكثر منه، أو أقلّ منه صح عند زفره ؛ لأنَّ الجواز هو الأصل، والفساد لوجودِ الفضل الخالي عن العوض، فما لم يعلم لا يفسد البيع، وعندنا : لا يصح؛ لأنَّ الفضل الذي هو متوهّم الوجودِ كالمتحقق في بـاب الـربا احتياطاً ، كذا في «كمال الدراية»(۲) نقلا عن شرح «الوافي». [٢] قوله : بالثجير؛ تجير كامير کنجاره که تفل باشد كذا في منتهى الأرب»، وكنجاره بمعنی کنجارا ست که نخاله کنجد و هر تخم که روغن آنرا کشیده باشند ، كذا في «البرهان القاطع».
And unripe dates with ripe dates except equal, and wheat with flour or with sawiq, or flour with sawiq (whether) excess or equal, and olives with oil, and sesame with vinegar such that the oil and vinegar are more than what is in the olives and sesame. ... (Unripe dates with ripe dates except equal). The author conditioned by his saying 'from the ribawi'. And the second: because the term 'tamr' applies to busr, so it is of its genus, and excess is not allowed among homogeneous items. So sale in these two forms is not allowed except equally. He said in Muntaha al-Arab: Busr: with damma of ba, it is raw dates ... end. [1] His statement: 'And wheat...' meaning: It is not permissible to sell wheat for flour, i.e., wheat flour, or for sawiq, i.e., wheat sawiq, whether excess or equal. As for excess: because each of flour and sawiq is wheat in a sense, even if it has a specific name; because each of them is from parts of the wheat grain, for grinding only separates parts, and the aggregate does not become another genus by separation. And as for equal: because the standard is by measure, and it is not equal between them and wheat due to their density in it and the loosening of wheat. It is permissible according to Malik and Ahmad in one narration to sell wheat for flour and sawiq by measure and weight. And selling flour for sawiq is not permissible, whether excess or equal, according to Abu Hanifa; due to genus remaining similar in some respect, for sawiq is parts of parched wheat, and flour is parts of unparched wheat, and selling parched wheat for unparched is not valid at all, so too selling flour for sawiq. (1) Busr: dates before they become ripe due to freshness, when they color but do not ripen; when they ripen they become rutab. See Taj al-Arus (10:174) etc. And bread is borrowed by weight, not by count, according to Abu Yusuf, and with it is given fatwa. [1] So that some of the oil (is exchanged) for the oil in the olives, and the remainder for the marc. [2] (And bread is borrowed by weight, not by count, according to Abu Yusuf, and with it is given fatwa). As for Abu Hanifa, it is not permissible neither by weight nor by count. And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: Selling flour for sawiq is permissible in any way because they are different genera; due to difference in their names and the intended purpose; because one is suitable for what the other is not, for flour is suitable for bread, and sawiq is not suitable for it, rather it is mixed with ghee or honey and eaten, and flour is not like that. And it was answered: that nutrition is the main purpose, and it includes them, and the loss of some purposes does not matter, like parched wheat with unparchd, for parched is not suitable for cultivation, etc., and the other is suitable for it, and that does not necessitate difference in genus, so too flour with sawiq, as in Kamal al-Dirayah and other detailed books. (1) His statement: 'so that...' Know that selling olives for oil has four aspects: 1. If it is known that the oil in the olives is more than the separate oil, it is not valid due to the existence of excess without compensation in terms of increase of oil and pulp. 2. Similarly if it is known that it is equal, because the pulp is excess without compensation. 3. If it is known that the separate oil is more, it is valid, and the excess is for the pulp; these three are by consensus. 4. If it is not known whether it is equal, or more, or less, it is valid according to Zufar because permissibility is the original, and invalidity is due to existence of excess without compensation, and as long as it is not known, the sale is not invalidated. According to us: it is not valid because the excess which is possibly existing is like actual in the chapter of riba as a precaution, as in Kamal al-Dirayah quoting from Sharh al-Wafi. [2] His statement: 'with marc': Tajir like amir, is the marc, as in Muntaha al-Arab, and 'kunjara' means the husk of sesame and any seed whose oil has been extracted, as in al-Burhan al-Qati.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the rulings on ribawi transactions, specifically dealing with the exchange of similar food items. The author, commenting on the matn of Al-Wiqayah, begins with the case of unripe dates (busr) for ripe dates (tamr): they are of the same genus, so exchange must be equal in measure. He then defines 'busr' in detail from Muntaha al-Arab, showing the stages of date development. Next, he addresses wheat (burr) exchanged for flour (daqiq) or parched grain (sawiq). The Hanafi position holds that these are all from the same genus despite name changes, so excess is prohibited. He notes that Malik and Ahmad allow such exchange. For flour with sawiq, Abu Hanifa forbids it, analogizing to parched versus unparched wheat, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider them different genera and allow it. The author then discusses olives with oil: if the oil in the olives is equal or more, sale is invalid due to ambiguous excess; if the separate oil is known to be more, it is valid; if unknown, Zufar allows but the Hanafis do not, as precaution against riba. Finally, the ruling on borrowing bread: Abu Yusuf says it is permissible by weight (not count), and this is the fatwa; Abu Hanifa forbids it entirely. The commentary maintains the Hanafi framework, presenting the relied-upon positions.

Key Terms
ثجير (thujīr): The marc or residue after oil extraction.
زيت (zayt): Olive oil.
زيتون (zaytūn): Olives, the fruit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Oil in olives is more than the oil given
B) Oil in olives equals the oil given
C) Oil given is more than the oil in olives
D) Unknown proportions

Key Takeaway
The validity of selling olives for oil hinges on knowledge of proportions; unknown case is invalid in Hanafi law due to precaution against riba.

Borrowing Bread: Weight vs Count, and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on borrowing bread with intra-Hanafi dispute and fatwa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the mufta bihi ruling on borrowing bread and explain why weight is preferred over count.

Core Idea
Borrow bread by weight, not by count, to avoid riba.

This passage addresses the permissibility of borrowing bread (qard). Abu Hanifa prohibits it entirely, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow it but only by weight, not by count. The fatwa is given with Abu Yusuf's view. The reasoning is that weight ensures precision, avoiding the possibility of riba due to size variations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the positions on borrowing bread
Abu Hanifa: not allowed at all. Abu Yusuf: allowed only by weight. Muhammad agrees with Abu Yusuf.
The text says 'أما عند أبي حنيفة لا يجوز لا وزناً ولا عدداً'.
2
Note the fatwa statement
The phrase 'وبه يفتى' indicates the relied-upon position is Abu Yusuf's.
This is explicit.
3
Understand the reasoning for weight
Weight ensures equality; count can lead to inequality because loaves may differ in size.
This prevents riba in repayment.

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the rulings on ribawi transactions, specifically dealing with the exchange of similar food items. The author, commenting on the matn of Al-Wiqayah, begins with the case of unripe dates (busr) for ripe dates (tamr): they are of the same genus, so exchange must be equal in measure. He then defines 'busr' in detail from Muntaha al-Arab, showing the stages of date development. Next, he addresses wheat (burr) exchanged for flour (daqiq) or parched grain (sawiq). The Hanafi position holds that these are all from the same genus despite name changes, so excess is prohibited. He notes that Malik and Ahmad allow such exchange. For flour with sawiq, Abu Hanifa forbids it, analogizing to parched versus unparched wheat, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider them different genera and allow it. The author then discusses olives with oil: if the oil in the olives is equal or more, sale is invalid due to ambiguous excess; if the separate oil is known to be more, it is valid; if unknown, Zufar allows but the Hanafis do not, as precaution against riba. Finally, the ruling on borrowing bread: Abu Yusuf says it is permissible by weight (not count), and this is the fatwa; Abu Hanifa forbids it entirely. The commentary maintains the Hanafi framework, presenting the relied-upon positions.

Selling Flour for Parched Flour: Genus Distinction in Riba
Intra-Hanafi dispute on genus in ribawi food items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi criterion for genus unity in food items and evaluate the intra-madhhab argument on flour and sawiq.

Core Idea
Flour and sawiq are the same genus because nutrition is the main purpose, even if names and other uses differ.

This passage presents a dispute within the Hanafi school about whether flour (دقيق) and sawiq (سويق, parched barley flour) belong to the same genus for the prohibition of riba. Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) argue they are different due to distinct names and culinary uses. The opposing view, likely that of Imam Abu Hanifa, responds that the core purpose (التغذي) unites them. Understanding this debate reveals how Hanafis define genus (جنس) in commodity exchanges — a key skill for mastering the laws of riba.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi positions
Identify the two positions: which imams say it is permissible, and which imam (or later authority) says it is not.
Sahibayn: permissible; the rebuttal (Abu Hanifa): impermissible due to same genus.
2
Analyze Sahibayn's proof
What is their evidence? They cite different names (qawl) and different primary uses (af'al). Why do they consider that sufficient?
Names differ: 'flour' vs 'sawiq'; uses: flour for bread, sawiq eaten mixed with fat/honey.
3
Understand the rebuttal's counter-argument
The respondent (presumably Abu Hanifa) uses an analogy (qiyas) to compare with fried vs. non-fried wheat. How does the criterion of nutrition override differences?
Nutrition (التغذي) is the main purpose; specific uses like planting or bread are secondary.
4
Derive the operative test for genus unity
From the rebuttal, extract the Hanafi ruling principle: two food items are the same genus if their ultimate purpose (nutrition) is identical, regardless of names or secondary uses.
This principle is applied to ribawi items: only same genus items may not be exchanged with excess.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference? If same genus, equal exchange is required; if different, any amount is allowed. Which view is mufta bihi?
The text cites Kamal al-Dirayah supporting the rebuttal, suggesting the mufta bihi is the unity view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد الله : يجوز بيع الدقيق بالسويق كيف ما كان؛ لأنهما جنسان مختلفان ؛ لاختلاف اسمهما والمقصود منهما؛ لأنَّ أحدهما يصلح لما لا يصلح الآخر، فإنَّ الدقيق مثلاً يصلح للخبز، والسويق لا يصلح له، بل يلت بالسمن أو العسل ويؤكل، والدقيق ليس كذلك. وأجيب : بأن التغذي معظم المقصود، وهو يشملها، ولا يضـر فـواتُ بعض المقاصدِ كالحنطة المقلية مع غير المقلية، فإنَّ المقلية لا يصلح للزراعة مثلاً، وغير المقلية تصلح لها، وذا لا يوجب اختلاف الجنس، فكذا الدقيق مع السويق، كذا في «كمال الدراية وغيره من الكتب المبسوطة.
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on them, said: The sale of flour for sawiq (parched barley flour) is permissible in any manner because they are two different genera due to the difference in their names and their purpose. Because one is suitable for what the other is not. For example, flour is suitable for bread, and sawiq is not suitable for that; rather it is mixed with clarified butter or honey and eaten, and flour is not like that. And it was answered: That nutrition is the main purpose, and it includes them both. And the loss of some purposes does not harm, like the case of fried wheat with non-fried wheat, for the fried wheat is not suitable for cultivation for example, and the non-fried is suitable for it, and that does not necessitate a difference in genus. Similarly flour with sawiq. Thus it is in Kamal al-Dirayah and other extensive books.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the permissibility of exchanging flour for sawiq. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad rule that it is permissible in any manner because they consider them different genera. They argue that the names differ and the primary use differs: flour is used for bread, while sawiq is eaten mixed with fat or honey. Therefore, it is not riba. The respondent (likely Abu Hanifa or the later authoritative view) replies that the main purpose of both is nutrition, which is common. The difference in specific uses does not change the genus, analogous to fried and non-fried wheat: one can be planted, the other cannot, yet they are the same genus. Thus, flour and sawiq are the same genus, so selling them with excess would be riba, rebutting the permissibility view.

Key Terms
التغذي (al-taghadhdhi): Nutrition; the primary purpose of food consumption.
جنس (jins): Genus; a category of objects sharing an essential attribute.
سويق (sawiq): Parched barley flour; a foodstuff made from roasted barley, often eaten mixed with fat or honey.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main criterion used in the rebuttal to argue that flour and sawiq are the same genus?
A) They are both made from wheat.
B) They share the same name in some dialects.
C) The main purpose of both is nutrition (التغذي).
D) Both are used for bread-making.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the genus of food items for riba is determined by the common purpose of nutrition; differences in names or secondary uses do not create different genera.

Selling Olives for Olive Oil: Four Scenarios and the Riba Ruling
Detailed ruling on four cases of olives-for-oil exchange

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for each of the four scenarios of exchanging olives for oil and explain why uncertainty triggers invalidity due to precaution in riba.

Core Idea
Any uncompensated excess of the same genus invalidates the sale, and speculative excess is treated as actual in riba.

This passage explores a nuanced transaction: bartering olives for olive oil. Since both items are of the same genus (both involve oil), riba rules apply. The Hanafi school analyzes four possible relationships between the oil inside the olives and the oil being exchanged for them. Understanding these cases sharpens your ability to detect riba in commodity exchanges.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four scenarios
The text lists four permutations based on known or unknown proportions of oil content: known excess of internal oil, known equality, known deficit, and unknown proportion.
2
Analyze the presence of excess (fadl)
In each case, determine if there is an uncompensated excess of the same genus. Cases 1 and 2 involve actual excess. Case 3 lacks excess. Case 4 involves potential excess.
The dregs (thujir) are considered a different commodity, so they can compensate excess in case 3.
3
Apply the riba al-fadl rule
For cases with a definite uncompensated excess, the sale is fasid. For case 3, the excess is compensated by the dregs, making it sahih.
Consensus on these three cases.
4
Evaluate the dispute on uncertainty
Zufar permits the sale when the proportion is unknown, citing the original permissibility of contracts. The Hanafi majority prohibits due to precaution (ihtiyat): the potential excess is treated as actual in riba matters.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
5
Identify the mufta bihi position
The text states 'indana: la yasihhu' (according to us, it is not valid). The fatwa is on invalidity, supported by Kamal al-Dirayah.
Zufar's view is a minority within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ليكون بعض الزيت بالزيت الذي في الزيتون ، والباقي بالثجير؛ واعلم أن بيع الزيتون بالزيت على أربعة أوجه: إن علم أن الزيت الذي في الزيتون أكثر من الزيت المنفصل، لم يصح لتحقيق الفضل الخالي عن العوض من حيث زيادة الدهن والتفل. وكذا إن علم أنه مثله؛ لأن التفل يكون فضلاً خالياً عن العوض. وإن علم أن الزيت المنفصل أكثر جاز والفضل بالتفل، وهذه الثلاثة بالإجماع. وإن لم يعلم أنه مثله أو أكثر منه أو أقل منه صح عند زفر؛ لأن الجواز هو الأصل، والفساد لوجود الفضل الخالي عن العوض، فما لم يعلم لا يفسد البيع، وعندنا: لا يصح؛ لأن الفضل الذي هو متوهم الوجود كالمتحقق في باب الربا احتياطاً، كذا في كمال الدراية نقلا عن شرح الوافي. قوله: بالثجير؛ تجير كامير كنحاره که تفل باشد كذا في منتهى الأرب، وكنحاره بمعنی كنحارا ست که نخاله کنجد و هر تخم که روغن آنرا کشیده باشند، كذا في البرهان القاطع.
He said: 'So that some of the oil (is exchanged) for the oil that is in the olives, and the remainder for the dregs (al-thujir).' Know that the sale of olives for oil is of four types: (1) If it is known that the oil contained in the olives is greater than the separate oil, the sale is invalid due to the existence of an excess without compensation in terms of increase in fat and dregs. (2) Likewise if it is known that they are equal, because the dregs would be an excess without compensation. (3) If it is known that the separate oil is greater, it is permissible and the excess is for the dregs. These three are by consensus. (4) If it is not known whether it is equal, greater, or less, Zufar says it is valid because permissibility is the original basis, and invalidity is due to the existence of excess without compensation, so what is not known does not invalidate the sale. According to us (Hanafis), it is not valid because an excess that is possibly present is treated as actually present in the chapter of riba as a precaution. Thus in Kamal al-Dirayah, quoting from Sharh al-Wafi. He said: 'al-thujir' means the dregs, like the dregs of sesame oil, as in Muntaha al-Arab, and 'kanhareh' is the husk of sesame and any seed from which oil has been extracted, as in al-Burhan al-Qati'.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage details the ruling on exchanging olives for olive oil. The base principle: when exchanging olives for oil, the oil in the olives is considered part of the olives. If the oil in the olives exceeds the separate oil, there is riba al-fadl. Case 1: known excess – invalid. Case 2: known equality – invalid because the dregs are an uncompensated excess. Case 3: known deficit – valid, excess is for dregs. These three are unanimous. Case 4: uncertainty – Zufar says valid because presumption is validity. But the Hanafi school says invalid due to precaution: potential excess is treated as actual. The lexical note defines 'thujir' as the dregs (pomace) remaining after oil extraction.

Key Terms
تفل (tuful): Dregs, pomace, the solid residue left after oil extraction from olives.
ثجير (thujir): Dregs of oil, especially from sesame or olives.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school (indana), what is the ruling when it is unknown whether the oil content in the olives is equal to, more than, or less than the separate oil?
A) The sale is valid because permissibility is the original basis
B) The sale is invalid because potential excess is treated as actual
C) The sale is valid only if the seller swears
D) The sale is makruh but not invalid

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, uncertainty about excess in ribawi exchanges invalidates the contract as a precaution against riba.

Borrowing Bread: Weight vs. Count in the Hanafi School
Intra-Hanafi dispute on borrowing bread

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on borrowing bread and explain why the mufta bihi (Abu Yusuf) permits only by weight.

Core Idea
Bread cannot be returned like-for-like due to inherent variation — only weight-based borrowing overcomes this.

This passage examines a common transaction: borrowing bread from a neighbor. At first glance, it seems simple — you borrow a loaf and return a loaf. But the Hanafi imams disagreed on whether bread can be considered a mithli (homogenous) good. The disagreement hinges on the degree of variation in individual loaves. The fatwa follows Imam Abu Yusuf's middle position: borrowing by weight is valid because it provides a standard measure, but borrowing by count is invalid due to gross disparity in size, baking, and quality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal category
The act is istiqrad (borrowing a fungible commodity). Determine if it is permissible for bread. The passage presents three positions.
Borrowing is a contract of trust, not sale; but it requires the possibility of returning the exact like (mithl).
2
Locate the three positions within the Hanafi school
Abu Hanifa: not permissible at all. Abu Yusuf: permissible by weight only. Muhammad: permissible by both weight and count. Identify who holds each view.
These positions are recorded in the Zahir al-Riwayah.
3
Understand the illa (cause) for the ruling
The passage lists four sources of variation in loaves: bread itself (size, thickness), baker (skill), oven (new vs. old), and timing (beginning vs. end of baking). These cause gross disparity (تفاوت فاحش).
Bread by count is not mithli because the individual units are not identical.
4
Apply the condition for valid borrowing
Borrowing is only valid for mithli goods, defined as those that can be returned in like form. For bread, weight provides a reliable measure; count does not.
Ability to return the like (مثل) is a condition for istiqrad.
5
Determine the tarjih (weighting) and mufta bihi
The text says 'wa bihi yufta' (and by it fatwa is given), indicating Abu Yusuf's view is the operative ruling. Custom (تعامل) and need (حاجة) support weight-based borrowing.
The later Hanafi masters relied on Abu Yusuf's position as it balances practical need with legal precision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويستقرضُ الخبز وزناً لا عدداً عند أبي يوسف له وبه يُفْتَى، أما عند أبي حنيفة لا يجوز لا وزناً ولا عدداً. وعند محمد يجوز بهما للتعامل، وعند أبي يوسف يجوز وزناً للتعامل والحاجة، لا عدداً للتفاوت في آحاده. قوله: للتفاوت الفاحش؛ حاصله: إن الخبز وإن كان موزوناً لكنه متفاوت بتفاوت الخبز والخباز والتنور والتقدم والتأخر. وأما التفاوت بالخبز فمن حيث الطول والعرض والرقة والغلظ. وأما التفاوت بالخباز فلأن الخباز إذا كان حاذقاً في الخبز فخبزه يكون أحسن من خبز غيره لا محالة. وأما التفاوت بالتنور فإن التنور إذا كان جديداً يجيء خبزه جيداً، وإذا كان عتيقاً لا يجيء كذلك. وأما التفاوت بالتقدم والتأخر فإن في آخر التنور تكون النار قوية فيحترق الخبز بذلك وفي أوله لا ينضج كما ينبغي. والاستقراض إنما يصح في المثلي؛ لأن من شرطه القدرة على أداء مثل المستقرض، ولا قدرة على مثل الخبز فلا يصح استقراضه.
Bread is borrowed by weight, not by count, according to Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, and by it fatwa is given. As for Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, it is not permissible neither by weight nor by count. According to Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, it is permissible by both due to customary practice. According to Abu Yusuf, it is permissible by weight due to practice and need, not by count due to gross disparity in its individual pieces. He said: The gross disparity: bread, even though it is weighable, varies due to variation in the bread itself, the baker, the oven, and the timing. As for variation in the bread itself, it is in length, width, thinness, and thickness. As for variation in the baker, because if the baker is skilled, his bread is undoubtedly better than that of another. As for variation in the oven, because if the oven is new, its bread turns out good, and if it is old, it does not. As for variation in timing, because at the end of the oven, the fire is strong so the bread burns, and at its beginning, it does not bake as it should. Borrowing is only valid in mithli goods because one of its conditions is the ability to return the like of what was borrowed, and there is no ability to return the like of bread, so its borrowing is not valid.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on the ruling of borrowing bread. The matn states that bread is borrowed by weight, not count, according to Abu Yusuf, and this is the position given for fatwa. The commentator then expands: Abu Hanifa forbids it entirely because bread is not a mithli (homogenous) commodity due to significant variation. Muhammad al-Shaybani allows it both by weight and count based on customary practice. Abu Yusuf takes a middle position: weight only, because count involves gross disparity. The commentator explains the reasons for disparity: differences in the bread itself (size, thickness), the skill of the baker, the condition of the oven, and the timing of baking. Because of these variations, it is impossible to return exactly the same type, and the condition for valid borrowing is the ability to return the like; lacking that, borrowing is invalid. The fatwa is according to Abu Yusuf's view.

Key Terms
استقراض (istiqrad): Borrowing a fungible commodity with the obligation to return its like.
مثلي (mithli): A commodity that has standard units of measure and can be returned in like form.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the ruling of borrowing bread according to Imam Abu Hanifa?
A) Permissible by weight and count
B) Permissible only by weight
C) Not permissible at all
D) Permissible only by count

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, borrowing bread is valid only by weight (the mufta bihi of Abu Yusuf) because bread by count is not mithli due to gross disparity in individual loaves.

No Riba Between Master and Slave
Ruling that riba does not occur between a master and his unauthorized slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that riba does not occur between a master and his unauthorized slave and explain the legal reasoning based on ownership.

Core Idea
No riba between master and slave because both are the master's property — one cannot trade with oneself.

This passage from the Hidayah commentary addresses a specific exception in the law of riba: transactions between a master and his slave. The Hanafi school rules that riba is not a concern when the slave is not authorized for trade (ghayr ma'dhun). The reasoning is that the slave and everything he possesses legally belong to the master, so any exchange is effectively with one's own property — a transaction with oneself cannot involve riba. This ruling highlights the importance of legal capacity and ownership in the Hanafi framework.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The passage states clearly that there is no riba between a master and his slave. Note the absolute nature of the statement.
Riba includes both riba al-fadl (excess in spot exchanges) and riba al-nasi'ah (deferment).
2
Recognize the condition
The commentary specifies that this ruling applies only when the slave is not authorized for trade (ghayr ma'dhun). If the slave is ma'dhun, the ruling differs.
An unauthorized slave cannot own property independently; everything belongs to the master.
3
Understand the legal cause (illah)
The reasoning given is 'because the slave and what he possesses belong to the master.' The underlying cause is the unity of ownership: the property is not legally distinct.
This is a talil (reasoning) based on ownership.
4
Apply the principle
Since the master owns both sides of the transaction, the exchange is akin to trading with oneself, which cannot involve riba.
Riba requires two distinct parties exchanging property.
5
Contrast with the authorized slave
An authorized slave (ma'dhun) has separate legal capacity; his property is his own for trade purposes. Hence, riba can occur between him and his master.
This distinction is unique to Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا ربا بين سيد وعبده؛ لأن العبد وما معه لمولاه. قوله: لأن العبد وما معه لمولاه؛ فلا يتحقق الربا، وهذا إذا كان غير مأذون.
There is no riba between a master and his slave because the slave and what he possesses belong to the master. He said: Because the slave and what he possesses belong to the master, so riba is not realized, and this is when the slave is not authorized.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage states a fundamental Hanafi principle: riba cannot occur between a master and his slave because the slave and his property are both the master's. A transaction with oneself is invalid for riba. The commentary clarifies that this applies when the slave is not authorized for trade (ghayr mādhūn). If the slave is authorized, he has independent legal capacity, and transactions between him and his master could potentially involve riba.

Key Terms
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave authorized by his master to engage in trade, thus having limited legal capacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When the slave is authorized for trade
B) When the slave is not authorized for trade
C) In all cases without exception
D) Only if the slave is a minor

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school exempts master-slave transactions from riba when the slave is unauthorized, based on the principle that one cannot trade with oneself through property one already owns.

Riba Between Master and Slave: Ownership and Indebtedness
Ruling on riba between master and slave with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on riba between a master and his slave, identify the condition of the slave's indebtedness that triggers riba, and explain the intra-madhhab difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Riba is impossible when property is unified, but a slave's debt severs that unity.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the foundational Hanafi ruling that there is no riba between a master and his slave. The key legal principle is that the slave and his property belong to the master, so an exchange is effectively with oneself. However, the passage introduces a critical exception: if the slave is indebted, riba occurs. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this exception and the differing analyses of Abu Hanifa and his two companions.

Guided Path
1
State the Base Ruling
Identify the core claim: 'There is no riba between a master and his slave.' Understand that this applies when the slave's property is fully owned by the master.
The ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Identify the Exception
Recognize the condition that triggers riba: if the slave is indebted. The passage explicitly says riba occurs between the master and an indebted slave.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf – both sides agree on the outcome.
3
Understand the Two Reasons (Intra-Madhhab Ikhtilaf)
Abu Hanifa holds that debt prevents the master's ownership altogether. The Sahibayn hold that debt does not prevent ownership but attaches creditors' rights, making ownership non-absolute. Both lead to riba.
The difference is in the rationale, not the ruling.
4
Examine the Analogy to Other Slave Categories
The text compares the indebted slave to the mukatab (slave under contract of manumission), where riba does occur, and distinguishes from the umm walad and mudabbar, where riba does not occur because earnings belong entirely to the master.
This shows the pattern: riba occurs when the slave has a separate financial interest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا ريا بين سيد وعبده للتفاوت الفاحش، وعند محمد الله يجوز بهما  للتعامل، وعند أبي يوسف يجوز وزناً للتعامل والحاجة، لا عدداً للتفاوتِ في آحاده. (ولا ربا بين سيد وعبدِهِ) : لأنَّ العبد وما معه لمولاه له [١] قوله : للتفاوتِ الفاحش ؛ حاصلُهُ : إِنَّ الخبز وإن كان موزوناً لكنَّه متفاوت بتفاوتِ الخبز والخباز والتنور والتقدم والتأخر. . أما التفاوتُ بالخبز فمن حيث الطول والعرض والرقةُ والغلظ. . وأما التفاوتُ بالخَبَازِ فلانَّ الخبار إذا كان حاذقاً في الخبز فخبزه يكون أحسن من خبز غيره لا محالة. . وأما التفاوت بالتنور فإنَّ التنور إذا كان جديداً يجيء خبره جيداً، وإذا كان عتيقاً لا يجيء كذلك. ٤. وأما التفاوت بالتقدم والتأخر فإنّ في آخرِ التنور تكونُ النَّارُ قويَّةً فيحترق الخبز بذلكِ وفي أوله لا ينضج كما ينبغي. والاستقراض إنما يصح في المثلي ؛ لأنَّ من شرطه القدرة على أداء مثل المستقرض، ولا قدرة على مثل الخبز فلا يصح استقراضه. [٢] قوله : يجوز بهما ؛ أي يجوز استقراض الخبز بالوزن والعددِ جميعاً ؛ للتعامل : أي لتعامل الناس بذلك ، فسقط التفاوت شرعاً ؛ للتعامل وحاجة الناس، وبه قال أحمد والشافعي له في قول، وعند مالك له : يعتبر الـعـرف كما صرح به العلامة العيني [۳] قوله : لأن العبد وما معه لمولاه ؛ فلا يتحقق الربا، وهذا إذا كان غير مأذون أو كان مأذوناً غير مديون، أما إن كان مديوناً فيتحقَّقُ الرّبا بينه وبين سيده ، أما عند أبي حنيفة له ؛ فلان عنده الدين على العبد يمنع ملك مولاه لماله. وأما عندهما وإن كان لا يمنع ؛ فلأنه لَمّا تعلّق حق الغرماء بما في يده لم يكن ملكاً لسيده مطلقاً، فيتحقق الربا بينهما كما يتحقق بين السيد ومكاتبه، وكذا لا ربا بين أم الولد وسيدها، ولا بين المدبّر وسيده ؛ لأن كسب كلّ واحدٍ منهما لسيده ، بخلاف المكاتب ؛ لأنه صار كالحريداً وتصرفاً، كذا في «كمال الدراية»).
And there is no usury between a master and his slave due to excessive disparity. And according to Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him), it is permissible by both (weight and count) due to common practice. And according to Abu Yusuf, it is permissible by weight due to common practice and need, not by count due to disparity in individuals. (And there is no usury between a master and his slave) because the slave and what is with him belong to his master. [1] His saying: due to excessive disparity; its summary is: bread, although it is weighed, is disparate due to disparity of the bread, baker, oven, and earliness and lateness. As for disparity due to bread, it is in terms of length, width, thinness and thickness. As for disparity due to the baker, since the baker if he is skilled in baking, his bread is inevitably better than another's bread. As for disparity due to the oven, if the oven is new, its bread comes out good; if old, it does not come out likewise. 4. As for disparity due to earliness and lateness, at the end of the oven the fire is strong, so the bread burns thereby; at its beginning it does not bake as it should. And borrowing is only valid in fungible goods (mithli), because one of its conditions is the ability to repay the like of what is borrowed, and one does not have the ability to pay the like of bread, so borrowing it is not valid. [2] His saying: permissible by both; i.e., borrowing bread is permissible by both weight and count together; due to common practice: i.e., because people practice that, so the disparity falls away legally due to common practice and people's need. And Ahmad and al-Shafi'i said similarly in one opinion, and according to Malik, custom is considered as the 'Allama al-'Ayni stated. [3] His saying: because the slave and what is with him belong to his master; so usury is not realized. This is when he is unpermitted or he is permitted but not indebted. However, if he is indebted, then usury occurs between him and his master. According to Abu Hanifa, because in his view debt on the slave prevents his master's ownership of his property. And according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), although it does not prevent, because when the creditors' right attaches to what is in his hand, it is not absolute ownership of his master, so usury occurs between them just as it occurs between the master and his mukatab. Similarly, there is no usury between an umm walad and her master, nor between a mudabbar and his master, because the earnings of each of them belong to his master, contrary to the mukatab because he has become like a free person in disposal. So in 'Kamil al-Diraya'.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the topic of riba (usury) between a master and his slave. The matn states that there is no riba between them, and the commentary explains the reasoning. The passage opens by stating the ruling: 'There is no riba between a master and his slave.' The commentator then clarifies that this is because the slave and his property belong to the master, so a transaction with one's own property cannot involve riba. The text then enters a detailed discussion about the permissibility of borrowing bread, which is a side issue. The commentary explains that borrowing bread is not valid because bread is not a fungible good (mithli) due to excessive disparity in individual loaves; however, due to common practice and need, some scholars allow it by weight or count. The passage then returns to the main topic of the slave. It explains that the ruling that there is no riba applies when the slave is not permitted to trade (ghayr ma'dhun) or if he is permitted but not indebted. If the slave is indebted, then riba does occur between master and slave. This is because according to Abu Hanifa, debt prevents the master's full ownership of the slave's property, so the master and slave become like separate parties. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), although debt does not completely prevent ownership, the rights of creditors make the property not absolutely owned by the master, so riba occurs. The passage then extends this ruling: there is also no riba between a master and his umm walad (slave mother of his child) or mudabbar (slave promised freedom upon the master's death), because all their earnings belong to the master. However, riba can occur between a master and his mukatab (slave under a contract of manumission) because the mukatab is like a free person in terms of disposal of property. This is a nuanced ruling that distinguishes between different categories of slaves based on ownership rights.

Key Terms
الربا (al-riba): Usury or interest prohibited in Islamic law; an unequal exchange in a ribawi transaction.
العبد (al-'abd): A male slave whose property is owned by his master unless he is permitted to trade or indebted.
التفاوت الفاحش (al-tafawut al-fahish): Excessive disparity in value, such as in loaves of bread due to various factors, which prevents them from being considered fungible (mithli).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does riba occur between a master and his slave?
A) When the slave is permitted to trade.
B) When the slave is indebted.
C) When the slave is a mukatab.
D) Never, because all property belongs to the master.

Key Takeaway
Riba is not possible between a master and his non-indebted slave due to unified ownership, but indebtedness severs that unity and triggers riba.

Riba with a Harbi in Dar al-Harb: The Exception Based on Property Protection
Ruling with proof on riba between Muslim and harbi in dar al-harb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on riba between a Muslim and a harbi in dar al-harb, explain the reasoning based on the concept of isma (protection), and distinguish the opposing views of Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
In dar al-harb, the harbi's wealth is mubah – thus riba is permitted.

This lesson explores a specific exception to the general prohibition of riba in Islamic law. The Hanafi school permits riba transactions between a Muslim and a harbi (enemy non-Muslim) within dar al-harb. The key legal concept is the absence of protection (isma) over the harbi's property in that territory. We will examine the textual proof, the reasoning, and the intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Riba between a Muslim and a harbi in dar al-harb is ruled as mubah (permissible). This is the core position.
2
Locate the dalil
The reasoning is based on the lack of isma (protection) for the harbi's wealth in dar al-harb. The harbi's property is considered mubah (permissible to take).
The text from al-Mabsut states: 'because his heart is satisfied and his wealth is taken by way of permissibility.'
3
Understand the restriction
The ruling is conditioned on the harbi being in dar al-harb. If the harbi enters dar al-Islam with aman, his property becomes protected and riba is prohibited.
The text explicitly says: 'if the harbi enters dar al-Islam and contracts with a Muslim and another harbi, then usury is not permissible.'
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf and Imam al-Shafi'i disagree. They analogize the harbi to a musta'min (harbi with aman) and consider his property protected regardless of location.
The Hanafi response is that the harbi in dar al-harb does not have protection (isma), unlike the musta'min in dar al-Islam.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi position (majority of the school) is the mufta bihi. It is supported by al-Mabsut and al-Nihaya.
The text cites al-Mabsut and al-Nihaya, indicating reliance on Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومسلم وحربي في داره [1]قوله : في داره ؛ وإنما قيد بقوله : في داره ؛ لأنه إذا دخل الحربي دار الإسلام فعقد مع مسلم وحربي آخر فلا يجوز الربا ؛ لأنَّ مال كل منهما معصوم، وفي «المبسوط» : ولا فرق بين أن يأخذ المسلم الدرهمين بالدرهم، أو الدرهم بالدرهمين في دار الحرب ؛ لأنه طيب نفس الكافر بما أعطاه ، وأخذ ماله بطريق الإباحة، ذكره البرْجُنْدِي. [۳] قوله : اعتباراً...الخ ; فكما يتحقق الربا بين المسلم والحربي الذي دخل دارنا بأمان في دارنا ، فكذلك في دراهم، والجامع تحقق الفضلُ الخالي عن العوض المستحق بالعقد، كذا في «النهاية».
And a Muslim and a harbi in his abode. [1] His saying: in his abode; and he only restricted it by his saying: in his abode, because if the harbi enters the abode of Islam and contracts with a Muslim and another harbi, then usury is not permissible because each of their wealth is protected. And in al-Mabsut: There is no difference between the Muslim taking two dirhams for one dirham, or one dirham for two dirhams in the abode of war, because the heart of the disbeliever is satisfied with what he gave, and taking his wealth is by way of permissibility (ibaha). Mentioned by al-Birjandi. [3] His saying: considering... etc.; just as usury occurs between a Muslim and a harbi who entered our abode with aman in our abode, so too in their dirhams, and the common element is the realization of excess devoid of compensation rightful by contract, as in al-Nihaya.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with proof on the topic of riba between a Muslim and a harbi (non-Muslim from enemy territory) in dar al-harb (enemy territory). The matn states: 'And a Muslim and a harbi in his abode.' The commentary explains that this ruling is specific to dar al-harb (his abode). If the harbi enters dar al-Islam and contracts with a Muslim or another harbi, then riba is not allowed because the wealth of each is protected (ma'sum). However, in dar al-harb, the harbi's wealth is considered mubah (permissible to take) because there is no protection of life or property. Therefore, any transaction involving riba between a Muslim and a harbi in dar al-harb is permissible. The commentary from al-Mabsut further states that it makes no difference whether the Muslim takes two dirhams for one or one for two; all is permissible because the harbi is content and his wealth is taken by way of ibaha. The text also mentions a contrasting view from Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i, who analogize to the musta'min (a harbi who enters dar al-Islam with aman) in our dar. The commentary refutes this by stating that in dar al-harb, the harbi's wealth is not protected. Thus, the ruling is specific to the location. This shows the Hanafi principle that the sanctity of property is based on protection (isma) and in dar al-harb, there is no isma for the harbi's wealth.

Key Terms
حربي (harbi): A non-Muslim who is in a state of war with Muslims and resides in dar al-harb.
دار الحرب (dar al-harb): Territory under the control of non-Muslims with whom Muslims are in a state of war, where Islamic law does not govern.
الإباحة (al-ibaha): Permissibility; the legal status that an action or transaction is allowed without restriction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is riba permissible between a Muslim and a harbi in dar al-harb according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because the harbi is a non-Muslim.
B) Because the harbi's wealth is not protected (mubah) in dar al-harb.
C) Because the Muslim is in need.
D) Because the transaction is not a real sale.

Key Takeaway
In dar al-harb, the harbi's wealth is mubah, so riba is permitted; in dar al-Islam, property is protected and riba is prohibited.

Riba with a Harbi in Dar al-Harb: The Hanafi Position
Inter-madhhab dispute on riba permissibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school permits riba with a Harbi in Dar al-Harb and evaluate the opposing argument of Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
A Harbi's property in Dar al-Harb is mubah, so riba is permissible — protection changes the ruling.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah addresses a critical question: Can a Muslim engage in riba with a non-Muslim harbi when both are in Dar al-Harb? The Hanafi school, led by Abu Hanifa, permits it because the harbi's property is not protected (mubah). However, Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i disagree, analogizing to a musta'min in Dar al-Islam. We will unpack the reasoning and practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and context
Recognize that the ruling concerns riba (excess in exchange) between a Muslim and a harbi (non-Muslim from enemy territory) while both are physically present in Dar al-Harb.
2
State the Hanafi position
Abu Hanifa rules that such riba is permissible because the harbi's property is mubah (free for taking) in Dar al-Harb. The Muslim may take it by any means, including riba.
3
Present the opposing view
Abu Yusuf (within the Hanafi school) and al-Shafi'i prohibit it by qiyas (analogy) to a musta'min (protected non-Muslim) in Dar al-Islam, whose property is inviolable.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The analogy fails because the harbi in Dar al-Harb has no protection; his property is mubah. The common factor of 'surplus without consideration' exists, but the protective status differs, making the analogy invalid (istihsan).
The thamarah al-khilaf: in Dar al-Harb, the transaction is valid per Abu Hanifa; void per others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومسلم وحربي في داره ) : أي في دار الحرب؛ لأنَّ مالَهُ مباح فيجوزُ أخذُهُ بأَيِّ طريق كان خلافاً لأبي يوسف الله والشافعي له اعتباراً" بالمستأمن في دارنا أو كان مأذوناً غير مديون، أما إن كان مديوناً فيتحقَّقُ الرّبا بينه وبين سيده ، أما عند أبي حنيفة له ؛ فلان عنده الدين على العبد يمنع ملك مولاه لماله. وأما عندهما وإن كان لا يمنع ؛ فلأنه لَمّا تعلّق حق الغرماء بما في يده لم يكن ملكاً لسيده مطلقاً، فيتحقق الربا بينهما كما يتحقق بين السيد ومكاتبه، وكذا لا ربا بين أم الولد وسيدها، ولا بين المدبّر وسيده ؛ لأن كسب كلّ واحدٍ منهما لسيده ، بخلاف المكاتب ؛ لأنه صار كالحريداً وتصرفاً، كذا في «كمال الدراية»). [1]قوله : في داره ; وإنما قيد بقوله : في داره ; لأنه إذا دخل الحربي دار الإسلام فعقد مع مسلم وحربي آخر فلا يجوز الربا ; لأنَّ مال كل منهما معصوم، وفي «المبسوط» (۳) : ولا فرق بين أن يأخذ المسلم الدرهمين بالدرهم، أو الدرهم بالدرهمين في دار الحرب ; لأنه طيب نفس الكافر بما أعطاه , وأخذ ماله بطريق الإباحة، ذكره البرْجُنْدِي. [۳] قوله : اعتباراً...الخ ; فكما يتحقق الربا بين المسلم والحربي الذي دخل دارنا بأمان في دارنا , فكذلك في دراهم، والجامع تحقق الفضلُ الخالي عن العوض المستحق بالعقد، كذا في «النهاية».
(And a Muslim and a Harbi in his house): i.e. in Dar al-Harb; because his property is mubah (free for taking), so it is permissible to take it by any means, contrary to Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) and al-Shafi'i, who consider it by analogy with a musta'min in our land, or if he was a permitted (slave) not indebted. But if he was indebted, then riba occurs between him and his master. According to Abu Hanifa, because with him the slave's debt prevents the master's ownership of his property. According to the two (Sahibayn), although it does not prevent, because when the creditors' right attaches to what is in his hand, it is not absolute ownership of the master, so riba occurs between them just as it occurs between a master and his mukatib. Similarly, there is no riba between umm walad and her master, nor between a mudabbar and his master, because the earnings of each of them belong to the master, unlike the mukatib, because he has become like a free person and exercises disposal. Thus in Kmal al-Dirayah. [1] His saying: 'in his house'; he only restricted it by 'in his house' because if the Harbi enters Dar al-Islam and contracts with a Muslim and another Harbi, riba is not permissible, because the property of each is protected. In al-Mabsut: There is no difference between the Muslim taking two dirhams for one dirham, or one dirham for two dirhams in Dar al-Harb, because the infidel is content with what he gave and taking his property by way of ibaha (permissibility). Al-Burjandi mentioned it. [3] His saying: 'by analogy' etc. For just as riba occurs between a Muslim and a Harbi who entered our land under aman in our land, so also in their dirhams, and the common factor is the existence of surplus free from consideration due by contract. Thus in al-Nihayah.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning riba between a Muslim and a Harbi (a non-Muslim from Dar al-Harb) when both are in Dar al-Harb. The ruling: it is permissible for the Muslim to take the Harbi's property by any means, including riba (e.g., exchanging two dirhams for one), because the Harbi's property is mubah—not protected by Islamic law. Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i disagree, analogizing to a musta'min (a non-Muslim given safe passage) in Dar al-Islam, whose property is protected. The Hanafi response: the analogy fails because the Harbi's property is not protected in Dar al-Harb, unlike a musta'min in Dar al-Islam. The passage then addresses an exceptional case: if the Harbi is a slave who is indebted, riba may occur between him and his master, with a nuance between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). It also clarifies that the restriction 'in his house' means the Harbi must be in Dar al-Harb; if he enters Dar al-Islam, his property becomes protected, and riba is prohibited. The text further states there is no difference between the Muslim giving the excess or receiving it, as both are forms of taking mubah property. Finally, it reinforces the common element in riba: surplus without consideration due by contract.

Key Terms
حربي (harbi): A non-Muslim who is in a state of war with Muslims and resides in Dar al-Harb; his property is not protected in Islamic law.
دار الحرب (dar al-harb): Territory under non-Muslim control that is not under Islamic rule; its inhabitants are harbis.
مباح (mubah): Legally permissible; in this context, the property of a harbi is mubah meaning it can be taken without liability.
مستأمن (musta'min): A non-Muslim who enters Dar al-Islam with a temporary safe conduct (aman); his property is protected during his stay.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, is riba permissible between a Muslim and a Harbi when both are in Dar al-Islam?
A) Yes, always permissible.
B) No, because the Harbi's property becomes protected when he enters Dar al-Islam.
C) Yes, but only if the Harbi is a slave.
D) No, because all riba is haram unconditionally.

Key Takeaway
A Muslim may engage in riba with a Harbi in Dar al-Harb because the Harbi's property is mubah, but this permissibility is immediately lost if the Harbi enters Dar al-Islam or receives aman.

Included Items in a House Sale: Building, Key, Upper Storey, Latrine
States the ruling and explains the legal basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which items enter the sale of a house by attachment (taba'iyyah) and which by custom (urf), and explain the Hanafi principle of taba'iyyah.

Core Idea
In a house sale, attached structures enter by default; the key enters by custom, not by legal analogy.

When you buy a house, does the sale automatically include the front door key? The upper floor? Hanafi law uses the principle of taba'iyyah (accessoriness) and custom to determine which items transfer without explicit mention. This lesson unpacks the ruling that the building, key, upper storey, and latrine are included, and why.

Guided Path
1
Identify the items listed
The ruling states that four items enter the sale of a house: the building (al-bina'), the key (al-miftah), the upper storey (al-'ulu), and the latrine (al-kanif). Note that 'building' includes the structure itself.
The latrine is considered attached.
2
Classify items by attachment
The building, upper storey, and latrine are physically attached to the land (arsa). The key is not attached. This distinction determines the basis for inclusion.
Attachment here means physical connection that provides stability.
3
Apply the principle of taba'iyyah
According to Hanafi law, attached items enter the sale by way of taba'iyyah (accessoriness). Because the land (arsa) is the principal (asl), the building and its permanent attachments follow it.
This is based on legal analogy (qiyas) with the building itself.
4
Identify the exception: the key
The key is not attached, so by pure analogy it would not be included. However, it is included because of custom (urf): sellers customarily give the keys, and buyers expect them. Custom can override the default rule.
Custom must be prevalent and not contrary to a clear text.
5
Synthesize the legal outcome
Thus, the building and attached structures (upper storey, latrine) enter by attachment; the key enters by custom. No explicit mention is needed for these items in the contract.
Unattached items not covered by custom require explicit mention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الحقوق والاستحقاق فصل في الحقوق [1] (يَدْخُلُ البناء، والمفتاح، والعلو، والكنيف في بيع الدار) ١ ] قوله : باب الحقوق والاستحقاق ; لَمّا كانت الحقوق توابع، فذكرها بعد مسائل البيوع، وأوردها عقيب باب الرِّبا ; لأنَّ فيه بيان فضل حرام، وهاهنا بيان فضل على المبيع، وهو حلال، وفي هذا الترتيب اتِّباعُ صاحب «الجامع الصغير» أيضاً، والحقوق جمعُ حق وهو خلاف الباطل، وهو مصدر، حق الشيء من بابي ضرب وقتل ، إذا وجب وثبت ; ولهذا يقال لمرافق الدار : حقوقها. كذا في «المصباح المنير»، وقال في «القاموس» (۲) : الحقُّ من أسمائِهِ تعالى، ومن صفاته، والقرآن، وضد الباطل، والأمر المقتضى به ،والموت والحرم ، والحقة أخص منه، وحقيقة الأمر. انتهى. والاستحقاق هو طلب الحق، قال في «المصباح»(۳) : استحق فلان الأمر : استوجبه ، قاله الفارابي وجماعة ، فالأمرُ مستحق بالفتح : اسم مفعول، ومنه خروج المبيع مستحقاً. [٢]قوله : يدخل ... الخ ; لأنَّ العرصة أصل في الدار; لقرار البناء عليها، وإنما دخل البناء، وما اتصل به في بيع الدَّارِ بطريق التبعية ; لاتصاله بالعرصة اتصال قرار، فما لا يكون متصلاً بالبناء لا يدخلُ في بيع الدَّارِ إلا إذا جرى العرف أنَّ البائع يسامح به يدخل بلا ذكر، فيدخل العلو بلا ذكر لاتصاله بالبناء، ولا يدخلُ المفتاح في القياس; لأنه غير متصل بالبناء.
Chapter of Rights and Entitlement; Section on Rights [1] (The building, the key, the upper storey, and the latrine are included in the sale of a house.) 1] His saying: Chapter of Rights and Entitlement; since rights are appurtenances, he mentioned them after the issues of sales, and he presented them after the chapter on riba, because in it is a statement of prohibited surplus, and here is a statement of surplus on the sold item, which is lawful. In this arrangement, he also followed the author of al-Jami' al-Saghir. Rights (huquq) is the plural of haqq, which is the opposite of falsehood. It is a verbal noun; haqqa al-shay' (the thing became due) from the patterns daraba and qatala, meaning it became obligatory and established. Hence, the conveniences of a house are called its rights. Thus in al-Misbah al-Munir. Al-Qamus says: al-Haqq is among His names, may He be exalted, and among His attributes, the Qur'an, the opposite of falsehood, the matter that is required, death, the Sacred Precinct (al-Haram), and al-Hiqqah is more specific than it, and the reality of the matter. End. And al-istihqaq is the seeking of right. Al-Misbah says: Istahaqqa fulan al-amr: he deserved it, said by al-Farabi and others. So al-amr mustahaqq (with fatha) is a passive participle; from it is the exit of the sold item as mustahaqq (claimed). [2] His saying: 'includes... etc.' Because the court (arsa) is the origin of the house, as it is the place for the building. The building and what is attached to it enter the sale of the house by way of being appurtenances (taba'iyyah) due to its attachment to the court with an attachment of stability. So whatever is not attached to the building does not enter the sale of the house unless custom has it that the seller is lenient, then it enters without mention. Thus the upper storey enters without mention due to its attachment to the building, and the key does not enter by qiyas because it is not attached to the building.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage opens a new section on rights (huquq) and entitlement (istihqaq) in the context of sales. The ruling at hand is: 'The building, the key, the upper storey, and the latrine are included in the sale of a house.' The commentator explains that this chapter follows the chapter on riba because riba discussed unlawful surplus, while here the surplus (e.g., the key) is lawful as part of the sale. The term 'haqq' is defined in linguistic and legal terms, and 'istihqaq' is defined as seeking a right. The core ruling is based on the principle of taba'iyyah (accessoriness): the building (including its attached parts) enters the sale by virtue of attachment to the land (arsa), which is the principal (asl). The key is not attached, so it would not enter by qiyas (analogy), but it is included by custom (urf), as sellers typically include it. The upper storey is attached to the building, so it is included. The latrine, being attached, is also included. The key's inclusion is an exception based on custom, not pure legal analogy.

Key Terms
حق (haqq): A right or entitlement; in this context, appurtenances to a sold property that are included in the sale by way of accessory.
استحقاق (istihqaq): The act of claiming a right; in sales, it refers to the existence of a right in the sold item that can be claimed by someone else, e.g., 'خروج المبيع مستحقاً' meaning the sold item is found to be owned by another.
تبعية (taba'iyyah): Accessoriness; a legal principle where an inferior item follows the principal item in a contract without explicit mention, due to strong connection or custom.
العرصة (al-arsa): The land or court of a house; the principal part upon which the building is erected.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the upper storey enter the sale of a house without explicit mention?
A) Because it is customary.
B) Because it is attached to the building.
C) Because it is a right.
D) Because the seller is lenient.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sales law, attached structures enter a house sale by taba'iyyah, while unattached items like the key enter by custom, not by analogy.

Default Inclusions in a House Sale: Attachment and Custom
Commentary on default inclusions in house sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which items are included by default in a Hanafi house sale and explain the legal basis of attachment (ittisal) and custom (urf/istihsan).

Core Idea
Permanently attached fixtures enter by subsidiary; unattached movables enter only by custom, not by qiyas.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi default rule for inclusions in a house sale. The commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains how attachment to the plot (arsa) makes the building and fixtures enter by subsidiary, while the key enters by istihsan (customary lenience). The shade structure (zullah) is an exception—it requires explicit mention.

Guided Path
1
List the default items
Read the matn: the building, key, upper floor, and privy enter the sale. Identify each item.
The privy (kanif) is attached; the key is movable.
2
Understand the principle of attachment
The commentary states that the plot (arsa) is the origin, and the building enters by subsidiary (taba'iyyah) due to permanent attachment (ittisal qarar). Items physically attached to the building follow this rule.
Attachment determines inclusion by qiyas.
3
Distinguish qiyas and istihsan
The key is not attached, so by qiyas it would not be included. However, custom (urf) that sellers usually allow the key makes it enter by istihsan. This shows how Hanafi fiqh balances analogy and custom.
Istihsan overrides qiyas here.
4
Identify the exception: the zullah
The shade structure (zullah) is not included by default, even though it may be attached. It only enters if explicitly mentioned or if the sale includes 'all rights' or 'appurtenances'.
The commentary treats it like a garment placed in the house.
5
Apply the rule to other items
The commentary gives examples: a ladder attached to the building enters; a detached ladder does not. A placed door does not enter. Practice distinguishing attachment.
Attachment is key, but custom can override.

المبيع مستحقاً.

لأنه غير متصل بالبناء.
