Chapter of Rights and Entitlement. Section on Rights. The building, key, upper floor, and privy enter into the sale of a house. Chapter of Rights and Entitlement. Section on Rights. [1] Statement: 'The building, key, upper floor, and privy enter into the sale of a house.' 1) His statement: 'Chapter of Rights and Entitlement'; since rights are accessories, he mentioned them after the issues of sales, and presented them after the chapter on riba because that chapter explains the excess that is forbidden, while here [the topic] is the explanation of the excess on the sold item, which is lawful. In this arrangement, there is also following the author of al-Jami' al-Saghir. Rights (huquq) is the plural of haqq, which is the opposite of falsehood, and it is a verbal noun; haqqa al-shay' from the patterns daraba and qatala means it became obligatory and established. Hence, the appurtenances of a house are called its 'rights'. So it is in al-Misbah al-Munir. And in al-Qamus: 'Haqq is among His names, may He be exalted, and among His attributes, and the Qur'an, and the opposite of falsehood, and the matter that is required, and death, and the sanctuary, and the specific haqq (hiqqa) is more specific than it, and the reality of the matter.' End. And al-istihqaq is the demand for a right. He said in al-Misbah: 'He deserved the matter: he merited it,' said by al-Farabi and a group. So the matter is mustahaqq (with fatha) as a passive participle. From this is the expression 'the sold item turning out to be entitled [to another]'. [2] His statement: 'he enters… etc.' because the plot (arsa) is the origin in the house due to the settlement of the building upon it. The building and what is attached to it enter into the sale of the house by way of subsidiary because of their attachment to the plot with a settlement attachment. So what is not attached to the building does not enter into the sale of the house unless the custom is that the seller is lenient with it, so it enters without mention. Thus, the upper floor enters without mention due to its attachment to the building. The key does not enter in qiyas because it is not attached to the building. Not the shade structure (zullah) except with the mention of every right that belongs to it, or with its appurtenances, or with every small and great thing that is in it or from it. The privy is the restroom. Not the shade structure. In al-Mughrib: 'The shade structure (zullah) of the house is the canopy above the door.' And from the author of al-Hasr: 'It is that whose one end of its beams is on this house and its other end on the opposite neighbor's wall.' Except with the mention of every right that belongs to it, or with its appurtenances, or with every small and great thing that is in it or from it. So it becomes like a garment placed in the house. But it enters in istihsan because it is customary that the seller does not prevent it from the buyer, but rather delivers it with the house to him. The placed door, nor the excess, nor its key do not enter. The ladder attached to the building enters even if made of wood, and the unattached does not enter. [1] His statement: 'In al-Mughrib… etc.' In al-Mughrib: 'The statement of the jurists: they mean by 'zullah of the house' the canopy over the door.' And he claimed in Idah al-Islah that this is mistaken; rather it is the sabat (covered passage) whose one end is on the house and the other on another house or on the columns in the alley. This is what is followed in Fath al-Qadir and others. So it is in Radd al-Muhtar, quoting from al-Nahr. [2] His statement: 'Except with mention… etc.', i.e., the shade structure does not enter into the sale of the house except with the mention of every right that belongs to it – i.e., to the house – or with its appurtenances, meaning by mentioning the appurtenances of the house which are its rights, so he says: 'I sold you this house with its appurtenances.'
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This is a passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah explaining what is included by default in the sale of a house. The author begins with the matn stating that the building (building materials), the key, the upper floor, and the privy (toilet) are included in the sale of a house. The commentary then explains why: the plot (arsa) is the primary subject of the sale, and the building and attached fixtures enter by way of subsidiary (taba'iyyah) because they are permanently attached. Unattached items do not enter by default but may enter by custom (urf) if sellers are lenient. For example, the key is not included by qiyas (analogy) because it is not attached, but it is included by istihsan due to customary usage. Similarly, the upper floor is included because it is attached. The shade structure (zullah) is not included unless explicitly mentioned alongside all rights or appurtenances. The commentary distinguishes between different definitions of zullah: some say it is the canopy over the door, while others say it is the covered passage (sabat) between houses. The passage also clarifies that a ladder attached to the building enters, but a detached ladder does not. This shows the Hanafi reliance on custom and istihsan alongside qiyas.

Key Terms
الظلة (az-zullah): A shade structure; commentary defines it as a canopy over the door or a covered passage (sabat) between houses.
الاستحقاق (al-istihqaq): Demand for a right; derived from haqq, meaning entitlement.
الكنيف (al-kunayf): The privy or restroom; included in sale of house as it is attached to the building.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is NOT included by default in the sale of a house without explicit mention?
A) The building
B) The upper floor
C) The shade structure (zullah)
D) The privy

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi house sales, permanently attached items enter by subsidiary; unattached items like the key enter by custom, but the shade structure requires explicit mention.

Inclusion of the Canopy (Zullah) in House Sale
Intra-Hanafi dispute on canopy inclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the inclusion of a canopy (zullah) in a house sale and apply the rule to cases based on custom.

Core Idea
The canopy's inclusion depends on whether its door is inside the house; otherwise, rights must be mentioned.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether a canopy (zullah) attached to a house is automatically included in its sale. Imam Abu Hanifa held that explicit mention of rights is required, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) considered it a dependency if the door opens inside. The ruling also considers custom: if the house is built on the canopy, it is included without mention.

Guided Path
1
Define the canopy (zullah) and its physical characteristics
The zullah is a shed with one end on the house and the other on a neighbor's wall or columns in the lane. Distinguish it from a sabat (covered passageway).
The definition clarifies that the canopy is not entirely within the house property.
2
Identify the general ruling from the matn
The matn states: 'The canopy is not included except with the mention of every right belonging to it, or with its appurtenances, or with every small or large thing that is in it or from it.'
This is the default rule from the earlier text being commented on.
3
Isolate Imam Abu Hanifa's position and his reasoning
Abu Hanifa argues the canopy has a dual nature: one end on the house (making it a dependency) and the other on a neighbor's wall (making it not a pure dependency). Thus, explicit mention of rights is needed to resolve the doubt.
This is based on qiyas with similar ambiguous cases.
4
Isolate the Sahibayn's position and their reasoning
The Sahibayn hold that if the canopy's door is inside the house, it is like a toilet or upper floor—a clear dependency. Therefore, it is included without explicit mention.
They rely on custom (urf) and istihsan.
5
Apply the exception: house built on the canopy
If the house is built outside on the canopy, custom dictates that the canopy is considered part of the house and is included without mention, even according to Abu Hanifa.
This shows that custom can override the default rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if the house is built outside on the canopy, it enters the sale of the house without mentioning the rights, because it is considered from the house customarily, as they explicitly stated.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the inclusion of a canopy (zullah) in the sale of a house. The author first states the general ruling: the canopy is not included in the sale unless explicitly mentioned along with every right belonging to it, its appurtenances, or every small or large thing in or from it. The text then defines 'zullah' and discusses differing lexical opinions. The key ruling is that according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the canopy is not included without explicit mention of rights because one end rests on the neighbor's wall, making it partially independent. However, according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), if the canopy's door is inside the house, it is considered a dependency like a toilet or upper floor and is included without mention. The passage reconciles these views by noting that mentioning rights resolves the ambiguity. It also adds that if a house is built outside on a canopy, the canopy is included without mention due to custom. The text then transitions to the rules for trees and crops, which are covered in the next lesson.

Key Terms
ظلة (zullah): A canopy or shed attached to a house, with one end on the house wall and the other on the neighbor's wall or columns in the lane.
مرافق (marafiq): Appurtenances of a house, including rights of way, water rights, etc.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; here used to include the canopy in sale because of custom, even though analogy might exclude it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The inclusion of a canopy in a house sale depends on its degree of attachment to the house and the mention of rights, with the Sahibayn looking to the location of its door and custom overriding the default rule when the house is built on the canopy.

Inclusion of Trees, Crops, and Fruits in Sales
Rulings on inclusion of trees, crops, and fruits in land and tree sales.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for inclusion of attached items in land sales by identifying the underlying illa of stability.

Core Idea
Trees are included in land sale like a building; crops and fruits are not, because their attachment is temporary.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya discusses which items are automatically included when land or trees are sold, and which require explicit mention. The ruling hinges on the nature of attachment: permanent stability (li al-qarar) versus temporary connection. Understanding this distinction is crucial for drafting valid sale contracts and resolving disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three items
The passage covers three types of items attached to land: trees (shajar), crops (zar'), and fruits (thamar). Note the default ruling for each.
Trees are included by default; crops and fruits are not.
2
Understand the qiyas for trees
Trees are analogized to buildings because both are attached for stability (li al-qarar). Since a building is included in a land sale without mention, trees are similarly included.
The illa is 'attachment for stability'.
3
Contrast crops and fruits
Crops are attached temporarily, like movable goods (mata'), so they are not included. Fruits, though attached firmly, are intended to be cut, so they are not included unless specified.
The purpose (cutting) overrides the firm connection for fruits.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on trees
Some Hanafi scholars differentiate trees by size or fruit-bearing, but the relied-upon view (asahh) is unconditional inclusion of all trees.
This ikhtilaf does not affect the general rule.
5
Apply the rule consistently
Use the stability criterion: if the item is attached for permanence, it is included; if for temporary use or harvest, it is not. The fetus exception (part of mother) confirms the principle.
Always check custom (urf) as a secondary factor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: والشجر ...الخ; أي يدخل الشجر في بيع الأرض، وإن لم يذكر صغيراً كان أو كبيراً، مثمراً كان أو غير مثمر، وهذا هو الأصح. ومنهم من قال: المثمر يدخل من غير ذكر، وغير المثمر لا يدخل من غير ذكر. ومنهم من قال: الكبيرة إذا كانت مثمرة تدخل من غير ذكر، وإن كانت غير مثمرة لا تدخل، والصغيرة لا تدخل إلا بالذكر مثمرة كانت أو غير مثمرة، ذكره البرجندي، وتفصيل الجزئيات في فتاوى الثقات. لا الزرع; لا يدخل الزرع في بيع الأرض بلا ذكره، والفرق بينهما أن اتصال الشجر بالأرض للقرار، فكان كالبناء، واتصال الزرع بها لا للقرار، فكان كالمتاع، وإنما دخل الجنين في بيع أمه من غير ذكره مع أن اتصاله لا للقرار؛ لأنه جزء منها. وفي «جامع الاسبيجابي»: الزرع إنما لا يدخل قبل النبات وبعد التقوم، وأما إذا نبت ولم يصر متقوماً فإنه يدخل، والأول أصح، ذكره في «كمال الدراية». [2] قوله: ولا الثمر ... الخ; يعني لا يدخل الثمر - بالثاء المثلثة والميم المفتوح - في بيع شجر فيه ذلك الثمر إلا بشرطه، سواء كان لذلك الثمر قيمة وقت البيع أو لم يكن؛ لأن اتصال الثمر بأصله وإن كان اتصال قوام كاتصال الشجر بالأرض، إلا أن قطع الثمر مقصود بخلاف الشجر.
His saying: And the tree...etc.; meaning, the tree enters the sale of land, even if not mentioned, whether small or large, fruit-bearing or not fruit-bearing, and this is the most correct. And among them is one who said: the fruit-bearing enters without mention, and the non-fruit-bearing does not enter without mention. And among them is one who said: the large (tree) if it is fruit-bearing enters without mention, and if it is non-fruit-bearing it does not enter, and the small (tree) does not enter except with mention whether fruit-bearing or non-fruit-bearing, mentioned by al-Barjandi, and the detail of particulars is in the Fatawa of the reliable. Not the crop; the crop does not enter the sale of land without its mention, and the difference between them is that the connection of trees to the land is for stability, so it is like a building, and the connection of crops to it is not for stability, so it is like goods. And the fetus enters the sale of its mother without mention even though its connection is not for stability, because it is a part of her. And in Jami' al-Isbijabi: the crop does not enter before growth and after becoming valuable, but if it has grown but not yet become valuable, it does enter, and the first is more correct, mentioned in Kamal al-Diraya. [2] His saying: And not the fruit...etc.; meaning, the fruit - with the dotted tha and open mim - does not enter the sale of a tree that has that fruit except with its condition, whether that fruit has value at the time of sale or not; because the connection of fruit to its origin, although it is a firm connection like the connection of trees to the earth, yet cutting the fruit is intended unlike the tree.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage provides the rulings on whether trees, crops, and fruits are included in sales. The author begins by stating that trees are automatically included in the sale of land, regardless of size or fruit-bearing status, and this is the relied-upon (asahh) view. He then mentions variant opinions among later scholars: some differentiate based on fruit-bearing, others based on size. The correct view, however, is comprehensive inclusion. For crops (zar'), the ruling is the opposite: they are not included in the sale of land unless explicitly mentioned. The underlying rationale (illa) is that trees are attached to the land for stability (li al-qarar), akin to a building, while crops are attached for temporary growth, akin to movable goods. An exception is noted: a fetus in the womb is included in the sale of its mother even though its connection is not for stability, because it is considered a part of her. The passage also cites a minority view from Jami' al-Isbijabi that crops grown but not yet valuable may be included, but the contrary is stronger. Finally, fruits (thamar) on a tree are not included in the sale of the tree unless specifically conditioned, regardless of whether they have value, because the purpose is to cut the fruit, unlike the tree itself which remains.

Key Terms
شجر (shajar): Trees (any kind) attached to land for stability.
زرع (zar'): Crops planted in land, not for permanent stability.
ثمر (thamar): Fruit on a tree at the time of sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, why are trees included in the sale of land without mention?
A) Because they are valuable.
B) Because they are attached to the land for stability, like a building.
C) Because the buyer always wants them.
D) Because the seller intends to include them.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sales law, items attached for permanence (trees) are included automatically, while temporary items (crops and fruits) require explicit mention.

Inclusion of Trees and Crops in Sale of Land
Ruling on automatic inclusion of trees and exclusion of crops in land sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on automatic inclusion of trees and crops in a land sale, and apply the distinction based on attachment permanence.

Core Idea
Trees are part of the land in sale; crops are not — attachment for permanence vs. temporary.

When buying land, what exactly are you buying — the trees on it? The crops? Islamic sales law draws a clear line based on how things are attached. Trees, like buildings, are considered part of the land. Crops, like furniture, are not. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi position, the reasoning, and some fine points about immature crops and the fetus exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Read the first sentence: trees are included even if not mentioned; crops are not. Note that this is stated as the most correct view (al-asahh).
2
Examine the variants for trees
The text mentions that some Hanafis differentiate based on fruit-bearing or size. List these variants and note that the accepted position is all trees included.
Al-Birjandi's view: large fruit-bearing trees included, small trees excluded.
3
Understand the distinction between trees and crops
The reason given: trees are attached for permanence (like a building), crops are attached temporarily (like goods). This illa determines inclusion.
Contrast with the fetus exception: fetus is included because it is part of the mother, despite temporary attachment.
4
Consider the nuance about crops before and after sprouting
According to 'Jami' al-Isbijabi', if crops have sprouted but not yet become valuable (taqawwum), they are included. But the author says the first view (crops never included) is more correct.
Taqawwum here means the crops have reached a stage where they have monetary value (e.g., nearly harvestable).
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Given a land sale contract without mention of vegetation, determine what passes to the buyer. Use the rule: all trees pass; crops do not unless explicitly stated.
If crops were sprouting but not yet valuable, the minority view would include them; but mufta bihi excludes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[١] قوله : والشجر ...الخ ؛ أي يدخلُ الشجرُ في بيع الأرض، وإن لم يذكر صغيراً كان أو كبيراً ، مثمراً كان أو غير مثمر، وهذا هو الأصح.
ومنهم من قال : المثمر يدخل من غير ذكر ، وغير المثمر لا يدخلُ من غير ذكر. ومنهم من قال : الكبيرة إذا كانت مثمرة تدخلُ من غير ذكر، وان كانت غير مثمرة لا تدخل، والصغيرة لا تدخل إلا بالذكر مثمرة كانت أو غير مثمرة، ذكـره البرْجَندِي، وتفصيل الجزئيات في فتاوى الثقات.
لا الزرع ؛ لا يدخل الزرعُ في بيع الأرض بلا ذكره، والفرق بينهما أن اتصال الشجر بالأرض للقرار، فكان كالبناء، واتّصالُ الزرع بها لا للقرار، فكان كالمتاع ، وإنما دخل الجنين في بيع أمه من غير ذكرِهِ مع أنَّ اتصاله لا للقرار ؛ لأنه جزء منها. وفي «جامع الاسبيجابي»: الزرعُ إنما لا يدخلُ قبل النبات وبعد التقوم، وأما إذا نبت ولم يصر متقوماً فإنه يدخل ، والأوّلُ أصح، ذكره في «كمال الدراية).
[1] His saying: 'And the trees, not the crops...' etc.; meaning: trees are included in the sale of land, even if not mentioned, whether small or large, fruit-bearing or not, and this is the most correct view. Some said: fruit-bearing trees are included without mention, non-fruit-bearing are not. Some said: large fruit-bearing trees are included, large non-fruit-bearing are not, and small trees are not included without mention whether fruit-bearing or not, as mentioned by al-Birjandi; details are in reliable fatwas. Not the crops: crops are not included in the sale of land without mention. The difference between them is that the attachment of trees to the land is for permanence, so they are like a building, while the attachment of crops to it is not for permanence, so they are like goods. The fetus is included in the sale of its mother without mention even though its attachment is not for permanence, because it is a part of her. In 'Jami' al-Isbijabi': crops are not included before sprouting and after becoming valuable; but if they have sprouted and not yet become valuable, they are included; the first is more correct, mentioned in 'Kamil al-Dirayah'.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn ruling regarding what is included in the sale of land. The author clarifies that trees are included automatically, regardless of size or fruit-bearing status, according to the most correct position. There are variant views within the Hanafi school: some differentiate based on fruit-bearing, others based on size. However, crops are never included without explicit mention because their attachment is temporary, analogous to movable goods. The exception is the fetus in animals, which is included because it is considered a part of the mother. A nuance is mentioned from al-Isbijabi: crops that have sprouted but not yet become valuable are included, but the accepted view is the opposite.

Key Terms
شجر (shajar): Trees, any kind, attached to land for permanence.
زرع (zar'): Crops, planted vegetation not for permanent attachment.
تقوم (taqawwum): Becoming valuable (as property with monetary worth).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the most correct Hanafi position, which of the following are automatically included in the sale of land?
A) Both trees and crops.
B) Trees only, regardless of size or fruit.
C) Only fruit-bearing trees.
D) Only large trees.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, trees are considered part of the land in a sale because of permanent attachment, while crops are not; this distinction prevents gharar by setting clear default rules.

Fruit Not Included in Sale of Trees Without Stipulation
Explains that fruit on trees is not included in sale of trees unless stipulated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the inclusion of fruit in the sale of trees, explain the Hanafi proof using qiyas, and identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) with the opposing schools.

Core Idea
Fruit on trees is separate property; the seller must remove it before delivery unless stipulated otherwise.

This passage addresses a common point of confusion: when you buy a tree with fruit on it, do you automatically get the fruit? The Hanafi school says no—the fruit belongs to the seller and must be cut and delivered separately. Other schools disagree, relying on custom. Let's explore the reasoning behind the Hanafi position and its implications.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify clearly what the Hanafi school holds: fruit is not included in the sale of trees, whether it has value or not. The seller must cut the fruit and deliver the tree.
Note that this applies even if the fruit has no value; only explicit stipulation includes it.
2
Identify the opposing view and its proof
Mālik, al-Shāfiʿī, and Aḥmad hold that fruit is included until it ripens, based on customary delivery (ʿurf). Understand their argument: it is not customary to cut fruit before it is ready.
The opposing dalil is based on custom and analogy to a lease where crops are left.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response (lana) argues that the seller's obligation is absolute delivery (taslīm), which requires emptying the property of his own belongings. Fruit and crops are like movable goods (متاع) that must be removed.
The qiyas is to a land sale with the seller's goods on it: the seller must remove them.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Hanafi law, the buyer gets the tree without fruit; the seller must harvest. Under the other schools, the buyer gets the fruit once ripe, benefiting him.
This difference affects pricing and contractual clarity.
5
Examine the exception for valueless fruit and the scope of 'rights'
Some argued that valueless fruit goes to the buyer, but the text rejects this. Also, mentioning 'rights' or 'appurtenances' does not include fruit or crops; only an unqualified 'every little and much' does.
This clarifies that fruit/crops are not considered appurtenances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢]قوله : ولا الثمر ... الخ ؛ يعني لا يدخلُ الثمَرُ - بالثاء المثلثة والميم المفتوح – في بيع شجر فيه ذلك الثمر إلا بشرطه، سواء كان لذلك الثمر قيمة وقت البيع أو لم يكن ؛ لأنَّ اتصال الثمر بأصلِهِ وإن كان اتصال قوام كاتّصالِ الشجرِ بالأرض ، إلا أنَّ قطع الثمـر لـه غاية معلومة، فصار في حكم المقطوع، ويقال للبائع : اقطع الثمرة وسلم الشجر، وكذا إذا بيعت الأرض وبها زرع يؤمَرُ البائع بالحصاد والتسليم. وقال مالك والشافعي وأحمد الله : يترك الثمر حتى يظهر صلاحه، ويترك الزرع حتى يستحصد ؛ لأنَّ الواجب التسليم المعتاد ، ولم تَجْرِ العادة بقطع الثمر قبل بدء صلاحه، ولا حصاد الزرع قبل استحصاده، فصار كما لو مضت مدة الإجارة وفي الأرض زرع لم يدرك. ولنا : إنَّ التسليم واجب على البائع، ولا يتمكن منه إلا بالتفريغ، ولا يحصلُ التفريغ في الثمر والزرع إلا بالقطع والحصاد فيلزمانه ، وصار كما لو بيعت أرض وفيها متاع للبائع، حيث يؤمر بقلعه وتسليمها للمشتري. وقيل : من اشترى شجراً عليه ثمر لا قيمة له فهو للمشتري ؛ لأن بيعه على الانفراد لا يجوز. ورد هذا القول بأن بيعه على الصحيح على الانفراد يجوز، فلا يدخل في بيع الشجر بلا ذكر. ولو ذكر في بيع الأرض أو بيع الشجرِ الحقوق أو المرافق لا يدخلُ الزرعُ ولا الثمر حينئذ ؛ لأنهما ليسا من الحقوق والمرافق ، وكذا إن قال : بكل قليل وكثير هو فيها ، أو منها، وقال : أثر ذلك من حقوقها أو قال : أثر ذلك من مرافقها، وإن لم يقل : أثر ذلك دخلا في البيع ؛ لأنهما من القليل والكثيرِ الذي هو فيها أو منها للاتصال في الحال، ذكره في «كمال الدراية»(1).
[2] His saying: 'And not the fruit...' etc.; meaning: the fruit – with thā and mīm fatḥa – is not included in the sale of a tree on which that fruit is present unless stipulated, whether the fruit has value at the time of sale or not; because the attachment of the fruit to its origin, though it is a sustaining attachment like the attachment of trees to the earth, yet cutting the fruit has a known limit, so it is considered as already cut. The seller is told: cut the fruit and deliver the tree. Similarly, if land is sold with crops on it, the seller is ordered to harvest and deliver. Mālik, al-Shāfiʿī, and Aḥmad (may Allāh have mercy on them) said: the fruit is left until it appears ripe, and the crop is left until it is ready for harvest, because the obligatory delivery is customary, and it is not customary to cut fruit before its ripening begins, nor to harvest crops before they are ready; it is like when the lease period has passed and there is unripe crop on the land. Our proof: delivery is obligatory on the seller, and he cannot accomplish it except by emptying (the property), and emptying of fruit and crop is only achieved by cutting and harvesting, so these become obligatory on him. It is like when land is sold and there is goods of the seller on it, where he is ordered to remove them and deliver the land to the buyer. It was said: one who buys trees with fruit having no value, the fruit is for the buyer, because its separate sale is not valid. This statement is refuted: its separate sale is valid according to the correct view, so it is not included in the sale of the tree without mention. If he mentions in the sale of land or trees the rights or appurtenances, crops and fruit are not included even then, because they are not among rights and appurtenances. Similarly, if he says: 'with every little and much that is in it or from it', and he says: 'following that from its rights' or 'from its appurtenances', but if he does not say 'following that', they are included in the sale, because they are among the little and much that is in it or from it due to physical attachment at the moment, mentioned in 'Kāmil al-Dirāyah'.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and an inter-madhhab dispute on the inclusion of fruit in the sale of trees. The Hanafi position is that fruit is not automatically included because it will eventually be cut and is thus treated as separate property. The seller must cut the fruit and deliver the tree. The majority (Mālik, Shāfiʿī, Aḥmad) argue that it is customary to leave fruit until ripening, so the sale includes it. The Hanafi response (lana) is that the seller must deliver the property free of his own property; since the fruit belongs to the seller, he must remove it. The text also addresses the case of valueless fruit: some said it is for the buyer, but this is rejected because even valueless fruit can be sold separately. Finally, the text clarifies that mentioning 'rights' or 'appurtenances' does not include fruit or crops; only if the seller says 'every little and much that is in it' without restricting to rights, then fruit and crops are included due to their physical attachment at the moment.

Key Terms
ثمر (thamar): Fruit on trees, considered as separate property due to eventual cutting.
حقوق (huquq): Rights or appurtenances that are permanently attached to the property.
مرافق (marāfiq): Appurtenances or facilities attached to the property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if someone buys a tree with fruit on it without stipulating the fruit, what is the ruling?
A) The fruit belongs to the buyer automatically.
B) The seller must cut the fruit and deliver the tree.
C) The fruit is left on the tree until it ripens.
D) The buyer has the option to keep the fruit or demand removal.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, fruit on trees is considered separate property because it has a known cutting time; therefore, it is not included in the sale of trees unless explicitly stipulated.

Inclusion via 'Bi Kulli Haqq' in Sales
Clarifies the scope of 'bi kulli haqq'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on which items are included when a seller says 'with every right' in a property sale, and distinguish between rights (huquq) and other items like fruit.

Core Idea
Saying 'with every right' only includes legal appurtenances, not produce or separate items.

In sales of real estate, sellers often add phrases like 'with everything attached' or 'with every right.' The Hanafi school precisely defines what falls under 'right' (haqq) in this context. This lesson examines a short passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explaining that mentioning 'rights' does not automatically include all contents of the property – only those things that are legally subordinate appurtenances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key phrase
Locate the phrase 'bi kulli haqq' (بكل حق) in the passage. Understand that 'haqq' here refers to a legal right or appurtenance, not general items.
'Haqq' is a technical term; it does not mean 'everything' in a literal sense.
2
Understand the exclusion rule
The passage states that mentioning 'haqq' does not include fruit or crops because a 'right' is subordinate to the property (tabi') and inferior (duna). Goods like fruit are independent and not subordinate.
This exclusion is based on the earlier context where fruit was explicitly excluded from rights.
3
Apply the rule to a scenario
Given a sale of a house with the phrase 'with every right,' determine which items are included: included – an easement (right of way); not included – fruit on trees, crops, or separate structures.
The key is to ask: Is this item a legal appurtenance subordinate to the property? If not, it is not included.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢]قوله : بكل حق؛ ولم يدخل بذكرِ الحقِّ ؛ لأنَّ حق الشيء تبع له فهو دونه، كذا في «الفتح».
[2] His saying: 'with every right'; and it does not enter by mentioning 'right', because the right of a thing is subordinate to it and is inferior to it. This is as in 'Fatḥ al-Qadīr'.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This brief note explains the phrase 'bi kulli haqq' (with every right) in the context of selling a house. The author clarifies that simply mentioning 'rights' does not include items like fruit or crops, because a 'right' is something subordinate to the main property and less significant. The earlier passage already stated that fruit and crops are not considered rights or appurtenances. The point here is that even if the seller says 'with every right', it does not expand inclusion to things that are not rights.

Key Terms
حق (haqq): A right or entitlement that is subordinate to the property, such as an easement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The fruit is included because it is a right.
B) The fruit is not included because it is not a right.
C) The fruit is included only if the buyer demands it.
D) The fruit is included if it is attached.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sales law, the phrase 'with every right' only covers legal appurtenances; it does not extend to produce or separate movable items.

Fruit and Crops in Sale of Trees and Land
Inter-madhhab dispute on cutting fruit and harvesting crops in sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on cutting fruit and harvesting crops upon sale, identify the opposing schools' argument, and explain the Hanafi qiyas-based rebuttal.

Core Idea
Seller must cut fruit and harvest crops to deliver empty property; custom does not override the obligation.

This passage presents a classic fiqh dispute between the Hanafi school and the majority regarding fruit and crops on sold trees/land. The Hanafi school argues that the seller must immediately cut and harvest, analogizing to movable goods. The opposing schools rely on custom. We will unpack the reasoning and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling
Identify the obligation: the seller must cut fruit and harvest crops to deliver the property empty.
The ruling applies to fruit with a known ripening time and any standing crops.
2
Identify the opposing view
Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad hold that the seller leaves fruit and crops until ripe/harvested.
Their reasoning is based on customary delivery.
3
Examine the opposing proof
They argue that custom does not require cutting before ripeness, and analogize to lease where unripe crop remains.
The lease analogy: after lease ends, unripe crop is not cut immediately.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school uses qiyas: just as land sold with movable goods requires removal, so too fruit/crops must be removed.
The illa is the need to vacate the property (tafriqh) for delivery.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: Hanafi – buyer can demand immediate cutting; other schools – seller waits until ripeness.
This affects ownership of the produce and timing of possession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا بيع الثمر له غاية معلومة، فصار في حكم المقطوع، ويقال للبائع : اقطع الثمرة وسلم الشجر، وكذا إذا بيعت الأرض وبها زرع يؤمَرُ البائع بالحصاد والتسليم. وقال مالك والشافعي وأحمد الله : يترك الثمر حتى يظهر صلاحه، ويترك الزرع حتى يستحصد ؛ لأنَّ الواجب التسليم المعتاد ، ولم تَجْرِ العادة بقطع الثمر قبل بدء صلاحه، ولا حصاد الزرع قبل استحصاده، فصار كما لو مضت مدة الإجارة وفي الأرض زرع لم يدرك. ولنا : إنَّ التسليم واجب على البائع، ولا يتمكن منه إلا بالتفريغ، ولا يحصلُ التفريغ في الثمر والزرع إلا بالقطع والحصاد فيلزمانه ، وصار كما لو بيعت أرض وفيها متاع للبائع، حيث يؤمر بقلعه وتسليمها للمشتري. وقيل : من اشترى شجراً عليه ثمر لا قيمة له فهو للمشتري ؛ لأن بيعه على الانفراد لا يجوز. ورد هذا القول بأن بيعه على الصحيح على الانفراد يجوز، فلا يدخل في بيع الشجر بلا ذكر. ولو ذكر في بيع الأرض أو بيع الشجرِ الحقوق أو المرافق لا يدخلُ الزرعُ ولا الثمر حينئذ ؛ لأنهما ليسا من الحقوق والمرافق ، وكذا إن قال : بكل قليل وكثير هو فيها ، أو منها، وقال : أثر ذلك من حقوقها أو قال : أثر ذلك من مرافقها، وإن لم يقل : أثر ذلك دخلا في البيع ؛ لأنهما من القليل والكثيرِ الذي هو فيها أو منها للاتصال في الحال، ذكره في «كمال الدراية».
If fruit is sold while it has a known end point, it falls under the ruling of cut fruit, and the seller is told: cut the fruit and hand over the tree. Similarly, if land is sold with crops on it, the seller is ordered to harvest and hand over. Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad said: the fruit is left until its ripeness becomes apparent, and the crop is left until it is harvested; because the required delivery is customary delivery, and custom does not involve cutting fruit before its ripeness begins, nor harvesting crop before it is ready. So it is like when the period of lease has passed and there is unripe crop on the land. Our proof: delivery is obligatory upon the seller, and he cannot accomplish it except by emptying, and emptying of fruit and crop is not achieved except by cutting and harvesting, so they are incumbent upon him. And it is like when land is sold with the seller's goods on it, where he is ordered to remove them and hand over to the buyer. And it was said: whoever buys a tree with fruit that has no value, it belongs to the buyer; because selling it separately is not permissible. This statement is refuted by the fact that selling it separately is permissible according to the correct view, so it is not included in the sale of the tree without mention. If he mentions in the sale of land or sale of trees the rights or appurtenances, the crop and fruit are not included then, because they are not among rights and appurtenances. Similarly, if he says: with every little and much that is in it or from it, and he says: effect of that from its rights or effect of that from its appurtenances, and if he does not say: effect of that, they are included in the sale; because they are from the little and much that is in it or from it due to connection at the time. Mentioned in Kamal al-Diraya.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute within the commentary on the sale of trees and land. The Hanafi position is that when a tree with fruit that has a known ripening time or land with crops is sold, the seller is obligated to cut the fruit and harvest the crops to deliver the property empty to the buyer. This is because the seller must deliver the tree or land, and delivery cannot be complete without removing the fruit/crops. The opposing schools (Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali) argue that the seller should leave the fruit and crops until they are ripe/harvested because customary delivery does not require cutting before that. The Hanafi counter-argument is based on an analogy (qiyas) to a case where land is sold with movable goods of the seller; in that case, the seller is ordered to remove the goods. Similarly, fruit and crops are like movable goods attached temporarily. The passage also addresses a secondary issue: if the fruit has no value, there is a difference of opinion on whether it is included; the correct view is that it is included only if explicitly mentioned. Finally, if the seller mentions 'rights' or 'appurtenances' in the sale, the fruit and crops are not included because they are not considered rights or appurtenances. However, if the seller says 'with every little and much' without specifying, they are included due to their physical connection.

Key Terms
ثمر (thamar): Fruit on a tree at the time of sale.
زرع (zar'): Crops growing on land at the time of sale.
تفريغ (tafriqh): The act of emptying the property of the seller's belongings to deliver it to the buyer.
حق (haqq): A right that is attached to property, such as a right of way or water.
مرافق (marafiq): Appurtenances, conveniences associated with property, like a toilet or storage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what must the seller do if he sells a tree with unripe fruit that has a known ripening time?
A) Leave the fruit until it ripens.
B) Cut the fruit and deliver the tree empty.
C) Ask the buyer for permission to keep the fruit.
D) The sale is invalid.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the seller must cut fruit and harvest crops to deliver the sold property empty, based on qiyas to movable goods, and this applies regardless of custom.

Upper Floor Not Included in Sale of a House (Bayt)
Ruling on non-inclusion of upper floor in sale of bayt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on what is included in the sale of a bayt and identify when specific mention is required.

Core Idea
A bayt does not include its equal; the upper floor is a separate dwelling requiring explicit mention.

In Hanafi sales law, the scope of a sold property is determined by the words used and customary understanding. The term 'bayt' (house) has a specific technical meaning that does not automatically encompass upper floors. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind the rule and its practical implications for property transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of the ruling
The ruling concerns the sale of a 'bayt' (house) 'bi kulli haqq' (with all rights). Determine what exactly is being sold and what the phrase 'all rights' typically covers.
The passage clarifies that 'all rights' does not include the upper floor.
2
Understand the definition of 'bayt'
The commentator defines 'bayt' as a single roofed structure intended for sleeping. This definition excludes spaces that are functionally similar but structurally separate, like an upper floor.
Some add a hallway (dihliz) as part of the bayt, but the upper floor is not mentioned.
3
Apply the rule: a thing does not include its like
Since the upper floor (uluww) is itself a roofed sleeping area (similar to a bayt), it cannot be considered a subordinate part. The principle is that a thing does not include its equal, and the upper floor is not inferior to the lower house.
Therefore, explicit mention is needed for inclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ولا العلو ... الخ ؛ لأنَّ البيت اسم لمسقف واحد جُعِلَ ليبات فيه ، ومنهم من يزيد له دهليزاً فإذا باع البيت لا يدخلُ العلو ما لم يذكر اسم العلو صريحاً ؛ لأن العلو مثله في أنه مسقف يباتُ فيه، والشيء لا يستتبع مثله ، بل هو أدنى منه ، كذا في «الفتح».
His saying: 'Nor the upper floor...' etc.; because 'bayt' is a name for a single roofed structure made for sleeping in. Some add a hallway. So if he sells the bayt, the upper floor is not included unless he explicitly mentions the name 'upper floor', because the upper floor is like it in that it is roofed and slept in, and a thing does not include its like; rather it is lower than it. Thus in al-Fath.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary on Al-Hidayah (or Al-Wiqayah). The matn stated that the upper floor (علو) is not included in the sale of a house (بيت) with all rights. The commentator explains why: the word 'bayt' specifically refers to a single roofed dwelling. An upper floor (علو) is an independent living space similar to a bayt. Since the rule is that a thing does not include its equivalent, the upper floor cannot be considered a subordinate part of the lower house. Therefore, it is only included if explicitly named. This ruling is based on the custom of Kufa, as noted later in the text.

Key Terms
علو ('uluww): An upper floor or room above the main house that is roofed and used for living.
بيت (bayt): A single roofed structure intended for sleeping, i.e., a room or house.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the upper floor (علو) not included when a bayt (house) is sold with all rights?
A) Because the upper floor is considered a separate property.
B) Because the upper floor is not a right or appurtenance.
C) Because the upper floor is equivalent to the bayt itself, and a thing does not include its like.
D) Because the buyer must specifically request it.

Key Takeaway
The upper floor is not considered a right of the bayt; it is a separate dwelling requiring explicit mention in the sale contract.

Inclusion of Upper Floor in Sale of Bayt, Manzil, and Dar
Detailed rules on inclusion of upper floor in sales of dwellings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for when the upper floor (علو) is included in the sale of a bayt, manzil, and dar, and explain the reasoning based on the definitions of each dwelling and the role of custom.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, inclusion of the upper floor depends on dwelling type and mention of rights—but custom may overrule.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah parses the matn on sales of dwellings. It defines three types of residential properties—bayt, manzil, dar—and specifies when the upper floor (علو) is included as an appurtenance. Understanding these distinctions is essential for drafting valid sale contracts and avoiding disputes over property boundaries.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three dwelling types
The passage defines bayt (single roofed room), manzil (multiple rooms with roofed courtyard and kitchen, no stable), and dar (compound with rooms, manzils, and unroofed courtyard).
Focus on the key features that distinguish each: presence of stable for manzil, unroofed courtyard for dar.
2
Determine the ruling for each
For dar, upper floor always included; for manzil, included only if rights/appurtenances mentioned; for bayt, not included even if mentioned.
Contrast the three rulings.
3
Understand the reasoning
The upper floor is similar to a bayt (its like), so it cannot be an appendage of a bayt; manzil is intermediate, so it can become appended when rights mentioned.
Key principle: 'a thing does not include its like, but only what is below it.'
4
Note the role of custom
The passage explains that this detailed ruling is based on Kufan custom; in other customs, the upper floor may be included in all types.
Custom can override the default classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا في شراء منزل إلا بذكرِ ما ذُكِرَ ولا الطريق، والشرب، والمسيل في البيع إلا بذكر ما ذُكِرَ أيضاً بخلاف الإجارة [۱] ولا في شراء منزل إلا بذكرِ ما ذُكِرَ) : أي الحقوق والمرافق إلى آخرها. فالحاصل أن العلو يدخل في بيع الدَّارِ وإن لم يذكر الحقوق والمرافق، ويدخل في بيع المنزل إن ذكر الحقوق والمرافق، ولا يدخل في بيع البيت وإن ذَكَرَ الحقوق والمرافق. فالمنزل فيما بين البيت والدَّارِ لا يكون فيه مربط الدواب، بل يكون فيه بيتان أو ثلاثة أو نحو ذلك، يتعيش فيه الرَّجلُ المتأهل، فالعلو يكون من توابعه لا من توابع البيت ؛ لأن الشيء لا يستتبع مثله ، بل دونَه. (ولا الطريق، والشرب، والمسيل في البيع إلا بذكرِ ما ذُكِرَ أيضاً بخلاف الإجارة) والعلو مثل البيت لا دونَه فلا يدخلُ فيه إلا بالتنصيص عليه، وفي «الكافي»: إنَّ هذا التفصيل مبني على عرف الكوفة ، وفي عرفنا : يدخلُ العلو في الكل سواء باع باسم البيت أو المنزل أو الدار، والأحكام تبتني على العرف في كل إقليم وفي كل عرف أهله. 11) قوله : ولا في شراء...الخ ؛ لأنَّ المنزل اسم لما يشتمل على بيوت وصحن مسقف، ومطبخ لسكنة الرجل بعياله، والبيتُ اسم لمسقف واحدٍ كما عرفت، والدار اسم لما يشتمل على بيوت ومنازل وصحن غير مسقف. فالمنزل يكون بين الدَّارِ والبيت إذ يتأتى فيه مرافق السكني بنوع قصور، بانتفاء منزل الدواب فيه ، فلشبهة بالدار يدخلُ العلو فيه تبعاً عند ذكر الحقوق، والشبه بالبيت لا يدخل فيه بدونه. [۲] قوله : يدخل ...الخ ؛ لأنَّ الدار لما كان اسماً لما يدار عليه الحدود من الحائط ويشتمل على بيوت ومنازل وصحن غير مسقف، والعلو من توابع الأصل وأجزائه، فيدخل فيه من غير ذكر. (۳) قوله : ولا الطريق... الخ ؛ أي لا يدخلُ الطريق في بيع ما له طريق، والشرب في بيع ما له شرب والمسيلُ في بيع ما له سيل، إلا بذكر الحقوق أو المرافق ؛ لأن كل واحدٍ
Nor in the purchase of a manzil except with mention of what was mentioned, nor the pathway, water right, and drainage in the sale except with mention of what was mentioned also, unlike lease. [1] 'Nor in the purchase of a manzil except with mention of what was mentioned': meaning rights and appurtenances to the end. The conclusion is that the upper floor is included in the sale of a dar even if rights and appurtenances are not mentioned, and is included in the sale of a manzil if rights and appurtenances are mentioned, and is not included in the sale of a bayt even if rights and appurtenances are mentioned. So the manzil is between the bayt and the dar: it does not have a stable for animals, but it has two or three rooms or so in which a married man lives. So the upper floor is among its appendages, not among the appendages of the bayt, because a thing does not include its like, but only what is below it. (Nor the pathway, water right, and drainage in the sale except with mention of what was mentioned also, unlike lease.) And the upper floor is similar to a bayt, not below it, so it is not included except by explicit stipulation. In al-Kafi: this detail is based on the custom of Kufa, and in our custom the upper floor is included in all, whether sold as bayt, manzil, or dar, and rulings are based on custom in every region and every custom of its people. 11) His saying: 'Nor in the purchase...' etc.; because 'manzil' is a name for something that includes rooms and a roofed courtyard and a kitchen for a man and his family. The 'bayt' is a name for a single roofed structure as you know, and 'dar' is a name for something that includes rooms, manzils, and an unroofed courtyard. So the manzil is between the dar and the bayt; it has living amenities with some deficiency, lacking a stable for animals. Due to its resemblance to a dar, the upper floor is included as a subordinate when rights are mentioned; due to its resemblance to a bayt, it is not included without mention. [2] His saying: 'It is included...' etc.; because 'dar' is a name for something that is bounded by walls and includes rooms, manzils, and an unroofed courtyard, and the upper floor is among the appendages and parts of the original, so it is included without mention. (3) His saying: 'Nor the pathway...' etc.; meaning the pathway is not included in the sale of something that has a pathway, nor the water right in the sale of something that has a water right, nor the drainage in the sale of something that has drainage, except with mention of rights or appurtenances; because each one...
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage parses the matn concerning the inclusion of the upper floor (علو) and easements (pathway, water right, drainage) in sales. It distinguishes between three types of dwellings: bayt (single room/house), manzil (dwelling with multiple rooms and courtyard but no stable), and dar (compound with rooms, manzils, and unroofed courtyard). The ruling: the upper floor is always included in a dar without mention; in a manzil, it is included only if rights and appurtenances are mentioned; in a bayt, it is never included even if rights are mentioned. This is because the upper floor is similar to a bayt, and a thing does not include its like. However, for a manzil, which is between a bayt and a dar, the upper floor becomes a subordinate due to resemblance to dar when rights are mentioned. The same principle applies to easements: they are not included unless rights or appurtenances are explicitly mentioned. Importantly, the commentator notes that this detailed ruling is based on the custom of Kufa. In other customs, such as in the commentator's own time and place, the upper floor is included in all types of sale, because rulings are built on custom. This highlights the flexibility of Hanafi fiqh in adapting to local 'urf.

Key Terms
دار (dar): A compound or house that includes multiple rooms, manzils, and an unroofed courtyard, bounded by walls.
منزل (manzil): A dwelling that has two or three rooms, a roofed courtyard, and a kitchen, but no stable for animals; suitable for a married man.
طريق (tariq): A pathway or right of way serving the property.
شرب (shurb): The right to water, e.g., for irrigation or drinking.
مسيل (masil): A drainage channel or right to drain water.
عرف ('urf): Custom, the prevailing practice in a locality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which statement is true about the inclusion of the upper floor in a sale?
A) It is always included in a dar but never in a bayt.
B) It is included in a manzil only if rights are mentioned.
C) It is included in a bayt only if rights are mentioned.
D) It is never included unless the property is a dar.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the inclusion of the upper floor in a sale depends on the type of dwelling and the mention of rights, but local custom can override this default ruling.

Inclusion of Easements and the Role of Custom in Sales
Rules on easements in sales and the role of custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain that easements (tariq, shurb, masil) are not included in a sale unless explicitly mentioned, and identify how custom can override the default ruling.

Core Idea
Easements require explicit mention in a sale; but custom can change this rule entirely.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah continues with the rule for easements: the right of way, water, and drainage are not included in the sale unless expressly mentioned. The commentator then notes that these detailed rulings (including the upper floor distinctions) are based on Kufan custom, and in other customs, the upper floor is always included. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that rulings may be based on custom, and custom can vary by region.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three easements
The text lists tariq (pathway), shurb (water right), masil (drainage).
These are rights, not physical parts of the property.
2
State the default rule
These are not included in the sale unless the seller explicitly mentions 'rights' or 'appurtenances'.
The same rule applies to each easement.
3
Note the exception for lease
The text says 'unlike lease', implying that in lease contracts, these may be included without mention.
The passage does not elaborate; this is a comparative point.
4
Understand the role of custom
The commentator from al-Kafi states that the entire detailed ruling is based on Kufan custom, and in the commentator's own custom, the upper floor is included in all sales. This shows that custom can override the detailed distinctions.
Custom may also affect easements, but the passage does not explicitly state that.

Inclusion of the Upper Floor in Sale: Bayt, Manzil, Dar
Commentary on inclusion of upper floor in sale of bayt, manzil, dar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi default ruling on whether the upper floor enters the sale of a bayt, manzil, or dar, and explain how local custom can override this default.

Core Idea
The upper floor enters automatically in dar, only with mention in manzil, never in bayt—unless local custom says otherwise.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah unpacks the Hanafi ruling on whether the upper floor (al-'ulu) is automatically included when selling different types of residential property. The ruling hinges on the linguistic and customary definitions of bayt (single room), manzil (dwelling with multiple rooms but no stable), and dar (home with courtyard). Crucially, the commentary reveals that the default rule is based on Kufan custom and can shift if the local custom differs—a living application of the Hanafi principle that rulings are built on custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three property types
First, recognize the terms bayt, manzil, and dar as defined in the passage. Note their characteristics: bayt is a single roofed room; manzil has multiple rooms and a roofed courtyard but no stable; dar includes rooms, dwellings, and an unroofed courtyard.
These definitions are based on the custom of Kufa.
2
Determine the default inclusion for each type
According to the text, for dar the upper floor enters automatically without mention. For manzil it enters only if 'rights and facilities' (huquq wa marafiq) are mentioned. For bayt it does not enter even with such mention.
The reason: a thing does not follow its like, only something lower.
3
Apply the custom override
The commentator reports from al-Kafi that this detail is based on Kufan custom. In his own custom ('urfuna), the upper floor enters in all three types regardless of the term used. This illustrates the principle 'al-ahkam tabtani 'ala al-'urf'.
The later custom overrides the previous one; the mufta bihi may differ by region.
4
Derive the operative ruling for fatwa
Thus, the mufta bihi today may vary. The commentator leans toward the later custom. One must ascertain the prevailing custom in one's locality to give a correct fatwa.
This is an example of istihsan based on urf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع / باب الحقوق والاستحقاق وبيع الفضول

ولا في شراء منزل إلا بذكرِ ما ذُكِرَ ولا الطريق، والشرب، والمسيل في البيع إلا بذكر ما ذُكِرَ أيضاً بخلاف الإجارة
[1] ولا في شراء منزل إلا بذكرِ ما ذُكِرَ) : أي الحقوق والمرافق إلى آخرها. فالحاصل أن العلو يدخل في بيع الدَّارِ وإن لم يذكر الحقوق والمرافق، ويدخل في بيع المنزل إن ذكر الحقوق والمرافق، ولا يدخل في بيع البيت وإن ذَكَرَ الحقوق والمرافق.
فالمنزل فيما بين البيت والدَّارِ لا يكون فيه مربط الدواب، بل يكون فيه بيتان أو ثلاثة أو نحو ذلك، يتعيش فيه الرَّجلُ المتأهل، فالعلو يكون من توابعه لا من توابع البيت ؛ لأن الشيء لا يستتبع مثله ، بل دونَه.
(ولا الطريق، والشرب، والمسيل في البيع إلا بذكرِ ما ذُكِرَ أيضاً بخلاف الإجارة)
والعلو مثل البيت لا دونَه فلا يدخلُ فيه إلا بالتنصيص عليه، وفي «الكافي»: إنَّ هذا التفصيل مبني على عرف الكوفة ، وفي عرفنا : يدخلُ العلو في الكل سواء باع باسم البيت أو المنزل أو الدار، والأحكام تبتني على العرف في كل إقليم وفي كل عرف أهله.
11) قوله : ولا في شراء...الخ ؛ لأنَّ المنزل اسم لما يشتمل على بيوت وصحن مسقف، ومطبخ لسكنة الرجل بعياله، والبيتُ اسم لمسقف واحدٍ كما عرفت، والدار اسم لما يشتمل على بيوت ومنازل وصحن غير مسقف.
فالمنزل يكون بين الدَّارِ والبيت إذ يتأتى فيه مرافق السكني بنوع قصور، بانتفاء منزل الدواب فيه ، فلشبهة بالدار يدخلُ العلو فيه تبعاً عند ذكر الحقوق، والشبه بالبيت لا يدخل فيه بدونه. [2] قوله : يدخل ...الخ ؛ لأنَّ الدار لما كان اسماً لما يدار عليه الحدود من الحائط ويشتمل على بيوت ومنازل وصحن غير مسقف، والعلو من توابع الأصل وأجزائه، فيدخل فيه من غير ذكر. (3) قوله : ولا الطريق... الخ ؛ أي لا يدخلُ الطريق في بيع ما له طريق، والشرب في بيع ما له شرب والمسيلُ في بيع ما له سيل، إلا بذكر الحقوق أو المرافق ؛ لأن كل واحدٍ
فإن الشرب والطَّريق والمسيل يدخلُ في الإجارة بلا ذكر الحقوق والمرافق ، فإن الإجارة تقع على المنفعة، ولا تقع المنفعة بدون هذه الأشياء، وأما البيع فيرد على الرقبة، وأيضاً يُمكن أن ينتفع المشتري بالتجارة، ولا كذلك في الإجارة. من هذه الأشياء الثلاثة تبع من وجه وأصل من وجه، أما التبعية فمن حيث أنه خارج عن حدودِهِ ويقصد الانتفاع به فيه.
وأما كونه أصلاً فمن حيث أنه يتصوَّرُ بدونِ المبيع فلا يدخلُ إلا بذكرِ الحقوق أو المرافق عملا بالشبهين. [ 1 ] قوله : فإنَّ الإجارة...الخ ؛ حاصلُهُ : إِنَّ الإجارة لم تشرع إلا للانتفاع، وهو لا يتحقق فيما له طريق أو شرب أو سيل إلا بطريقه أو بشريه أو بمسيله بخلاف البيع فإنّه شرع بملك الرقبة، والانتفاعُ من ثمراته ؛ ولهذا جاز بيعُ ما لا ينتفع به كالمهر ولم تجز إجارته.
[٢] قوله : يمكن ... الخ ؛ فإنَّ المشتري عادة يشتري الدَّارَ مثلاً، ويتَّخِرُ فيه، فيبيعُهُ من غيره ، فحصلت الفائدة ، بخلاف الإجارة فإنّها ليست بهذه المثابة.
Book of Sale / Chapter of Rights, Claims, and Sale of a Usurper

And not in the purchase of a dwelling except with mention of what was mentioned, nor the path, the right to water, and the watercourse in sale except with mention of what was mentioned also, contrary to lease.
[1] (And not in the purchase of a dwelling except with mention of what was mentioned): meaning the rights and facilities to their end. So the conclusion is that the upper floor enters in the sale of a home even if the rights and facilities are not mentioned, and enters in the sale of a dwelling if the rights and facilities are mentioned, and does not enter in the sale of a house even if the rights and facilities are mentioned.
The dwelling is between the house and the home; it does not have a stable for animals, but it has two or three rooms or the like, in which a married man lives. So the upper floor is among its appendages, not from the appendages of the house; because a thing does not follow its like, but rather that which is lower than it.
(And not the path, the right to water, and the watercourse in sale except with mention of what was mentioned also, contrary to lease)
And the upper floor is like the house, not lower than it, so it does not enter except with explicit stipulation. And in al-Kafi: This detail is based on the custom of Kufa, and in our custom: the upper floor enters in all whether he sells by the name of house, dwelling, or home, and rulings are based on custom in every region and among every people.
11) His saying: And not in purchase...etc.; because 'dwelling' is a name for what includes rooms, a roofed courtyard, and a kitchen for the residence of a man with his family, and 'house' is a name for a single roofed space as you have learned, and 'home' is a name for what includes rooms, dwellings, and an unroofed courtyard.
So the dwelling is between the home and the house insofar as it permits living facilities with a kind of deficiency, by the absence of a place for animals. So by resemblance to the home, the upper floor enters as a dependent when rights are mentioned, and by resemblance to the house, it does not enter without it. [2] His saying: enters...etc.; because 'home' is a name for what is enclosed by boundaries of walls and includes rooms, dwellings, and an unroofed courtyard, and the upper floor is from the appendages of the original and its parts, so it enters without mention. (3) His saying: And not the path...etc.; meaning the path does not enter in the sale of what has a path, nor the right to water in the sale of what has a right to water, nor the watercourse in the sale of what has a watercourse, except with mention of the rights or facilities; because each one of them is a dependent in one aspect and an original in another. As for being dependent: insofar as it is outside its boundaries and one intends to benefit by it therein.
And as for being original: insofar as it can be conceived without the sold item, so it does not enter except with mention of the rights or facilities, acting on both resemblances. [1] His saying: For lease...etc.; its summary is: lease is only established for benefit, and benefit is not realized in what has a path, right to water, or watercourse except by its path, its right to water, or its watercourse, contrary to sale, for sale is established for ownership of the property, and benefit is among its fruits; therefore it is permissible to sell what cannot be benefited from, like the dower, and it is not permissible to lease it.
[2] His saying: It is possible...etc.; for the buyer usually buys the home, for example, and resides in it, then sells it to someone else, so benefit is attained, contrary to lease, which is not in this position.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on al-Wiqayah, explaining the inclusion of appurtenances (rights and facilities) in the sale of real estate. The ruling distinguishes between three types of property: bayt (single room), manzil (dwelling with multiple rooms but no stable), and dar (home with courtyard). For a dar, the upper floor (al-'ulu) is included automatically whether or not rights are mentioned. For a manzil, it is included only if rights or facilities are mentioned. For a bayt, it is never included even with mention. This differentiation is based on the linguistic and customary meanings of these terms in Kufa, as reported in al-Kafi. However, the commentary notes that in later custom, the upper floor is included in all three regardless of the term. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that rulings can change according to custom ('urf), as stated: 'al-ahkam tabtani 'ala al-'urf' (rulings are built upon custom).

The second part deals with the path (tariq), right to water (shurb), and watercourse (masil). In a sale, these are not included unless rights or facilities are explicitly mentioned. The reasoning is that these items have a dual nature: they are dependent on the property in one sense (since one uses them to benefit from the property) but are conceptually separate (one can imagine the property without them). In a lease (ijara), however, they are automatically included because the purpose of a lease is benefit (manfa'ah), and the benefit of a property with a path, water right, or watercourse cannot be realized without access to these. In contrast, a sale transfers ownership ('raqabah), and the buyer can still benefit from the property indirectly, e.g., by selling it or using it for trade. Therefore, the default in sale is non-inclusion unless specified.

Key Terms
العلو (al-'ulu): The upper floor or storey above ground; an appendage of the property.
البيت (al-bayt): A single roofed room; smallest unit.
المنزل (al-manzil): Dwelling with multiple rooms and roofed courtyard, no stable.
الدار (al-dar): Home with courtyard, rooms, and dwellings; most comprehensive.
الحقوق (al-huquq): Rights accompanying property, e.g., easements.
المرافق (al-marafiq): Facilities and amenities benefiting the property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, under Kufan custom, in the sale of which property does the upper floor enter automatically without mentioning rights?
A) Bayt (house)
B) Manzil (dwelling)
C) Dar (home)
D) It enters in all three automatically

Key Takeaway
The inclusion of the upper floor in a sale depends on the property type and local custom; the Hanafi school gives custom a determinative role.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Inclusion of Path, Water Right, and Watercourse in Sale vs Lease
Commentary on inclusion of path, water rights, and watercourse in sale vs lease

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between sale and lease regarding the automatic inclusion of path (tariq), water right (shurb), and watercourse (masil), and explain the underlying legal reasoning (talil).

Core Idea
In a lease, necessary access rights are included automatically; in a sale, they require express mention.

The commentary now turns to a related issue: the inclusion of the path, right to water, and watercourse when selling or leasing a property. While the earlier section dealt with the upper floor, this passage addresses external access rights. The key distinction is between a contract for ownership (sale) and a contract for benefit (lease). In a lease, these facilities are indispensable for the benefit, so they are automatically included. In a sale, they are not automatically included because the buyer can still derive value from the property without them, e.g., by reselling it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three items and the general rule for sale
The text states: path, water right, and watercourse are not included in a sale unless 'rights and facilities' are mentioned. This is the default.
These items are considered external to the sold property but necessary for its full use.
2
Contrast with lease
In a lease (ijara), these three are automatically included without mention. The text says: 'bikhilaf al-ijarah' (contrary to lease).
3
Understand the talil (legal reasoning)
The passage explains that lease is solely for benefit (manfa'ah), and benefit of a property with these access rights cannot be realized without them. Sale, on the other hand, is for ownership of the property itself (raqaba), and the buyer can still benefit indirectly, e.g., by reselling. Therefore, the default differs.
The reasoning is based on the nature of the contract: lease is for the usufruct, sale is for the corpus.
4
Note the dual nature of these items
The text mentions that path, water right, and watercourse are 'dependent in one aspect and original in another'. They are dependent because they are outside the property but intended for its benefit; they are original because they can exist without the property. This duality explains why they require mention in sale.
The dual nature is a subtle point: they are not automatically part of the property like the upper floor in a dar.

كتاب البيع / باب الحقوق والاستحقاق وبيع الفضول

ولا في شراء منزل إلا بذكرِ ما ذُكِرَ ولا الطريق، والشرب، والمسيل في البيع إلا بذكر ما ذُكِرَ أيضاً بخلاف الإجارة
[1] ولا في شراء منزل إلا بذكرِ ما ذُكِرَ) : أي الحقوق والمرافق إلى آخرها. فالحاصل أن العلو يدخل في بيع الدَّارِ وإن لم يذكر الحقوق والمرافق، ويدخل في بيع المنزل إن ذكر الحقوق والمرافق، ولا يدخل في بيع البيت وإن ذَكَرَ الحقوق والمرافق.
فالمنزل فيما بين البيت والدَّارِ لا يكون فيه مربط الدواب، بل يكون فيه بيتان أو ثلاثة أو نحو ذلك، يتعيش فيه الرَّجلُ المتأهل، فالعلو يكون من توابعه لا من توابع البيت ؛ لأن الشيء لا يستتبع مثله ، بل دونَه.

الطريق (al-tariq): The path or road leading to the property.
الشرب (al-shurb): The right to take water, e.g., from a well or stream.
المسيل (al-masil): The watercourse or canal for water flow.
الإجارة (al-ijarah): Lease; contract for usufruct in exchange for rent.

According to the text, why are path, water right, and watercourse automatically included in a lease but not in a sale?
A) Because lease is for a fixed period.

C) Because the lessor has more rights.
D) Because the lease contract is not formal.

Key Takeaway
The automatic inclusion of access rights in lease, but not in sale, is rooted in the fundamental difference between a contract for usufruct and a contract for ownership.

The Child and the Mother: Evidence vs Acknowledgement in Claims of Ownership
Discusses whether a child is taken with its mother when ownership is proven by evidence or acknowledgement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on taking a child when the mother is claimed by evidence vs acknowledgement, and explain why the distinction exists based on the nature of proof.

Core Idea
Evidence establishes ownership from the origin, including the child; acknowledgement establishes ownership only from the present, excluding an already separate child.

How does the nature of a proof affect who is taken when a slave-girl is reclaimed? The Hanafi school draws a sharp distinction between the retroactive force of evidence (bayyinah) and the restricted force of acknowledgement (iqrar). This passage from the sharh of Al-Wiqayah unpacks the reasoning, showing that a child born to the mother is included when ownership is proven by evidence but not by acknowledgement. We will work through the talil (causal reasoning) and note an intra-Hanafi nuance about the need for explicit judgment on the child.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two scenarios: the mother is claimed by evidence, or by acknowledgement. State the ruling for each regarding the child.
The two scenarios are mutually exclusive; the outcome depends entirely on the type of proof.
2
Analyze the nature of bayyinah (evidence)
Evidence is described as a 'hujjah mutlaqah' (absolute proof) that establishes ownership from the time of the original transaction. This retroactive effect includes any offspring connected to the mother at that time.
The retroactivity is key; the buyer also recovers the price from the seller in this case.
3
Analyze the nature of iqrar (acknowledgement)
Acknowledgement is a 'hujjah qasirah' (restricted proof) that establishes ownership only at the time of the acknowledgement, out of necessity. Since the child is already separate at that time, it is not included.
The buyer does not recover the price from the seller in this case because ownership is not retroactive.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on explicit judgment
Some Hanafis hold that judgment on the mother suffices for the child (since he is subsidiary); others require explicit judgment on the child. Note the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).
This dispute does not affect the core ruling, but it matters when the child is later claimed separately.

أفصل في الاستحقاق
ويؤخذ الولد إن استحقت أُمه ببينة، وإن أقربها لا
افصل في الاستحقاق
( ويؤخذ الولد إن استحقت أمه ببينة، وإن أقربها لا)، صورتها : اشترى رجل جاريةً، فولدت عنده فاستحقها رجل ببينة، فإنَّه يأخذها وولدها، وإن أقر بها لا ؛ لأنَّ البينة حجة مطلقة فيظهر بها ملكه من الأصل
١ ] قوله : لأن ... الخ ؛ توضيحه : إن البينة حجّة مطلقة فيثبت بها الملك من الأصل، وبهذا يرجع المشتري على البائع بالثمن في الاستحقاق بالبينة، وقد كان الولد متصلاً بأمه يومئذ فيثبت بالبينة الاستحقاق فيها.
والإقرار حجة قاصرة يثبت به الملك في المخبر به ضرورة صحة الخبر، وقد اندفعت الضرورة بإثباتِ الاستحقاق بعد انفصال الولد ، فلا يظهر الاستحقاق في الولد ؛ ولهذا لا يرجعُ المشتري على البائع بالثمن في الاستحقاق بالقرار.
وذكر التَّمُرْتَاشِيُّ : أنَّ الولد إنّما لا يدخلُ في الإقرار إذا لم يدعه المقر له، أما إذا ادعاه فإنه يكون له، ثمّ في البينة يكون القضاء بالأم عند البعض ؛ لأن الولد تابع لها ، فيدخلُ في الحكم عليها. وقيل : لا بُدَّ من القضاء بالولد ، وإليه أشار محمد الله حيث قال : إذا قضى القاضي بالأصل المستحق ولم يعلم بالزَّوائدِ لم تدخل الزَّوائد تحت القضاء، وكذا إن كانت الزوائد في يد آخر، وهو غائب، لم تدخل تحت القضاء ؛ لانفصالها عن الأصل يوم القضاء، ذكره في «كمال الدراية).
[٢] قوله : حجة مطلقة ؛ حتى تظهر في حق الكافّة؛ لأنَّ البينة تصير حجَّةً بالقضاء، وللقاضي ولاية عامة فيتعدّى إلى الكل.
[٣]قوله : فيظهر بها ؛ فإنَّ الملك لا بدَّ له من زمان، وليس الزَّمانُ أولوية بالتعين، فيظهر الملك من الأصل، يعني من وقت الشراء لا في الحال ؛ لأنَّ الشهود لا يتمكنون من إثبات ما لم يكن ثابتاً، والولد كان متصلاً بالأم يوم الشراء فيأخذها.
Section on claim of ownership. The child is taken if his mother is proven to belong to the claimant by evidence; but if by acknowledgement, the child is not taken. Its scenario: A man bought a slave-girl, she gave birth with him, then a man claimed her with evidence; he takes her and her child. But if he claimed her by acknowledgement, he does not take the child. Because evidence is an absolute proof, so by it his ownership is shown from the origin. [1] His statement: 'because...' etc.; its explanation: Evidence is an absolute proof, so ownership from the origin is established by it, and by this the buyer recovers the price from the seller in the case of claim with evidence, and the child was connected to his mother at that time, so the claim in her is established by evidence. Acknowledgement is a restricted proof; by it ownership in the thing informed about is established out of necessity for the validity of the report, and the necessity is removed by establishing the claim after the separation of the child, so the claim in the child does not appear; for this reason the buyer does not recover the price from the seller in the case of claim by acknowledgement. Al-Tamurtashi mentioned that the child does not enter into the acknowledgement only if the person in whose favour the acknowledgement is made does not claim him; but if he claims him, then he is for him. Then, regarding evidence, judgment is given on the mother according to some, because the child is subsidiary to her, so he enters into the ruling on her. And it is said: Judgment must be given on the child, and Muhammad Allah alluded to this where he said: When the judge gives judgment on the original claimed and is not aware of the additions, the additions do not fall under the judgment; similarly if the additions are in the hand of another who is absent, they do not fall under the judgment due to their separation from the original at the time of judgment. This is mentioned in 'Kamal al-Diraya'. [2] His statement: 'absolute proof' means it appears in the right of everyone; because evidence becomes a proof by judgment, and the judge has general authority, so it extends to all. [3] His statement: 'by it is shown' means ownership must have a time, and time has no priority for specification, so ownership appears from the origin, i.e., from the time of purchase not at present; because witnesses are not able to prove what was not existent, and the child was connected to the mother at the day of purchase, so he takes her.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe on a ruling from the matn. The ruling states that if a slave-girl is later claimed by someone with evidence, the claimant takes both the mother and any child born to her during the buyer's ownership. However, if the claim is based on the seller's acknowledgement (i.e., the seller admits the girl belongs to the claimant), only the mother is taken, not the child. The reason is that evidence (bayyinah) is an absolute proof (hujjah mutlaqah) that establishes ownership retroactively from the time of the original purchase. Since the child was born and connected to the mother when the purchase occurred, the claimant's ownership extends to the child. In contrast, acknowledgement (iqrar) is a restricted proof (hujjah qasirah) that establishes ownership only out of necessity to validate the statement, and this necessity is fulfilled at the time of the acknowledgement. Since the child is already separate from the mother at that time, the claimant does not acquire ownership of the child. The commentary also notes a minor intra-Hanafi dispute: some hold that judgment on the mother suffices (since the child is a subsidiary), while others insist the judge must explicitly rule on the child. Al-Tamurtashi adds nuance: if the acknowledged person claims the child, it becomes his. The authority weight of this ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah as it is consistent with the established Hanafi position.

Key Terms
البينة (al-bayyinah): Evidence that is absolute and establishes ownership retroactively from the time of the original transaction.
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Acknowledgement that is a restricted proof, establishing ownership only from the time of the acknowledgement out of necessity.
الاستحقاق (al-istihqaq): Claim of ownership or right to property, proven by evidence or acknowledgement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person buys a slave-girl, and she gives birth. Later, a man claims the slave-girl with evidence. Which of the following is correct according to the Hanafi ruling?
A) The claimant takes only the mother, not the child.
B) The claimant takes both the mother and the child.
C) The claimant takes the child only if he explicitly claims him.
D) The claimant takes the child only if the seller acknowledges the claim.

Key Takeaway
When a mother is claimed by evidence, her child is automatically included; when claimed by acknowledgement, the child is not included because acknowledgement establishes ownership only from the present.
