Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Liability When a Free Person Claims to Be a Slave and Orders His Sale
Ruling on whether the person who claims to be a slave is liable for the price when the buyer discovers he is free.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a person who falsely claims to be a slave is liable for the sale price according to the Hanafi school, and explain the rationale (talil) and the dissenting view of Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Liability arises only when the buyer cannot recover from the seller, to prevent harm.

This passage from the Hidayah commentary ('Umda al-Ri'aya) examines a case where a free person orders another to buy him as a slave, claiming to be a slave. When the buyer discovers the truth, the question is whether the claimant must pay the price. The ruling depends on whether the buyer can recover from the actual seller, and there is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the basis of liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act: a free person says 'Buy me, I am a slave.' The buyer purchases him. The ruling: if the buyer cannot find the seller (location unknown), the claimant is liable for the price; if the seller is known, the buyer must recover from the seller and the claimant is not liable.
The ruling applies only when both the order and the claim of slavery are present.
2
Trace the dalil (rationale)
The rationale (talil) is that the claimant, by ordering and claiming slavery, has effectively guaranteed the safety of the transaction. When recourse to the seller is difficult, the claimant must compensate to prevent harm (دفعاً للضرر).
This is the majority view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf holds that the claimant is never liable. His reasoning: liability arises only from exchange or suretyship, neither of which exists here. The statement is merely a false report, akin to a third party saying 'Buy him, he is a slave' without ordering.
The majority view is the mufta bihi (relied-upon position).
4
Apply the ruling to scenarios
If the buyer knows the seller's location, he must sue the seller. If the seller is absent or unknown, the claimant must pay, then the claimant can recover from the seller if found. If the claimant only claimed slavery without ordering, or ordered without claiming, there is no liability by consensus.
This is from the commentary's summary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

والإقرار حجة قاصرة، فيثبت الملك به ضرورةً صحة الإخبار، فتندفع الضرورة
بثبوت الملك بعد انفصال الولد
(شخص) قال لآخر : اشترني فإني عبد" فاشترى ، فبان حُراً ، ضَمِنَ إن لم يدر مكان بائعه) ؛ لأنَّه بالأمر بالشَّراءِ يصيرُ ضامناً للثّمن عند تعدرِ الرجوع على البائع دفعاً للضرر، وعند أبي يوسف له لا ضمان عليه وإن علم لا ، (ورجع
عليه) : أي رَجَعَ هذا الشَّخصُ بما ضَمِنَ على البائع ، (وإن علم لا. [١] قوله : حجَّةٌ قاصرة ؛ لأنه لا يتوقف على قضاء القاضي، وله ولاية على نفسه دون غيره، فيقتصر عليه. [٢]قوله : شخص ...الخ ؛ حاصله : إنَّ شخصاً إذا قـال لـرجل يطلب شراء عبد : اشترني فإنّي عبد، فاشترى الرَّجلُ ذلك الشخص بناءً على كلامه، فظهر أنه حرّ، فإن كان البائع حاضراً أو غائباً ، ولكنَّ مكانه ،معلوم لا يضمن العبد الآمر لوجودِ من عليه

وعند أبي يوسف : لا يرجعُ المشتري على العبد بشيء ؛ لأنَّ ضمان الثمن بالمعاوضة أو بالكفالة، ولم توجد منهما ، بل الموجود ليس إلا إخباراً كاذباً ، فصار كما إذا قال الأجنبي : اشتره ، فإنّه عبد ، فاشتراه فظهر أنه حُرّ، فليس على الأجنبي شيء. [٣]قوله : اشترني فإني عبد ؛ إنما قيَّد بهذين القيدين ؛ لأنه قال وقت البيع : إنّي عبد، ولم يأمره بالشراء، أو قال : اشترني، ولم يقل : إنّي عبد لا يرجع في قولهم جميعاً، كذا في «النهاية».
A person said to another: 'Buy me, for I am a slave,' so he bought him, and he turned out to be free. He is liable if he does not know the whereabouts of his seller, and he has recourse against him; but if he knows, he is not liable. Acknowledgement is a restricted proof, so ownership is established by it out of necessity for the validity of the report, so the necessity is removed by the establishment of ownership after the separation of the child. (A person) said to another: 'Buy me, for I am a slave,' so he bought him, and he turned out to be free. He is liable if he does not know the whereabouts of his seller. Because by the order to buy, he becomes liable for the price when recourse to the seller is difficult, to prevent harm. According to Abu Yusuf, there is no liability on him even if he knows. (And he has recourse) against him: meaning this person has recourse against the seller for what he was liable for. (But if he knows, no liability). [1] His statement: 'restricted proof' because it does not depend on the judge's ruling, and he has authority over himself but not over others, so it is confined to him. [2] His statement: 'person...etc.': The summary: If a person says to a man seeking to buy a slave: 'Buy me, for I am a slave,' and the man buys him based on his statement, and it turns out he is free, then if the seller is present or absent but his location is known, the person who ordered the purchase is not liable because there is someone upon whom the right rests, i.e., the seller. If the seller is not present or his location is not known, then he is liable, meaning the buyer recovers the price from the slave according to both parties. Because the person who acknowledged slavery has guaranteed the safety of himself, and the buyer relied on his order and his acknowledgement that he is a slave. Because the statement regarding freedom is accepted from him, so he is made liable for the price when the buyer has recourse against the seller, to prevent fraud and harm. And the slave has recourse against the seller for the price when he appears, because he paid a debt on his behalf, and he is compelled in doing so, so he is not a volunteer. According to Abu Yusuf: The buyer does not recover anything from the slave because the guarantee of the price is either by exchange (mu'awadah) or by suretyship (kafalah), and neither exists here. What exists is merely a false report, so it becomes like when a third party says: 'Buy him, for he is a slave,' and he buys him and it turns out he is free; the third party is not liable. [3] His statement: 'Buy me, for I am a slave'; it is qualified by these two conditions because if he said at the time of sale 'I am a slave' and did not order him to buy, or said 'Buy me' and did not say 'I am a slave', there is no recourse according to all of them, as in al-Nihayah.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe on a ruling from the matn regarding a person who tells another to buy him as a slave, but he is actually free. The ruling has two scenarios: if the buyer knows the location of the seller, he can recover the price from the seller, so the person who claimed to be a slave is not liable. If the seller's location is unknown, the person is liable to the buyer for the price, and then he can recover from the seller when found. The reasoning is that by ordering the purchase and claiming to be a slave, he has effectively guaranteed the safety of the transaction; if the buyer cannot recover from the seller, the person must compensate to prevent loss. This is the majority view (including Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani). Abu Yusuf disagrees: he holds that the person is not liable at all because the guarantee is based on either an exchange or a suretyship, neither of which exists; it is merely a false statement. The commentary also specifies that for liability, the person must both order the purchase and claim to be a slave; if only one is present, there is no liability by consensus. The authority weight of the majority ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah, while Abu Yusuf's opinion is a minority view within the school.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Liability or guarantee; here it refers to the obligation to pay the price when recourse to the seller is not possible.
كفالة (kafalah): Suretyship; one of the bases for liability, where one person guarantees the obligation of another.
إخبار كاذب (ikhbar kadhib): False report; in this context, the statement 'I am a slave' when the person is free constitutes a false report.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view, in what scenario does the person who said 'Buy me, I am a slave' become liable for the price when he turns out to be free?
A) Whenever the buyer discovers he is free.
B) Only if the buyer does not know the location of the seller.
C) Only if the buyer knows the location of the seller.
D) The person is never liable.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a free person who orders his purchase as a slave is liable for the price when the buyer cannot recover from the seller, based on the principle of preventing harm.

Liability of a Free Person Who Commands His Purchase as a Slave: Ruling and Conditions
Ruling on guarantee when a free person claims to be a slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a free person who falsely claims to be a slave and commands his purchase, and explain the condition regarding the seller's location.

Core Idea
A free person who falsely claims slavery guarantees the price only if the buyer does not know the seller's location.

In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, we examine the foundational Hanafi ruling on a deceptive purchase command. The ruling hinges on a key condition that protects the buyer from loss while allowing recourse against the actual seller. Understanding this condition is essential for grasping the school's approach to preventing harm (darar) in transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and parties
A free person (the 'false slave') says to a buyer: 'Buy me, for I am a slave.' The buyer buys him, and it turns out he is free. Identify the roles: the false slave, the buyer, and the actual seller (who is absent or unknown).
2
State the ruling in the matn
The matn states: 'He guarantees if he does not know the location of his seller, and he has recourse against him; and if he knows, not.' This is the basic ruling.
The ruling distinguishes based on knowledge of the seller's location.
3
Identify the condition for liability
The false slave is liable (daman) only if the buyer does not know the location of the actual seller. If the seller's location is known, the buyer can pursue the seller directly, so the false slave is not liable.
This condition prevents double liability and focuses on the deceived buyer's ability to recover.
4
Explain the reasoning (talil)
The commentary explains that the false slave's command to buy combined with his acknowledgment of being a slave creates a guarantee (daman) of the price. The buyer relied on this statement. The guarantee is imposed to prevent harm (darar) and deception (ghurur). Additionally, the false slave can later recover from the seller when found, as he paid a debt under compulsion.
The key principle is daf' al-darar (prevention of harm).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شخص قال لآخر: اشترني فإني عبد فاشترى، فبان حُراً، ضَمِنَ إن لم يدر مكانَ بائعه، ورجع عليه، وإن علم لا. [١] قوله: حجَّةٌ قاصرة؛ لأنه لا يتوقف على قضاء القاضي، وله ولاية على نفسه دون غيره، فيقتصر عليه. [٢]قوله: شخص ...الخ؛ حاصله: إنَّ شخصاً إذا قال لرجل يطلب شراء عبد: اشترني فإنّي عبد، فاشترى الرَّجلُ ذلك الشخص بناءً على كلامه، فظهر أنه حرّ، فإن كان البائع حاضراً أو غائباً، ولكنَّ مكانه معلوم لا يضمن العبد الآمر لوجودِ من عليه الحق، وهو البائع. وإن لم يكن حاضراً أو لم يكن مكانه معلوماً ضَمِن، يعني رجع المشتري على العبد بالثمن عند الطرفين؛ لأنَّ المقرَّ بالعبوديَّةِ ضَمِنَ سلامة نفسه، والمشتري اعتمد على أمره وإقراره أنه عبد؛ لأنَّ القول قوله في الحرية، فيجعل ضامناً للثمن عند رجوعه على البائع؛ دفعاً للغرر والضرر، ورجع العبد على البائع بالثمن إذا حضر؛ لأنه قضى دينا عليه، وهو مضطر فيه، فلا يكون متبرعاً. وعند أبي يوسف: لا يرجعُ المشتري على العبد بشيء؛ لأنَّ ضمان الثمن بالمعاوضة أو بالكفالة، ولم توجد منهما، بل الموجود ليس إلا إخباراً كاذباً، فصار كما إذا قال الأجنبي: اشتره، فإنّه عبد، فاشتراه فظهر أنه حُرّ، فليس على الأجنبي شيء. [٣]قوله: اشترني فإني عبد؛ إنما قيَّد بهذين القيدين؛ لأنه قال وقت البيع: إنّي عبد، ولم يأمره بالشراء، أو قال: اشترني، ولم يقل: إنّي عبد لا يرجع في قولهم جميعاً، كذا في «النهاية». [٣]قوله: قال في «الهداية»؛ عبارة «الهداية» هكذا: ثم في وضع المسألة ضرب والتناقض إشكال على قول أبي حنيفة له؛ لأنَّ الدعوى شرط في حريَّة العبد عنده، فسد الدعوى. انتهى. وحاصله إن الدعوى شرط عنده بقبول الشهادة بالحرية؛ لكون العتق حق العبد، والتناقض فيه يمنعُ صحة الدعوى، فإنَّ العبد بعد ما قال: اشترني فإني عبد، إما أن يدعي الحريَّة أو لا. فالأول: تناقض، فكيف يثبت به الحرية. والثاني: ينفي شرط الحريّة. والجواب عن هذا الإشكال أنَّ وضع المسألة: ١. إن كان في حريَّة الأصل فالدعوى فيها ليس بشرط عند الإمام.
A person said to another: 'Buy me, for I am a slave,' and he bought him, and he turned out to be free. He guarantees if he does not know the location of his seller, and he has recourse against him; and if he knows, not. [1] He said: 'It is a restricted proof; because it does not require the judgment of a judge, and he has authority over himself but not others, so it is confined to him.' [2] He said: 'Person...etc.' Summary: If a person says to a man seeking to buy a slave: 'Buy me, for I am a slave,' and the man buys that person based on his statement, and it appears he is free, then if the seller is present or absent but his location is known, the slave-claimant is not liable because the one owed the right (the seller) exists. If he is not present or his location is unknown, he is liable, meaning the buyer recovers the price from the slave-claimant by both parties; because the one who acknowledged being a slave guaranteed the safety of himself, and the buyer relied on his command and acknowledgment that he is a slave; because the statement is taken as true regarding freedom, so he is made a guarantor of the price when he has recourse against the seller, to prevent deception and harm. And the slave-claimant recovers the price from the seller when he appears, because he paid a debt on his behalf, and he is compelled to do so, so he is not a volunteer. And according to Abu Yusuf: The buyer does not recover anything from the slave-claimant; because guarantee of the price is by exchange or suretyship, and neither occurred here; rather, there is only a false statement, so it is like when an outsider says: 'Buy him, for he is a slave,' and he buys him, and it appears he is free, then the outsider is not liable for anything. [3] He said: 'Buy me, for I am a slave' is qualified by these two conditions; because if he said at the time of sale: 'I am a slave' and did not command him to buy, or said: 'Buy me' and did not say: 'I am a slave,' there is no recourse according to all of them, as in 'al-Nihaya'. [3] He said: 'He said in al-Hidayah...' The wording of al-Hidayah is: 'Then in the situation of the case there is a difficulty and contradiction: an objection to Abu Hanifa's statement, because claim is a condition for the freedom of the slave according to him, and contradiction invalidates the claim.' End. Its summary: Claim is a condition according to him for accepting testimony of freedom, because emancipation is the slave's right, and contradiction in it prevents the validity of the claim. So the slave, after he said: 'Buy me, for I am a slave,' either claims freedom or not. The first: contradiction, so how is freedom proven by it? The second: negates the condition of freedom. The answer to this objection is that the situation of the case: 1. If it is about original freedom (freedom by origin), then claim is not a condition according to Imam.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage discusses the liability of a free person who falsely claims to be a slave and commands another to buy him. The matn states the ruling: if the buyer does not know the seller's location, the false slave must guarantee the price and can later seek recourse from the seller. If he knows the seller's location, no guarantee. The commentary explains that the false slave's acknowledgment of being a slave is a 'restricted proof' (hujjah qasirah) that only binds himself. The commentator then elaborates the condition: if the seller's location is known, the buyer can claim from the seller, so the false slave is not liable. The key principle is to prevent harm (darar) and deception (ghurur). An intra-Hanafi dispute is presented: Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing that guarantee arises only from a contract of exchange or suretyship, not from a mere false statement. He analogizes to a stranger who says 'buy him, he is a slave.' The passage also addresses a potential contradiction in Abu Hanifa's position regarding the requirement of a claim (da'wa) for proving freedom. The commentator resolves it by distinguishing between original freedom (hurriyyat al-asl) and acquired freedom.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Liability to pay the price; guarantee of financial compensation.
غرور (ghurur): Deception; inducing someone to enter a transaction based on a false representation.
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment against oneself; here, the person's statement 'I am a slave' is an iqrar.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When the seller is present and the buyer knows his location.
B) When the buyer does not know the location of the seller.
C) In all cases, because the statement is false.
D) Only if the buyer actually loses the price.

Key Takeaway
A free person who falsely claims to be a slave and commands his purchase guarantees the price only if the buyer does not know the seller's location, based on the principle of preventing harm.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Liability: Abu Hanifa vs. Abu Yusuf and the Da'wa Objection
Intra-Hanafi dispute and the da'wa objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the conflict between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding the basis of liability in the false slave case, and analyze the objection concerning the requirement of a claim (da'wa) for proving freedom.

Core Idea
Abu Yusuf rejects liability because he requires a contract of exchange or suretyship; the da'wa objection challenges the consistency of Abu Hanifa's position on proof of freedom.

In the previous lesson, we learned the ruling. Now we explore the legal reasoning behind it. Abu Yusuf, a leading Hanafi authority, presents a compelling objection based on the sources of liability (daman). Additionally, the passage addresses a subtle contradiction regarding the requirement of a formal claim (da'wa) to prove freedom. This lesson sharpens your critical analysis skills.

Guided Path
1
State Abu Hanifa's position and its basis
Abu Hanifa holds that the false slave is liable because he guaranteed the safety of himself by his command and acknowledgment, and the buyer relied on that. The liability is imposed to prevent harm (darar).
The basis is the principle of daf' al-darar, not formal contract.
2
State Abu Yusuf's objection
Abu Yusuf argues that liability (daman) arises only from a contract of exchange (mu'awada) or suretyship (kafala). Here, there is no contract between the buyer and the false slave; only a false statement. So no liability.
He analogizes to an outsider who says 'Buy him, he is a slave' – no liability.
3
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under Abu Hanifa, the buyer recovers from the false slave. Under Abu Yusuf, the buyer must pursue the seller, who may be untraceable, leaving the buyer at a loss.
The difference affects the buyer's ability to recover.
4
Examine the da'wa objection and its resolution
The text raises an objection: in Abu Hanifa's view, a claim (da'wa) is required for proving the freedom of a slave who asserts it. But the false slave here first claimed to be a slave, then later claims freedom – a contradiction. The answer: if the case involves 'original freedom' (hurriyyat al-asl), no claim is needed because the presumption is freedom.
This resolves the apparent inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شخص قال لآخر: اشترني فإني عبد فاشترى، فبان حُراً، ضَمِنَ إن لم يدر مكانَ بائعه، ورجع عليه، وإن علم لا. [١] قوله: حجَّةٌ قاصرة؛ لأنه لا يتوقف على قضاء القاضي، وله ولاية على نفسه دون غيره، فيقتصر عليه. [٢]قوله: شخص ...الخ؛ حاصله: إنَّ شخصاً إذا قال لرجل يطلب شراء عبد: اشترني فإنّي عبد، فاشترى الرَّجلُ ذلك الشخص بناءً على كلامه، فظهر أنه حرّ، فإن كان البائع حاضراً أو غائباً، ولكنَّ مكانه معلوم لا يضمن العبد الآمر لوجودِ من عليه الحق، وهو البائع. وإن لم يكن حاضراً أو لم يكن مكانه معلوماً ضَمِن، يعني رجع المشتري على العبد بالثمن عند الطرفين؛ لأنَّ المقرَّ بالعبوديَّةِ ضَمِنَ سلامة نفسه، والمشتري اعتمد على أمره وإقراره أنه عبد؛ لأنَّ القول قوله في الحرية، فيجعل ضامناً للثمن عند رجوعه على البائع؛ دفعاً للغرر والضرر، ورجع العبد على البائع بالثمن إذا حضر؛ لأنه قضى دينا عليه، وهو مضطر فيه، فلا يكون متبرعاً. وعند أبي يوسف: لا يرجعُ المشتري على العبد بشيء؛ لأنَّ ضمان الثمن بالمعاوضة أو بالكفالة، ولم توجد منهما، بل الموجود ليس إلا إخباراً كاذباً، فصار كما إذا قال الأجنبي: اشتره، فإنّه عبد، فاشتراه فظهر أنه حُرّ، فليس على الأجنبي شيء. [٣]قوله: اشترني فإني عبد؛ إنما قيَّد بهذين القيدين؛ لأنه قال وقت البيع: إنّي عبد، ولم يأمره بالشراء، أو قال: اشترني، ولم يقل: إنّي عبد لا يرجع في قولهم جميعاً، كذا في «النهاية». [٣]قوله: قال في «الهداية»؛ عبارة «الهداية» هكذا: ثم في وضع المسألة ضرب والتناقض إشكال على قول أبي حنيفة له؛ لأنَّ الدعوى شرط في حريَّة العبد عنده، فسد الدعوى. انتهى. وحاصله إن الدعوى شرط عنده بقبول الشهادة بالحرية؛ لكون العتق حق العبد، والتناقض فيه يمنعُ صحة الدعوى، فإنَّ العبد بعد ما قال: اشترني فإني عبد، إما أن يدعي الحريَّة أو لا. فالأول: تناقض، فكيف يثبت به الحرية. والثاني: ينفي شرط الحريّة. والجواب عن هذا الإشكال أنَّ وضع المسألة: ١. إن كان في حريَّة الأصل فالدعوى فيها ليس بشرط عند الإمام.
A person said to another: 'Buy me, for I am a slave,' and he bought him, and he turned out to be free. He guarantees if he does not know the location of his seller, and he has recourse against him; and if he knows, not. [1] He said: 'It is a restricted proof; because it does not require the judgment of a judge, and he has authority over himself but not others, so it is confined to him.' [2] He said: 'Person...etc.' Summary: If a person says to a man seeking to buy a slave: 'Buy me, for I am a slave,' and the man buys that person based on his statement, and it appears he is free, then if the seller is present or absent but his location is known, the slave-claimant is not liable because the one owed the right (the seller) exists. If he is not present or his location is unknown, he is liable, meaning the buyer recovers the price from the slave-claimant by both parties; because the one who acknowledged being a slave guaranteed the safety of himself, and the buyer relied on his command and acknowledgment that he is a slave; because the statement is taken as true regarding freedom, so he is made a guarantor of the price when he has recourse against the seller, to prevent deception and harm. And the slave-claimant recovers the price from the seller when he appears, because he paid a debt on his behalf, and he is compelled to do so, so he is not a volunteer. And according to Abu Yusuf: The buyer does not recover anything from the slave-claimant; because guarantee of the price is by exchange or suretyship, and neither occurred here; rather, there is only a false statement, so it is like when an outsider says: 'Buy him, for he is a slave,' and he buys him, and it appears he is free, then the outsider is not liable for anything. [3] He said: 'Buy me, for I am a slave' is qualified by these two conditions; because if he said at the time of sale: 'I am a slave' and did not command him to buy, or said: 'Buy me' and did not say: 'I am a slave,' there is no recourse according to all of them, as in 'al-Nihaya'. [3] He said: 'He said in al-Hidayah...' The wording of al-Hidayah is: 'Then in the situation of the case there is a difficulty and contradiction: an objection to Abu Hanifa's statement, because claim is a condition for the freedom of the slave according to him, and contradiction invalidates the claim.' So the slave, after he said: 'Buy me, for I am a slave,' either claims freedom or not. The first: contradiction, so how is freedom proven by it? The second: negates the condition of freedom. The answer to this objection is that the situation of the case: 1. If it is about original freedom (freedom by origin), then claim is not a condition according to Imam.

No Guarantee in Mortgage: Rahn as Security, Not Exchange
Ruling that no guarantee arises from a mortgage and no recourse in a claim after settlement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why no liability (daman) arises from a false statement in a mortgage contract and apply the analogous ruling to other non-exchange transactions.

Core Idea
In mortgage, a false statement creates no liability because mortgage is a security, not an exchange.

This passage addresses a key distinction in Hanafi fiqh: liability for deception (ghurur) depends on the nature of the contract. In a sale (exchange), a false claim triggers guarantee, but in a mortgage (security), it does not. The commentary illustrates this with an analogy to road safety: advising someone that a road is safe does not make the advisor liable if the traveler is robbed, because the advice is not part of a contract of exchange. Likewise, falsely claiming to be a slave to induce a mortgage does not create liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Read the first line: 'ولا ضمان في الرهن أصلاً' (no guarantee in mortgage at all) and the second: 'ولا رجوع في دعوى حق مجهول' (no recourse in claim of unknown right). These are two separate rulings; focus on the first as it is expanded by the commentary.
2
Understand the rationale: mortgage is not mu'awadah
The commentary states: 'ليس عقد معاوضة، بل هو وثيقة' (it is not a contract of exchange, rather it is a security document). Because mortgage is merely a security (wathiqah), it does not involve the exchange of consideration, so deception (ghurur) occurring in the command to mortgage does not generate liability.
Contrast with sale where a false claim of ownership leads to liability (daman al-musawimah).
3
Examine the qiyas (analogy) to road safety
The commentary analogizes to a person asking about road safety: if the informant says 'it is safe' and the traveler is robbed, the informant is not liable because the deception is outside a contract of exchange. Apply the same 'illa' (deception not in mu'awadah) to mortgage: the false claim 'I am a slave' is not part of an exchange, so no guarantee.
The qiyas is valid because both cases share the operative cause (illa): deception without an exchange contract.
4
Apply the principle to other non-exchange scenarios
Consider any situation where a false statement induces a non-exchange transaction (e.g., forgivable loan, guarantee, deposit). The ruling is that no liability arises from the deception itself, though the underlying contract may be vitiated (fasid) if it involves fraud.
However, if the deception leads to an exchange contract indirectly, liability may arise through other means (e.g., khiyar al-ghubn).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع / باب الحقوق والاستحقاق وبيع الفضول  ولا ضمانَ في الرَّهن أصلاً، ولا رجوع في دعوى حق مجهول في دارٍ صولح على شيء واستحق بعضها [١] قوله: ليس عقد معاوضة، بل هو وثيقة لاستيفاء عين حقه، فإنَّ الرهن هو جعل الشيء محبوساً بحق يمكن استيفاؤه وأخذه من ماليَّة المرهون لا من عينه. [٢]قوله: فلا يكون...الخ؛ فإنَّ الأمر بالارتهان لم يكن غروراً في عقدِ معاوضة، فلا ينتظر سبباً للضمان؛ ولهذا إذا سأل رجلٌ غيره عن أمن الطريق، فقال له: اسلك هذا الطريق فإنّه آمن، فسلكه، فسلب اللصوص أمواله، لم يضمن المخبر بشيء؛ لأنه غرور فيما ليس بمعاوضة، كذا في «الكفاية».
Book of Sale / Chapter on Rights, Claims, and Sale of Usurped Property. And there is no guarantee in mortgage at all, and no recourse in a claim of an unknown right in a house concerning which a settlement was made for something and some of it is claimed. [1] He said: 'It is not a contract of exchange; rather it is a document for recovering his exact right, for mortgage is making a thing detained for a right that can be recovered and taken from the monetary value of the mortgaged property, not from its substance.' [2] He said: 'So it is not...etc.' For the command to mortgage was not a deception in a contract of exchange, so no cause for guarantee is expected. Hence, if a man asks another about the safety of a road, and he says: 'Take this road, for it is safe,' and he takes it, and robbers steal his property, the informer is not liable for anything, because it is deception in what is not a contract of exchange, as in 'al-Kifaya'.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage presents two distinct rulings. First, if someone says 'Mortgage me, for I am a slave' and the person mortgages him, but he turns out to be free, there is no guarantee on the person who claimed to be a slave. The reason is that mortgage is not a contract of exchange (mu'awadah); it is merely a security document (wathiqah) to secure a debt. Therefore, the command to mortgage does not create a guarantee for safety, unlike the purchase case in Lesson 1. The commentary analogizes this to a case where a person asks about road safety and is told it is safe, then gets robbed; the informant is not liable because the deception occurred outside a contract of exchange. Second, there is no recourse in a claim of an unknown right in a house after a settlement has been made for a specific thing and part of that thing is claimed by another. The commentary does not elaborate on this second part in the given excerpt.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): Mortgage; a contract where property is held as security for a debt.
ضمان (daman): Liability to compensate; here, guarantee for the mortgage debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because the person did not command the mortgage.
C) Because mortgage is not a contract of exchange; it is a security document.
D) Because the buyer knew the person was free.

Key Takeaway
In mortgage, a false statement creates no liability because the contract is a security (wathiqah), not an exchange (mu'awadah).

Return of Consideration Upon Reclamation of Claimed Property
Ruling on settlement when the entire claimed property is reclaimed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on returning consideration when the entire claimed property is reclaimed and explain the rationale based on the claim being included in the reclaimed property.

Core Idea
Consideration is returned only if the entire claimed property is reclaimed; partial reclamation leaves settlement intact.

This passage addresses a specific scenario in ṣulḥ (settlement) where a third party reclaims property that was the subject of a dispute. The ruling distinguishes between partial and total reclamation, clarifying when the defendant can recover the consideration paid. It also implicitly confirms the validity of settling an unknown claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two cases: (1) part of the claimed property is reclaimed, and (2) the entire property is reclaimed.
Distinction based on extent of reclamation.
2
State the ruling for each scenario
For partial reclamation: the defendant cannot recover any consideration. For full reclamation: the entire consideration must be returned.
No return vs full return.
3
Explain the rationale
The claimant's original claim was that a right existed in the property. If the whole property is reclaimed, the claimed right is completely subsumed, so the settlement fails. If only part is reclaimed, the claimant may have claimed the remainder, so the settlement stands.
Rationale: 'because the claimed (right) is included in the reclaimed'.
4
Derive the broader principle
This ruling shows that settlement for an unknown right is valid. The invalidity of the claim itself does not invalidate the settlement contract.
Hanafi prioritizes dispute avoidance (qaṭʿ al-nizāʿ).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو استحق كلها ردَّ كلَّ العوض ; لأنَّ المدَّعي به داخل في المستحق ، وفهم صحةِ الصلح عن المجهول أي إذا ادعى حقاً مجهولاً في دارٍ فَصُولِح على شيء، ثم استحق بعض الدَّارِ، فالمدعى عليه لا يرجعُ على المدَّعِي بشيءٍ ; لأن للمدعي أن يقول: دعواي في غيرِ ما استحق، (ولو استحق كلها ردَّ كل العوض ; لأنَّ المدعي به داخل في المستحق) ( وفهم صحة الصلح عن المجهول)
And if the entire (property) is reclaimed, all the consideration is returned, because the claimed (right) is included in the reclaimed (property). And it is understood the validity of settlement from the unknown. That is, if one claims an unknown right in a house and settles for something, then part of the house is reclaimed, the defendant does not get anything from the claimant, because the claimant can say: my claim was in what was not reclaimed. And if the entire house is reclaimed, the entire consideration is returned, because the claimed (right) is included in the reclaimed. And it is understood the validity of settlement from the unknown.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage states a ruling regarding settlement (ṣulḥ) when the property subject to a claim is later reclaimed by a third party. The ruling distinguishes between partial and total reclamation. If only part is reclaimed, the defendant cannot recover any consideration because the claimant's original claim may have been limited to the remainder. If the entire property is reclaimed, the consideration is fully returned because the claimant's right was encompassed by the reclamation. The passage also deduces that settlement for an unknown right is valid, as this ruling shows that the invalidity of the claim does not invalidate the settlement itself. This is a Hanafi position that prioritizes the avoidance of dispute (qaṭʿ al-nizāʿ).

Key Terms
استحقاق (istihqāq): Reclamation of property by a third party establishing ownership.
صلح (ṣulḥ): A settlement or compromise agreement to end a dispute.
عوض (ʿiwaḍ): The consideration or compensation given in a settlement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if the entire claimed property is reclaimed, what happens to the consideration paid in the settlement?
A) The defendant keeps the consideration.
B) The consideration is fully returned to the defendant.
C) Half of the consideration is returned.
D) The consideration is forfeited to the state.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a settlement for an unknown right is valid, but the consideration is fully refunded if the entire claimed property is reclaimed by a third party.

Returning a Proportional Consideration When Part of the Claimed Property Is Reclaimed
Ruling on proportional return in settlement upon partial reclamation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule for returning a proportional consideration in a settlement when part of the claimed property is reclaimed, and explain the underlying principle regarding unknown claims.

Core Idea
Settlement for an unknown claim is valid; upon partial reclamation, the consideration is reduced proportionally.

This passage from a Hanafi fiqh text addresses a specific scenario in sales and claims. When a claimant settles for the entire property and later part of it is reclaimed by a third party, the defendant is entitled to a proportional refund. This ruling also affirms the validity of settlement for an unknown claim, a key Hanafi principle.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the scenario
Identify that the claimant claimed the entire property (e.g., a house) and settled for a fixed consideration.
2
Understand the effect of partial reclamation
When part of the property is reclaimed, it becomes clear that the claimant did not own that portion; thus the settlement contract was partially without cause.
The settlement was based on full ownership.
3
Apply proportional return
The defendant returns the same proportion of the consideration as the reclaimed portion of the property.
E.g., if half is reclaimed, half the consideration is returned.
4
Note the principle about jahala
The ruling confirms that settlement (ṣulḥ) for an unknown claim is valid because ignorance in what is dropped does not lead to dispute.
This is a Hanafi position, contrary to some fatwas that require a valid claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع باب الحقوق والاستحقاق وبيع الفضول ورجع بحصته في دعوى كلّها إن استحق شيءٌ منها وإِنَّما أي دلت هذه المسألة على أن الصلح عن المجهول على مال معلوم صحيح يصح; لأنَّ الجهالة فيما يسقط لا تفضي إلى المنازعة، وقد يُنْقَلُ عن بعض الفتاوى أن الصلح لا يصلح إلا أن يكونَ الدَّعوى صحيحة ، فهذه المسألة تدل على أن هذه الرواية غير صحيحة ; لأنَّ دعوى الحقِّ المجهول دعوى غير صحيحة، وكثير من مسائل «الذخيرة» تدل على عدم صحة تلك الرواية. (ورجع بحصته في دعوى كلها إن استحق شيءٌ منها ) : أي إن ادعى كل الدار فَصُولِح على شيءٍ ، ثُمَّ استحق نصفها يرجع بنصف البدل. لأنَّ الدعوى المجهولة في الدار ليست بصحيحة، حتى لو أقامَ البيِّنةَ لا تقبلُ إلا إذا ادعى إقرار المدعى عليه بالحق، فحينئذ يصح الدعوى، وتقبل البينة كما صرحوا به. [1] قوله : يرجع ... الخ ; لأنَّ الصلح على مئةٍ مثلاً وقـع عـلـى كـل الـدار ، فإذا استحق منها شيء تبيَّنَ أنَّ المدَّعي لا يملك ذلك القدر، فيرد حسابه من العوض.
The book of sales, chapter on rights and claims and sale of superfluous things. And he returns a portion of it in the claim of the whole, if part of it is reclaimed. That is, it indicates that settlement for an unknown (claim) for a known property is valid, because ignorance in what is dropped does not lead to dispute. And it is reported from some fatwas that settlement is not valid unless the claim is valid; this case shows that such a report is not correct, because the claim of an unknown right is not a valid claim. And many cases in al-Dhakhira show the incorrectness of that report. (And he returns a portion of it in the claim of the whole, if part of it is reclaimed) i.e., if he claimed the entire house and settled for something, then half is reclaimed, he returns half of the consideration. Because the unknown claim in the house is not valid, so if he establishes evidence, it is not accepted unless he claims that the defendant acknowledged the right; then the claim becomes valid and the evidence is accepted as they have stated. [Commentary:] His saying: he returns... because the settlement on one hundred, for example, was on the entire house, so when part of it is reclaimed, it becomes clear that the claimant does not own that portion, so he returns its share of the consideration.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling regarding a settlement (ṣulḥ) where the claimant claimed the entire property (e.g., a house) and settled for a fixed amount. If later part of the house is reclaimed by a third party, the defendant is entitled to return a proportional amount of the consideration. For example, if the settlement was for 100 and half the house is reclaimed, the defendant returns 50. The reasoning is that the settlement was based on the entire house, and the reclaimed portion was not owned by the claimant, so the consideration must be adjusted. The passage also reiterates that settlement for an unknown claim is valid, contrary to some fatwas that require a valid claim. The underlying principle is that the claim in a settlement need not be precise; it is sufficient to establish the right through the defendant's acknowledgment. This ruling is consistent with the Hanafi position that the validity of ṣulḥ does not depend on the specificity of the claim.

Key Terms
بدل (badal): Consideration or compensation paid in a settlement.
دعوى كلها (daʿwā kullihā): A claim that encompasses the entire property, as opposed to a claim of a share or partial right.
جهالة (jahāla): Ignorance or uncertainty regarding the right claimed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person settles for the entire house for 100 dirhams, and half the house is later reclaimed, how much does the defendant return?
A) 100 dirhams
B) 50 dirhams
C) 25 dirhams
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
The consideration is reduced proportionally when part of the claimed property is reclaimed, because the settlement was based on full ownership.

Sulh for an Unknown Claim: Validity and Proportional Return
Commentary on a ruling about sulh and return of consideration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that sulh for an unknown claim with known consideration is valid, and apply the rule of returning a proportionate part of the consideration when part of the claimed property is reclaimed.

Core Idea
Sulh for an unknown claim is valid; partial reclamation requires proportional return of consideration.

This lesson examines a key ruling from the Book of Sale regarding sulh (compromise) when the claim is unspecified. The commentator explains that such sulh is valid because ignorance in what is waived does not lead to dispute, and he refutes a contrary narration. We then explore the practical consequence: if part of the property is later reclaimed, the claimant must return a proportionate share of the settlement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Conditions
Read the main ruling: 'And he returns his portion in a claim of the whole if something of it is reclaimed.' Identify that this applies when someone claims an entire property, reaches a sulh, and then part of the property is proven to belong to another. The key condition is that the claim was for the whole property and the settlement was for a known consideration.
The claim can be unknown; the consideration must be known.
2
Understand the Rationale
The commentator states that jahalah (ignorance) in what is waived (the claim) does not lead to future dispute, so the sulh is valid. This refutes a view from some fatawa that require the claim to be sound. Recognize that the Hanafi position prioritizes certainty in the consideration over specificity in the claim.
The reasoning is based on avoiding niza' (dispute) after the sulh.
3
Apply the Proportional Return Rule
If half the property is reclaimed, the claimant returns half of the consideration. Work through the example: claim for an entire house, sulh for 100 dirhams, then half reclaimed → return 50 dirhams. Generalize: return (reclaimed proportion) × (total consideration).
The sulh is treated as partially rescinded for the portion the claimant does not own.
4
Note the Refutation of a Contrary View
The commentator dismisses a narration that sulh is only valid if the original claim is sound. He argues that an unknown claim itself is not sound, yet the sulh is valid; thus the narration is incorrect. This highlights the importance of distinguishing between the validity of the claim and the validity of the sulh in Hanafi law.
Reference is made to al-Dhakhirah for supporting evidence.

Source Text

The Book of Sale, Chapter on Rights and Entitlement and Sale by a Non-Owner
And he returns his portion in a claim of the whole [property] if something of it is reclaimed.
Indeed, this issue indicates that settlement (sulh) for an unknown [right] in exchange for a known property is valid and sound, because ignorance (jahalah) concerning what is waived does not lead to dispute. It has been transmitted from some fatawa that sulh is not valid unless the claim is sound. So this issue indicates that this narration is not correct, because a claim for an unknown right is an unsound claim. And many issues in al-Dhakhirah indicate the unsoundness of that narration.
(And he returns his portion in a claim of the whole [property] if something of it is reclaimed:) That is, if he claimed the entire house and settled for something, then half of it is reclaimed, he returns half of the consideration. Because an unknown claim regarding the house is not sound, such that even if he produces evidence, it is not accepted unless he claims that the defendant acknowledged the right. Then the claim becomes sound, and the evidence is accepted, as they have clearly stated. [1] His saying: 'he returns...' etc.; because the sulh for, say, one hundred was concluded for the entire house. When something of it is reclaimed, it becomes clear that the claimant does not own that portion, so he returns its share of the consideration.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This lesson is a commentary on a ruling from the matn concerning a claim for an entire property followed by a settlement. The passage deals with two main issues: (1) the validity of sulh (compromise) when the claim is unspecified, and (2) the obligation to return a proportion of the settlement if part of the claimed property is later reclaimed.

The commentator first explains that this ruling demonstrates that sulh for an unknown claim against a known payment is valid. The reason is that ignorance pertains to what is being waived (the claim), and such ignorance does not lead to future disputes because the claimant gives up an uncertain right. This refutes a narration in some fatawa that requires the original claim to be valid for sulh to be valid. However, a claim for an unknown right is not a valid claim in itself, so that narration is incorrect. The commentator supports this with references to al-Dhakhirah.

The second part explains the ruling itself: if someone claims an entire house and reaches a sulh for a sum, then later half of the house is reclaimed (i.e., proven to belong to another), the claimant must return half of the settlement amount. This is because the sulh was for the entire house, and after the reclamation it becomes clear the claimant did not own that portion. Hence he must return the corresponding part of the consideration. The commentator also notes that an unknown claim about the house is not legally valid; even if the claimant brings evidence, it is not accepted unless he proves that the defendant had acknowledged the right. In that case, the claim becomes valid and evidence is accepted.

Key Terms
الصلح عن المجهول (al-sulh 'an al-majhul): A compromise where the right being waived is unspecified (unknown from the claimant's side), but the consideration is known.
دعوى كل الدار (da'wa kull al-dar): A claim asserting ownership of the entire house (the whole property).
الرجوع بحصته (al-ruju' bi-hissatihi): The obligation for the claimant to return a proportionate part of the consideration he received in sulh, corresponding to the portion of property that is later reclaimed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person claims an entire house, settles for 100 dirhams, then half the house is reclaimed by a third party, what does the claimant owe?
A) He returns nothing because the sulh is final.
B) He returns 50 dirhams (half the consideration).
C) He returns the entire 100 dirhams.
D) He may choose to return half or keep the house.

Key Takeaway
Sulh for an unknown claim with known consideration is valid; if part of the claimed property is reclaimed, the consideration must be returned proportionally.

Definition and Ruling of Sale by a Non-Owner (Bay' al-Fuduli)
Definition of fuduli and owner's option to ratify or rescind

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the legal definition of a fuduli, identify the owner's option, and explain the disputed expressions for ratification.

Core Idea
A sale by a non-owner is suspended until the owner ratifies or rescinds it — not automatically void.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi position on the sale of another's property without permission. This transaction, called bay' al-fuduli, is neither valid nor void from the start; it is suspended (mawquf) pending the owner's decision. Understanding this hinges on knowing who qualifies as a fuduli, what the owner may do, and which words or actions count as ratification.

Guided Path
1
Define al-fuduli linguistically and technically
Learn the root meaning (fudul = surplus) and how it became a term for meddling. In fiqh, a fuduli is one who is neither owner (asil) nor agent (wakil).
The text corrects the pronunciation: dammah on the fā’.
2
Identify the core ruling
The owner has the option to ratify (ijazah) or rescind (faskh). The sale is mawquf (suspended), not void ab initio.
Conditions for ratification: object still exists, parties unchanged, price (if in kind) still exists.
3
Classify forms of ratification
Ratification can be explicit (e.g., 'I ratify') or implied (e.g., receiving the price, demanding it). Not all positive statements count.
Praise like 'ahsanta' (you did well) is not ratification in the relied-upon view.
4
Analyse the intra-Hanafi dispute on praise expressions
Imam Muhammad considers 'ahsanta' and 'asabta' as ratification by istihsan; Abu Hanifa (Zahir al-Riwayah) disagrees due to possible mockery.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view: praise is not ratification.
5
Apply the ruling to expressions of blame
If the owner says 'bi'sa ma sana'ta' (you did badly), the Zahir al-Riwayah holds it as rejection (not ratification), and the fatwa is on that.
A divergent view in al-Muntaqa considers it ratification, but it is not relied upon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيع الفضول
ولمالك باغ غيره ملكه فسخه، وله إجازته
فصل في بيع الفضول
(ولمالك" باغ غيره ملكه فسخه ، وله إجازته
[ ١ ] قوله : ولمالك ؛ لَمَّا فرغ المصنف له عن مسائل الاستحقاق، وكان بيعُ الفضولي من صور الاستحقاق، فإنَّ المستحق إنّما يقول عند الدعوى : هذا ملكي ، ومن باعك إنما باعك بغير إذني ، فهو عين بيع الفضولي، فشرع في مسائله، وقال: ولمالك... الخ، ولو أتي بلفظ فصل هاهنا لكان أحسن كما لا يخفى.
والفضولي : بضم الفاء وفتحها خطأ ، نسبةً إلى الفُضُول، جمع الفضل، وهو الزيادة، قال في «المغرب» (۱) : الفضلُ الزّيادة، وقد غلب جمعه على ما لا خير فيه،
حتى قيل :
فُضُول بلا فضل وسِنِّ بلاسِنٌ وَطُولٌ بلا طَوْل وَعَرْضُ بِلا عِرْضِ ثم قيل لمن يشتغل بما لا يعنيه : فُضُولي ، إنّه صار بالغلبة لهذا المعنى كالعلم له ، ولم يصر إلى الواحدِ في النسبة ، كما في أعرابي وأنصاري، وفي اصطلاح الفقهاء : من ليس بأصيل ولا وكيل. وصورة هذه المسألة أنه إذا باع رجلٌ ملك غيره، فالمالك بالخيار إن شاءَ أَجازَ ذلك البيع إذا كان المعقود عليه باقياً، والمتعاقدان بحالهما، وكذا الثَّمن إن كان من العروض، وإن شاء فسخه. (٢) قوله : وله إجازته ؛ بأن يقول : أجزتُ البيع، أو بأن يقبض الثمن، أو يطلبه، فإن قال : أحسنت، أو أصبت، أو كفيتني مؤنة البيع، لا يكون إجازة ؛ لاحتمال الاستهزاء ، وقال محمد الله : إن قوله : أحسنت وأصبت إجازة استحساناً، ذكره القاضي خان له في «فتاواه».
وفي «المنتقى»: إنَّ المالك إذا قال : بئسما صنعت يكون إجازة، وفي ظاهر الرواية يكون رداً، وعليه الفتوى. كذا في الفصول العمادية ذكره العلامة البرْجَنْدِي له في
«شرح النقاية».
Section on Sale by a Non-Owner (Fuduli)
And for the owner of [property] whose property has been sold by someone else, he has the right to rescind it, and he has the right to ratify it.
Section on Sale by a Non-Owner
(And for the owner of [property] whose property has been sold by someone else, he has the right to rescind it, and he has the right to ratify it.)
[1] His saying: 'And for the owner' – When the author finished with the issues of reclamation (istihqaq), and since the sale by a non-owner (fuduli) is a form of reclamation (because the claimant says at the time of claim: 'This is my property, and the one who sold it to you sold it without my permission'), so he began its issues and said: 'And for the owner...' etc. And if he had used the word 'section' here it would have been better, as is not hidden.
And al-fuduli: with dammah on the fa and opening it is an error; it is a nisbah to al-fudul, the plural of al-fadl, which means increase. In al-Mughrib it is said: al-fadl is increase, and its plural has come to dominate over things in which there is no good, to the extent that it is said: 'Fudul without fadl, and sinn without sin, and tul without tawl, and 'ard without 'ird.' Then it was said to one who occupies himself with what does not concern him: fuduli. Indeed, it has become by prevalence for this meaning like a proper noun for him. And it does not take the form of the singular in the nisbah, like 'arabi and ansari. In the terminology of the jurists: it is one who is neither an original party (asil) nor a deputy (wakil). The scenario of this issue is that if a man sells another's property, the owner has a choice: if he wishes, he may ratify that sale, provided the object of sale still exists, and the two contracting parties are still as they were, and likewise the price if it is a commodity; and if he wishes, he may rescind it.
(2) His saying: 'and he has the right to ratify it' – by saying: 'I ratify the sale', or by receiving the price, or by demanding it. If he says: 'You have done well' (ahsanta) or 'You have hit the mark' (asabta) or 'You have spared me the trouble of the sale' (kafaytani mu'nat al-bay'), that does not constitute ratification because of the possibility of mockery. And Muhammad [al-Shaybani] said: his saying 'ahsanta' and 'asabta' is ratification by way of istihsan, as mentioned by al-Qadikhan in his Fatawa.
In al-Muntaqa: if the owner says 'You have done badly' (bi'sa ma sana'ta) that is ratification, but in the Zahir al-Riwayah it is rejection, and upon that is the fatwa. So it is in al-Fusul al-'Imadiyyah, mentioned by al-Birjandi in his Sharh al-Niqayah.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This lesson introduces the chapter on Bay' al-Fuduli (sale by a non-owner). The commentator begins by explaining the connection to the previous chapter on istihqaq (reclamation): a sale by a non-owner is essentially a scenario where the true owner claims that the seller acted without his permission, so it is a form of reclamation. The author then states the core ruling: the owner has the option to either ratify (ijazah) or rescind (faskh) the sale.

The lesson then defines 'fuduli' linguistically and technically. Linguistically, it is derived from 'fudul' (the plural of fadl, meaning surplus or excess), and it is used derogatorily for someone who meddles in what does not concern him. Technically, in fiqh, a fuduli is a person who is neither the owner (asil) nor an authorized agent (wakil).

The scenario: a person sells the property of another. The owner's ratification is subject to conditions: the sold item must still exist, the original parties must still be the same, and if the price was in kind ('urud), it must also remain. Ratification can be express (e.g., 'I ratify the sale') or implied (e.g., receiving or demanding the price). The commentary discusses borderline expressions: saying 'ahsanta' or 'asabta' (praise) is not ratification according to Abu Hanifa (due to possibility of sarcasm), but Imam Muhammad considers it ratification by istihsan. Saying 'bi'sa ma sana'ta' (criticism) is considered rejection in the Zahir al-Riwayah (the relied-upon position), though a divergent view in al-Muntaqa considers it ratification. The fatwa is on the Zahir al-Riwayah position.

Key Terms
بيع الفضولي (bay' al-fuduli): A sale executed by a person who is neither the owner nor an authorized agent of the owner, without his permission.
الفضولي (al-fuduli): The person who sells the property of another without permission.
الإجازة (al-ijazah): The owner's ratification of a sale by a non-owner, making the contract binding and valid retroactively.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person sells another's car without permission. The owner says: 'You have done well (ahsanta).' According to the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school, is this a valid ratification?
A) Yes, because it indicates approval.
B) No, because it may be sarcasm, so it is not clear consent.
C) Yes, according to Imam Muhammad by istihsan.
D) No, because the sale is void ab initio.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school treats a sale by a non-owner as suspended, not void, and the owner's ratification must be clear or by specific conduct; praise is not enough.

The Sale by an Unauthorized Person: Ruling and Conditions
Establishes ruling on bai' al-fuduli with conditions and evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a sale by an unauthorized person (fuduli) is concluded but suspended (mawquf), and identify the conditions required for the owner's ratification to be effective.

Core Idea
A sale by a non-owner is concluded, not void—it only becomes effective upon the owner's ratification.

What happens when someone sells property that does not belong to them? In the Hanafi school, such a contract is not automatically void; rather, it is considered validly structured but suspended, pending the owner's approval. This nuanced position is grounded in the hadith of 'Urwa al-Bariqi and distinguishes the Hanafi view from that of Imam al-Shafi'i. In this lesson, we will explore the ruling, the conditions for ratification, and the evidence that supports it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its parties
Recognize that 'bai' al-fuduli' involves a seller who is neither the owner nor an authorized agent. The owner has the right to either annul or ratify the sale.
2
Understand the Hanafi categorization
The sale is described as 'mun'iqad' (concluded) and 'mawquf' (suspended). This means the contract is structurally complete but its legal effects are postponed until the owner's ratification.
This is a unique Hanafi category; other schools consider it void.
3
List the conditions for effective ratification
Ratification requires that the contracting parties, the sold item (still existent and not substantially changed), and the price (if it is a specific commodity) remain as they were at the time of sale. The survival of the owner himself is also necessary.
Intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad presume existence unless known otherwise; Abu Yusuf later retracted.
4
Examine the proof (dalil)
The primary evidence is the hadith of 'Urwa al-Bariqi, who bought and sold on behalf of the Prophet without explicit prior permission, and the Prophet approved his transaction. This shows that a sale by an unauthorized person can be concluded and later ratified.
The hadith is narrated by al-Bukhari and others.
5
Note the inter-madhhab dispute
Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad argue that the sale is not concluded because the seller lacks authority. Their analogy with the sale of a runaway slave is rejected by Hanafis because the fuduli has possession, unlike a runaway slave.
The practical difference: In Hanafi, ownership transfers upon ratification; in Shafi'i, a new contract is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيع الفضول
ولمالك باع غيره ملكه فسخه، وله إجازته
فصل في بيع الفضول
ولمالك باع غيره ملكه فسخه، وله إجازته
[ ١ ] قوله : ولمالك ؛ لَمَّا فرغ المصنف عن مسائل الاستحقاق، وكان بيعُ الفضولي من صور الاستحقاق، فإنَّ المستحق إنّما يقول عند الدعوى : هذا ملكي ، ومن باعك إنما باعك بغير إذني ، فهو عين بيع الفضولي، فشرع في مسائله، وقال: ولمالك... الخ، ولو أتي بلفظ فصل هاهنا لكان أحسن كما لا يخفى.
والفضولي : بضم الفاء وفتحها خطأ ، نسبةً إلى الفُضُول، جمع الفضل، وهو الزيادة، قال في «المغرب» (١) : الفضلُ الزّيادة، وقد غلب جمعه على ما لا خير فيه، حتى قيل : فُضُول بلا فضل وسِنِّ بلاسِنٌ وَطُولٌ بلا طَوْل وَعَرْضُ بِلا عِرْضِ ثم قيل لمن يشتغل بما لا يعنيه : فُضُولي ، إنّه صار بالغلبة لهذا المعنى كالعلم له ، ولم يصر إلى الواحدِ في النسبة ، كما في أعرابي وأنصاري، وفي اصطلاح الفقهاء : من ليس بأصيل ولا وكيل. وصورة هذه المسألة أنه إذا باع رجلٌ ملك غيره، فالمالك بالخيار إن شاءَ أَجازَ ذلك البيع إذا كان المعقود عليه باقياً، والمتعاقدان بحالهما، وكذا الثَّمن إن كان من العروض، وإن شاء فسخه.
[٢] قوله : وله إجازته ؛ بأن يقول : أجزتُ البيع، أو بأن يقبض الثمن، أو يطلبه، فإن قال : أحسنت، أو أصبت، أو كفيتني مؤنة البيع، لا يكون إجازة ؛ لاحتمال الاستهزاء ، وقال محمد الله : إن قوله : أحسنت وأصبت إجازة استحساناً، ذكره القاضي خان له في «فتاواه». وفي «المنتقى»: إنَّ المالك إذا قال : بئسما صنعت يكون إجازة، وفي ظاهر الرواية يكون رداً، وعليه الفتوى. كذا في الفصول العمادية ذكره العلامة البرْجَنْدِي له في «شرح النقاية».
(١) «المغرب»(ص٣٦٢).
إن بقي العاقدان والمبيع، وكذا الثَّمن إن كان عرضاً
فسخه مبتدأ، ولمالك خبره مقدماً، وهذا بيعُ الفُضُولي، وهو منعقدٌ عندنا خلافاً للشَّافِعِي 
قوله : إن بقي ؛ أي إجازة المالك مشروط ببقاء المتعاقدين والمعقود عليه والمعقود له، فإنَّ الإجازة تصرُّف في العقدِ من التوقف إلى النفاذ البات، فيفتقر إلى قيام العقد، وقيامه بقيام هذه الأشياء.
قوله : والمبيع ؛ والمراد بكون المبيع قائماً : أن لا يكون متغيراً، بحيث يعد شيئاً كبيراً ذا طحن ؛ لأنَّ الملك لم ينتقل إليه بالعقد، فلا ينتقل بعد هلاكه، ولو لم يعلم حال المبيع وقت الإجازة من بقائه وصور جاز البيعُ في قول أبي يوسف له أوَّلاً ، وهو قول محمد ؛ لأنَّ الأصل بقاؤه ، ثم رجع وقال : لا يصح ما لم يعلم بقاؤه ، كذا في «البحر»(1).
وإنما لم يذكر بقاء المالكِ الأوَّلِ لكونه ظاهراً ، فإنَّ بموته يبطل العقد الموقوف، فبعد ذلك لا يفيد إجازة الوارث.
قوله : إن كان عرضاً ؛ لأن العرض يتعيَّن بالتعيين، فصار كالمبيع يشترط بقاؤه ، وبهذا يفهم أنَّ الثمن إذا كان ديناً كالدراهم والدنانير والفلوس، والكيلي والوزني الموصوف بغيرِ عينه يحتاجُ إلى الأربعةِ المذكورة، ولا يشترط بقاء الثمن.
قوله : خلافاً للشافعي له ؛ قال الشافعي له في الجديد، وأحمد له في رواية : لا ينعقد ؛ لأنه أضاف ذلك المبيع إلى محل لا ولاية له عليه ؛ ولأنَّ بيع الآبق لا ينعقد مع كونه مملوكاً ؛ لعدم القدرة على تسليمه فما لا يملكه البائع أولى.
ولنا : ما روى البخاري له وغيره : إِنَّ النبيَّ صلى الله عليه وسلم : «دفع إلى عروة البارقي ديناراً ليشتري له به شاءً لأضحية، فاشترى به شاتين، وباع أحدهما بدينار، وجاء بشاةٍ ودينار إلى النبي ، فقال له النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : بارك الله في صفقتك»(٢).
Chapter on the sale of the unauthorized person (fuduli). And the owner, if someone else sold his property, has the option to annul it, and he has the option to ratify it. [Commentary: His saying: 'and the owner' – when the author finished with issues of claim of ownership, and the sale of the fuduli is a form of claim of ownership, for the claimant says at litigation: 'This is my property, and the one who sold to you sold it without my permission', so it is exactly the sale of the fuduli, so he began its issues, and said: 'and the owner' ... etc. And it would have been better if he had used the word 'chapter' here, as is not hidden. Al-fuduli: with damm on the fa' and with fatha is an error, attributed to al-fudul, plural of al-fadl, which is increase. He said in al-Mughrib: al-fadl is increase, and its plural has come to predominate over what has no good in it, until it was said: 'Fudul without fadl, and sinn without sin, and tul without tawl, and 'ard without 'ird.' Then it was said for one who busies himself with what does not concern him: fuduli; it became by predominance for this meaning like a proper noun, and it did not become to the singular in attribution, as in a'rabi and ansari. And in the terminology of jurists: one who is neither the principal nor an agent. The form of this issue is that when a man sells the property of another, the owner has the choice: if he wishes, he may ratify that sale if the object of contract remains, and the two contractors are in the same state, and likewise the price if it is from commodities, and if he wishes, he may annul it. His saying: 'and he has the option to ratify it' – by saying: 'I have ratified the sale', or by taking possession of the price, or by demanding it. But if he says: 'You have done well', or 'You have done correctly', or 'You have spared me the trouble of selling', it is not a ratification, due to the possibility of mockery. And Muhammad said: His saying 'you have done well' and 'you have done correctly' is a ratification by istihsan, as mentioned by al-Qadikhan in his Fatawa. And in al-Muntqa: When the owner says 'you have done badly' it is a ratification, and in the apparent transmission it is a rejection, and upon it is the fatwa. Similarly in al-Fusul al-Imadiyya, mentioned by al-Barjandi in his Sharh al-Niqaya. (1) al-Mughrib p.362. If the two contractors and the sold item remain, and likewise the price if it is a commodity – 'its annulment' is the mubtada, and 'for the owner' is the predicate preceded, and this is the sale of the fuduli, and it is concluded according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i. His saying: 'if they remain' – meaning the owner's ratification is conditional on the remaining of the two contractors, the object of contract, and the party to whom the contract is made (the price), for ratification is a disposition in the contract from suspension to final execution, so it requires the existence of the contract, and its existence by the existence of these things. His saying: 'and the sold item' – and what is meant by the sold item being existent is that it has not changed to the extent that it is considered a different thing, like if it has been ground, because ownership did not transfer to him by the contract, so it cannot transfer after its destruction. And if the state of the sold item at the time of ratification regarding its existence is not known, the sale is valid according to the first opinion of Abu Yusuf, and it is the opinion of Muhammad, because the principle is its existence. Then Abu Yusuf retracted and said: it is not valid unless its existence is known. Thus in al-Bahr (1). And he did not mention the survival of the original owner because it is apparent, for by his death the suspended contract becomes void, so after that the ratification of the heir is not effective. His saying: 'if it is a commodity' – because a specific commodity is determined by specification, so it becomes like the sold item requiring its existence. And by this it is understood that when the price is a debt like dirhams, dinars, and coins, and measured or weighed items described without specification, it requires the four mentioned [contracting parties, sold item, price as a specific commodity?], and the existence of the price is not required. His saying: 'contrary to al-Shafi'i' – al-Shafi'i in the new opinion, and Ahmad in one narration said: it is not concluded because he attached that sale to a place over which he has no authority, and because the sale of a runaway slave is not concluded even though it is owned, due to inability to deliver, so that which the seller does not own is more deserving [of voidness]. And our proof is what al-Bukhari and others narrated: The Prophet (peace be upon him) gave a dinar to 'Urwa al-Bariqi to buy a sheep for him for sacrifice, so he bought two sheep with it, sold one of them for a dinar, and came with a sheep and a dinar to the Prophet, so the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'May Allah bless your transaction.' (2)
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah on the matn of al-Wiqayah, discussing the chapter on the sale of the unauthorized person (bai' al-fuduli). The author begins by linking this topic to the previous section on claims of ownership, since both involve a person selling property that is not his. He defines 'al-fuduli' linguistically and in legal terminology: one who is neither the principal nor an agent. The core ruling is stated: if a person sells the property of another without permission, the owner has the choice to either annul the sale or ratify it. Ratification is only valid if the contracting parties, the sold item, and the price (if it is a specific commodity) remain unchanged. The commentary then discusses the precise wording of ratification: explicit statements like 'I ratify' or actions like taking the price constitute ratification, but ambiguous praise like 'you did well' is not ratification due to possibility of mockery; however, Imam Muhammad holds it as ratification by istihsan. There is also a difference regarding the owner saying 'you did badly' – in the apparent transmission it is rejection, but some hold it as ratification. The text then elaborates on the conditions: the sold item must not have changed substantially (e.g., ground wheat). There is an intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Yusuf initially and Muhammad hold that the sale is valid if the existence is presumed, but Abu Yusuf later retracted. The survival of the original owner is not mentioned because it is obvious; upon his death the suspended contract becomes void. If the price is a debt (e.g., dirhams), its existence is not required. Finally, the passage presents the inter-madhhab dispute: Shafi'i and Ahmad argue that such a sale is not concluded because the seller has no authority and analogize it to the sale of a runaway slave. The Hanafi response is the hadith of 'Urwa al-Bariqi, where the Prophet approved 'Urwa's sale of a sheep bought with the Prophet's money without explicit prior permission, proving that such a sale is concluded and can be ratified. This establishes the Hanafi position that bai' al-fuduli is valid but suspended (mawquf).

Key Terms
الفضولي (al-fudūlī): One who is neither the principal nor the agent in a transaction; the unauthorized seller.
الإجازة (al-ijāzah): Ratification; the owner's approval of a contract made by an unauthorized person, making it binding.
البيع الموقوف (al-bay' al-mawqūf): A suspended sale; a contract that is concluded but its effect is pending the owner's ratification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, what is the status of a sale made by an unauthorized person (fuduli) at the time of the contract?
A) The sale is void (batil) and cannot be ratified.
B) The sale is concluded (mun'iqad) but suspended pending the owner's ratification.
C) The sale is valid only if the seller was unaware of his lack of authority.
D) The sale is considered a gift until the owner explicitly agrees.

Key Takeaway
A sale by a non-owner is not automatically void in Hanafi fiqh; it is concluded but suspended, and can become fully effective upon the owner's clear ratification, provided the conditions for ratification are met.

Conditions for Ratification of a Fuduli Sale
Conditions for effective ratification of fuduli sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid ratification of a fuduli sale and distinguish when the survival of the price is required.

Core Idea
Ratification requires survival of parties, subject matter, and price (if specific); destruction voids the contract.

Ratification (ijazah) transforms a suspended fuduli contract into a binding one, but it can only attach to an existing contract. This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya details the conditions that must remain intact at the time of ratification: the contracting parties, the sold item, and the price if it is a specific good. It also highlights an intra-Hanafi difference regarding knowledge of the item's existence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is ratification (ijazah) of a fuduli sale. It is a valid act that makes the suspended contract binding and retroactively effective.
The contract itself is mawquf (suspended) until ratification.
2
Extract the conditions for ratification
The text explicitly conditions ratification on: (1) survival of the buyer and seller, (2) survival of the sold item, (3) survival of the owner (implied), (4) survival of the price if it is a specific good. Note that the price condition depends on its nature.
The owner's survival is not mentioned because it is obvious; death voids the contract.
3
Understand the rationale
Ratification is a disposal in the contract; it requires the contract to be standing. The contract's standing depends on the existence of these elements.
If any required element is destroyed, the contract ceases to exist and ratification cannot revive it.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi difference
Abu Yusuf initially held that lack of knowledge of the sold item's existence does not prevent ratification, as the principle is its existence. Later he retracted and required knowledge. Muhammad held the initial view consistently.
The mufta bihi (operative position) is the later view of Abu Yusuf: knowledge of existence is required.
5
Apply the price distinction
If the price is a specific good ('ard), its survival is required. If it is a debt (dirhams, dinars, etc.), survival is not required; only the four general conditions apply.
This is because a specific good is determined, while a debt is a liability in the dhimmah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن بقي العاقدان والمبيع، وكذا الثَّمن إن كان عرضاً. قوله : إن بقي ؛ أي إجازة المالك مشروط ببقاء المتعاقدين والمعقود عليه والمعقود له، فإنَّ الإجازة تصرُّف في العقدِ من التوقف إلى النفاذ البات، فيفتقر إلى قيام العقد، وقيامه بقيام هذه الأشياء. قوله : والمبيع ؛ والمراد بكون المبيع قائماً : أن لا يكون متغيراً، بحيث يعد شيئاً كبيراً ذا طحن؛ لأنَّ الملك لم ينتقل إليه بالعقد، فلا ينتقل بعد هلاكه، ولو لم يعلم حال المبيع وقت الإجازة من بقائه وصور جاز البيعُ في قول أبي يوسف له أوَّلاً، وهو قول محمد؛ لأنَّ الأصل بقاؤه، ثم رجع وقال : لا يصح ما لم يعلم بقاؤه، كذا في «البحر». وإنما لم يذكر بقاء المالكِ الأوَّلِ لكونه ظاهراً، فإنَّ بموته يبطل العقد الموقوف، فبعد ذلك لا يفيد إجازة الوارث. قوله : إن كان عرضاً؛ لأن العرض يتعيَّن بالتعيين، فصار كالمبيع يشترط بقاؤه، وبهذا يفهم أنَّ الثمن إذا كان ديناً كالدراهم والدنانير والفلوس، والكيلي والوزني الموصوف بغيرِ عينه يحتاجُ إلى الأربعةِ المذكورة، ولا يشترط بقاء الثمن.
If the two contracting parties and the sold item remain, and likewise the price if it is a specific good. His statement: 'If [they] remain' means the owner's ratification is conditioned upon the continuation of the contracting parties, the object of contract, and the person for whom the contract is made. For ratification is a disposal in the contract from suspension to binding effect, so it requires the contract to be standing, and its standing is by the standing of these things. His statement: 'the sold item' means by the sold item being extant that it has not changed such that it is counted as a significant thing like milled grain, because ownership did not transfer to him by the contract, so it does not transfer after its destruction. If the state of the sold item at the time of ratification regarding its existence was not known, then the sale is valid in the opinion of Abu Yusuf initially, and it is the opinion of Muhammad, because the principle is its existence; then he retracted and said it is not valid unless its existence is known, as in 'al-Bahr'. The survival of the original owner was not mentioned because it is apparent, for with his death the suspended contract becomes void, and after that the heir's ratification is of no benefit. His statement: 'if it is a specific good' because a specific good is determined by specification, so it becomes like the sold item, its survival is required. By this it is understood that if the price is a debt, like dirhams, dinars, and fulus, and measured and weighed items described without specification, it requires the four mentioned things, and the survival of the price is not required.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary explaining the conditions for the ratification (ijazah) of a fuduli (unauthorized agent) sale. The author states that ratification is effective only if the contracting parties (buyer and seller), the sold item, and the price (if it is a specific good) are still extant. The rationale is that ratification is a disposal in a contract, which must be in existence. The sold item must not have changed substantially, such that it is no longer considered the same item; otherwise ownership cannot transfer after destruction. There is an intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Yusuf initially held that the sale is valid even if the existence of the item at ratification is unknown, because the principle is that it exists. He later retracted, saying that validity depends on knowledge of existence. Muhammad al-Shaybani held the former view. The death of the original owner voids the contract automatically, so ratification by an heir is ineffective. If the price is a specific good ('ard), it also must survive because it is determined similarly. But if the price is a debt (like currency or unspecified fungibles), only the four conditions (parties, object, and beneficiary) apply, and survival of the price is not required. This distinction is important in Hanafi muamalat.

Key Terms
فضولي (fudūlī): A person who conducts a transaction without authorization from the owner, acting on his own initiative.
إجازة (ijāzah): Ratification by the owner after the contract, making it binding and retroactively effective.
عرض (ʿaraḍ): A specific, tangible good (as opposed to a debt or currency).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling regarding a fuduli sale if the sold item has been destroyed before the owner's ratification?
A) The sale is valid and the fuduli must provide a substitute.
B) The sale is void because ownership could not transfer.
C) The sale is suspended and the fuduli is liable.
D) The sale is valid if the price remains.

Key Takeaway
For a fuduli sale to be ratified, the contracting parties, the sold item (unchanged), and the price (if specific) must survive; otherwise the contract is void.
