Validity of Fuduli Sale: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Proves fuduli sale is concluded with hadith and rebuts Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that a fuduli sale is concluded (munʿaqid) but suspended (mawqūf), and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
A fuduli sale is valid but suspended – authority affects effectiveness, not validity.

One of the classic inter-madhhab disputes in Islamic commercial law concerns the sale made by an unauthorized person (fuduli). Imam al-Shafi'i (in his new opinion) and Imam Ahmad (in one narration) consider such a sale void ab initio, while the Hanafi school maintains it is concluded but ineffective until ratified. This disagreement turns on whether authority is a condition of contract validity or only of its binding effect. In this lesson, you will trace the proof structure for the Hanafi position, examine the opposing arguments, and understand the practical consequence of the difference.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on fuduli sale
Identify what the Hanafi school holds: the sale is concluded (munʿaqid) but its effect (nafādh) is suspended (mawqūf) pending the owner's ratification. This is the muftā bihī position from Zahir al-Riwayah.
Note the distinction between inʿiqād (conclusion) and nafādh (effectiveness). The contract has all essential elements but lacks one condition for binding effect.
2
Identify the opposing view and its proofs
The text cites al-Shafi'i (new opinion) and Ahmad: the sale is not concluded at all. Their evidence: lack of authority and analogy to the sale of an escaped slave, which is invalid due to inability to deliver.
The opposing school treats authority (wilāyah) as a condition of validity (ṣiḥḥah), not just of effectiveness.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof from hadith
Examine the two narrated incidents (ʿUrwa al-Bāriqī and Ḥakīm ibn Ḥizām). In both cases, the Companion purchased without explicit prior authorization, and the Prophet approved the transaction. Extract the legal implication: the Prophet's approval indicates the contract was valid and could be ratified.
The ḥadīth is aḥad but its indication (dalālah) is clear (qaṭʿī) in showing approval of unauthorized sale, as per Hanafi uṣūl.
4
Examine the rational (ʿaqlī) argument
The text adds: the fudūlī is a capable person (sane, adult), the property is valuable, and there is no harm to the owner because he has the option to ratify or rescind. Thus the contract is concluded but its effectiveness is suspended.
This rational argument shows that the contract is beneficial and not harmful, supporting the ruling that it is valid but suspended.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilāf)
Under the Shafi'i view, the contract is void; no rights or obligations arise. Under the Hanafi view, the contract is suspended; if the owner ratifies, it becomes binding retroactively. If the owner rejects, the contract is rescinded without penalty.
The thamarah al-khilāf shows the real-world impact: the Hanafi view allows flexibility and avoids unnecessary voiding of transactions.

Source Text

قوله : خلافاً للشافعي له ؛ قال الشافعي له في الجديد، وأحمد له في رواية : لا ينعقد؛ لأنه أضاف ذلك المبيع إلى محل لا ولاية له عليه؛ ولأنَّ بيع الآبق لا ينعقد مع كونه مملوكاً؛ لعدم القدرة على تسليمه فما لا يملكه البائع أولى. ولنا : ما روى البخاري له وغيره : إِنَّ النبيَّ صلى الله عليه وسلم دفع إلى عروة البارقي ديناراً ليشتري له به شاءٌ لأضحية، فاشترى به شاتين، وباع أحدهما بدينار، وجاء بشاةٍ ودينار إلى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، فقال له النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : بارك الله في صفقتك. وروى الطبراني في «معجمه»، والترمذي في «جامعه» مثل هذا : عن حكيم بن حزام، إلا أنَّ حكيماً : أعطاه رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ديناراً ليشتري به أضحية، فاشترى أضحية بدينار، فباعها بدينارين، ثم اشترى أضحية بدينار، وجاءه بدينار وأضحية، فتصدق رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بالدينار، ودعا له بالبركة. ولأنَّ هذا تصرف صدرَ من أهله لأنه عاقل بالغ مضافاً إلى محله؛ لأنه مال متقوّم، ولا ضرر في انعقادِهِ على المالك؛ لأنه مخيّر، فإن رأى فيه مصلحة أجازه وإلا فسخه، فينعقد ويتوقف نفوذه على إجازة المالك، كذا في «كمال الدراية».
His statement: 'contrary to al-Shafi'i'. Al-Shafi'i said in his new opinion, and Ahmad in one narration: it is not concluded, because he attributed that sale to a domain over which he has no authority; and because the sale of an escaped slave is not concluded despite being owned, due to inability to deliver, so what the seller does not own is even more deserving. Our proof: what al-Bukhari and others narrated: that the Prophet gave a dinar to 'Urwa al-Bariqi to buy a sheep for sacrifice for him. He bought two sheep with it, sold one for a dinar, and came to the Prophet with a sheep and a dinar, and the Prophet said: 'May Allah bless your transaction.' And al-Tabarani in his 'Mu'jam' and al-Tirmidhi in his 'Jami' narrated similar from Hakim ibn Hizam, except that Hakim: the Messenger of Allah gave him a dinar to buy a sacrifice, so he bought a sacrifice for a dinar, sold it for two dinars, then bought a sacrifice for a dinar, and came to him with a dinar and a sacrifice, so the Messenger of Allah gave the dinar in charity and prayed for blessing for him. And because this is a disposal emanating from its proper person, as he is a sane adult, and attributed to its proper place, as it is a valuable property, and there is no harm in its conclusion for the owner, because he has a choice: if he sees a benefit he ratifies it, otherwise he rescinds it. So it is concluded, and its effectiveness is suspended upon the owner's ratification, as in 'Kamal al-Diraya'.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the legal status of a fuduli sale (a sale made by an unauthorized person). Imam al-Shafi'i (in his new opinion) and Ahmad ibn Hanbal (in one narration) hold that such a sale is not even concluded (i.e., it is void). Their argument: the seller has no authority over the item, and since one cannot deliver what he does not own, the sale is invalid. The Hanafi position, stated with the proof, is that the sale is concluded (valid but suspended) pending the owner's ratification. The Hanafis present two hadiths: the first from 'Urwa al-Bariqi, who bought two sheep with the Prophet's dinar without explicit permission, sold one and returned with a sheep and a dinar; the Prophet approved. The second from Hakim ibn Hizam, similarly showing approval. These prove that the Prophet validated an unauthorized sale. The Hanafi rational argument adds: the fuduli is a sane adult capable of contract, the subject matter is valuable, and the owner is not harmed because he can choose to ratify or rescind. Therefore, the contract is concluded but suspended. This ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school is that the fuduli sale is valid and suspended, as stated here.

Key Terms
منعقد (munʿaqid): A contract that has been validly concluded (all essential elements present) but may lack full effect due to a defect like lack of authority.
موقوف (mawqūf): A legal act that is valid but whose binding effect is suspended pending a condition (like ratification).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary Hanafi proof that a fuduli sale is valid (concluded)?
A) The sale of an escaped slave is valid by analogy.
B) The Prophet approved ʿUrwa al-Bāriqī's unauthorized purchase.
C) The owner can always ratify later, so there is no harm.
D) The fudūlī has implied authority as a representative.

Key Takeaway
A fuduli sale is concluded (munʿaqid) but not effective (nāfidh) until the owner ratifies it; this distinction is unique to the Hanafi school.

Ownership of the Price and Fuduli's Liability After Ratification
Hanafi ruling on fuduli liability after ratification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on the fuduli's liability for the sold item after ratification, distinguishing between price as debt (amanah) and price as specific good (liability to return like or value).

Core Idea
After ratification, the fuduli's liability depends on the nature of the price: if debt, no liability; if specific good, he must return the sold item's like or value.

The fuduli (unauthorized agent) who sells another's property faces different legal consequences after the owner ratifies the sale. This lesson examines a crucial passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya that clarifies when the fuduli is a mere trustee and when he becomes a buyer for himself, obligated to compensate the owner. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the rules of fuduli sales in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios for the price
Read the passage and note the two cases: when the price is not a specific good (i.e., debt) and when it is a specific good ('ard). These lead to different rulings.
The price is a specific good if it is a determined, individual item owned by the fuduli (e.g., a gold coin, a watch).
2
Determine the ruling for each scenario
For debt-price: the fuduli holds the price as a trust (amanah) and is not liable for accidental loss. For specific-good-price: the fuduli is a buyer for himself and must compensate the owner with the sold item's like or value.
The owner's ratification only affects the price ownership in the specific-good case, not the contract.
3
Analyze the qiyas (analogy)
The passage analogizes to paying a debt with another's property. The fuduli uses the owner's sold item as the price, effectively borrowing it. Thus, he must return the like or value, just as a borrower does.
This is a qiyas khafi (hidden analogy) because the borrowing is implicit (dimnan).
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school holds that the fuduli is not liable after ratification because the contract becomes retroactively valid for the owner. The Hanafi school distinguishes based on the nature of the price.
This difference is practical: in the specific-good-price case, the Hanafi fuduli must compensate; the Shafi'i fuduli does not.
5
Apply to a scenario
Consider a case: A sells B's car (worth 10,000) as a fuduli for a specific gold coin (worth 5,000) owned by A. After B ratifies, what is A's liability? According to the Hanafi ruling, A must provide a similar car or its value to B.
The gold coin remains A's; he effectively bought the car for himself using B's property as the price.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: للمجيز؛ وهو المالك، ولم يقل: للمالك؛ ليعلم أنه إنما يملكه بعد الإجازة أما قبل الإجازة فلا. قوله : ويكون أمانة عند البائع؛ وهو الفضولي؛ لأنه حينئذ بمنزلة الوكيل، فإنَّ الإجازة اللاحقة كالوكالة السابقة، حتى لا يضمن بالهلاكِ في يده سواء هلك بعد الإجازة أو قبله، وهذا إذا لم يكن الثمن من العروض. أما إذا كان عرضاً فكان مملوكاً للفُضُوليّ، وإجازة المالك إجازة نقد لا إجازة عقد؛ لأنه لَمَّا كان متعيّناً كان شراء من وجه والشراء لا يتوقف، بل ينفد على المباشر إن وجد نفاذاً، فيكون ملكاً له، وبإجازة المالكِ لا ينتقل إليه، بل تأخير إجازته في النقد لا في العقد. ويجب على الفضولي مثلُ المبيع إن كان مثليّاً، وإلا فقيمته؛ لأنه لَمَّا صَارَ البدلُ له صار مشترياً لنفسه بمال الغير مستقرضاً له في ضمن الشراء فيجب عليه ردّه، كما قضى دينه بمال الغير، واستقراض غير المثلي جائز ضمناً، وإن لم يجز قصداً، كذا في «مجمع الأنهر» وغيره.
His statement: 'for the ratifier' i.e. the owner, and he did not say 'for the owner' to indicate that he only owns it after ratification, not before. His statement: 'and it is a trust with the seller' i.e. the fuduli, because he is then like an agent, for subsequent ratification is like prior agency, so he is not liable for destruction in his hand, whether it perishes after ratification or before. This is if the price is not of specific goods. But if it is a specific good, then it was owned by the fuduli, and the owner's ratification is a ratification of the price, not of the contract, because when it is determined, it is a purchase from one aspect, and purchase is not suspended, but rather it takes effect on the direct party if it could take effect, so it becomes his property, and by the owner's ratification it does not transfer to him; rather, his ratification is delayed in the price, not in the contract. And the fuduli must provide a similar of the sold item if it is fungible, otherwise its value; because when the substitute became his, he became a buyer for himself with another's property, borrowing it within the purchase, so he must return it, just as he paid his debt with another's property, and borrowing a non-fungible is permissible indirectly, even if not permissible directly, as in 'Majma' al-Anhur' and others.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the legal consequences after ratification of a fuduli sale. The author explains two key points: (1) Ownership of the price: After ratification, the price (thaman) becomes the property of the ratifier (the original owner), but before ratification it is not owned by him. While in the hands of the fuduli, it is considered a trust (amanah), so he is not liable for accidental destruction, whether before or after ratification. This ruling applies only when the price is a debt (e.g., currency). (2) If the price is a specific good ('ard), it is owned by the fuduli at the time of sale, and the owner's ratification is only for the price, not the contract, because the purchase of a specific good by the fuduli is effective immediately on himself (since it is a determined item). The owner cannot reclaim the specific good; rather, the fuduli becomes liable to deliver the sold item to the owner? Actually the text says: the fuduli must deliver the like or value of the sold item to the owner. This is because when the price (the specific good) became the fuduli's, he effectively bought the sold item for himself with the owner's property (the sold item) by borrowing it within the sale. Therefore, he must return the like or value of the sold item to the owner. The proof is given by qiyas (analogy): just as if someone pays his debt using another's property, he must reimburse that other. The Hanafi school allows the implicit borrowing of non-fungibles even if explicit borrowing is not permitted. This ruling is from the relied-upon sources like 'Majma' al-Anhur'.

Key Terms
أمانة (amānah): An item held in trust; the holder is not liable for accidental loss unless negligent.
مثلي (mithlī): A fungible good that can be replaced by similar items (e.g., grain, currency).
استقراض (istiqrāḍ): Borrowing a property for use, with obligation to return a similar or value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What must the fuduli provide if the sold item is a non-fungible (qimi)?
A) The exact same item.
B) Its value (qimah).
C) A similar item (mithl).
D) Nothing, because ratification absolves him.

Key Takeaway
The fuduli's liability after ratification depends on whether the price is a specific good (he must compensate) or a debt (he is a trustee).

Status of the Price in a Fuduli Sale
Ruling that the price becomes owned by the owner upon approval and is a trust in the seller's hands

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on ownership and liability of the price (thaman) in a fuduli sale and apply it to distinguish cash from goods scenarios.

Core Idea
The price becomes the owner's property only after approval, and before that, it is a trust in the fuduli's hands.

This passage explains a fundamental ruling in Hanafi contract law: the fate of the price when an unauthorized person (fuduli) sells another's property. The owner's subsequent approval not only validates the sale but also retroactively determines who owns the price and how the fuduli holds it. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping the Hanafi concept of the suspended contract (bay' mawquf) and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key legal actors and terms
Note that the 'mujiz' is the owner who authorizes, the 'fuduli' is the unauthorized seller, and the 'thaman' (price) is the consideration. Recognize that approval is retrospective.
2
Extract the primary ruling from the text
The text states: 'And it is owned by the one who authorizes, and a trust with its seller.' Understand that ownership of the price transfers to the owner upon approval, and the fuduli holds it as a trust (amanah) before and after approval.
3
Analyze the evidence (dalil) provided
Two hadith are cited: 'Urwa's sale without permission and the Prophet's approval, and Hakim ibn Hizam's transaction. These show that subsequent authorization validates the sale and determines the status of the price. Also a rational argument is given: no harm to the owner because he has a choice.
The proofs are zanni in transmission and indication but sufficient for a ruling in mu'amalat.
4
Differentiate between cash and goods as price
If the price is cash ('ayn), it becomes a trust in the fuduli's hands. If the price is goods ('urud), the goods are owned by the fuduli from the start, and the owner's approval relates only to the monetary value. Consequently, the fuduli owes an equivalent or value of the sold item.
This distinction is critical for determining liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And it is owned by the one who authorizes, and a trust with its seller: meaning, if the owner approves, the price is owned by him, and it is a trust in the hand of the seller. And he has the right to cancel it before authorization. And the indication is that 'Urwa sold the second sheep without the Prophet's permission, and the Prophet authorized it. And al-Tabarani in his Mu'jam and al-Tirmidhi in his Jami' narrated similar: from Hakim ibn Hizam that the Prophet gave him a dinar to buy a sacrificial animal, so he bought one for a dinar, sold it for two dinars, then bought another for a dinar, and brought a dinar and a sacrificial animal, so the Prophet gave the dinar in charity and prayed for blessing for him. So it is concluded and its effect is suspended on the owner's authorization, as in Kamal al-Dirayah. [1] His statement: 'for the authorizer' means the owner, and he did not say 'for the owner' to indicate that he only owns it after authorization, not before. [2] His statement: 'and a trust with the seller' means the unauthorized seller; because at that time he is like an agent, indeed subsequent authorization is like prior agency, so he is not liable for destruction in his hand, whether destroyed after authorization or before. This is if the price is not from goods. If it is goods, then it is owned by the unauthorized person, and the owner's authorization is authorization of the money not authorization of the contract; because since it was specified, it was a purchase in one sense and the purchase is not suspended, rather it takes effect on the direct party if it takes effect, so it is owned by him, and by the owner's authorization it does not transfer to him; rather delaying his authorization concerns the money not the contract. And borrowing non-fungibles is permissible implicitly even if not explicitly, as in Majma' al-Anhur and others.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the status of the price (thaman) in a sale by an unauthorized person (fuduli). The author first states the ruling: the price becomes owned by the owner (mujiz) upon approval and is a trust (amanah) in the hands of the fuduli seller. The fuduli also has the right to cancel before approval. The proof (dalil) is provided from the hadith of 'Urwa, who sold a sheep without permission and the Prophet approved, and the hadith of Hakim ibn Hizam where the Prophet approved a similar transaction. A rational proof is also given: the action is valid because it comes from a qualified person (adult, sane) and relates to valuable property; there is no harm to the owner who has the choice to approve or cancel. The commentary by the sharh explains that the word 'mujiz' is used instead of 'malik' to emphasize that ownership is only after approval. The price is a trust in the fuduli's hand, similar to an agent, so he is not liable for accidental destruction. However, if the price is not cash but goods ('urud), then the goods are already owned by the fuduli, and the owner's approval pertains to the money value, not the contract. In that case, the fuduli is considered a buyer for himself using the owner's money, and he must return an equivalent or value if the sold item is fungible or not. This is according to the Hanafi school as recorded in Kamal al-Dirayah and Majma' al-Anhur.

Key Terms
المجيز (al-mujiz): The owner who authorizes the fuduli sale; his authorization makes the contract effective retroactively.
الفضولي (al-fuduli): The person who sells property without the owner's prior permission; his role is similar to an agent once authorization is given.
الأمانة (al-amanah): Trust; the fuduli holds the price as a trust, so he is not liable for accidental loss.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In a fuduli sale, the price becomes owned by the owner only after approval, and before that, it is a trust in the seller's hands, with a critical distinction between cash and goods.

Emancipation by Buyer from Usurper: Validity with Retroactive Approval
Dispute over validity of emancipation by buyer when owner later approves the usurper's sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school validates the buyer's emancipation when the owner later approves the usurper's sale, and evaluate the counter-argument based on the hadith 'La 'itq fīmā lā yamliku ibn Ādam'.

Core Idea
Ownership through subsequent approval (istinād) suffices for valid emancipation, say Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

This passage from the Hidayah commentary addresses a classic dispute: if a buyer frees a slave bought from a usurper, and the owner later approves the sale, does the emancipation take effect? The Hanafi school, led by Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, says yes, using the principle of istinād (retroactive validation). Muhammad al-Shaybani, Zufar, and other schools say no, citing the absolute hadith requiring ownership at the moment of emancipation. Let's walk through the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and the Hanafi position
Identify the fact pattern: usurper sells slave, buyer emancipates, owner approves. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold the emancipation is valid (nāfidh) upon approval.
2
Identify the opposing view and its proof
Muhammad, Zufar, al-Shafi'i, and Malik say it is void (bāṭil). Their proof: the hadith 'La 'itq fīmā lā yamliku ibn Ādam' (No emancipation in what one does not own) – an absolute text requiring complete ownership at the moment of emancipation.
The hadith is narrated by Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafis argue that the owner's approval (ijāzah) retroactively establishes the buyer's ownership from the time of the sale (istinād). Thus, at the moment of emancipation, the buyer had a form of ownership that is perfected by the subsequent approval. The absolute text does not preclude retroactive ownership.
Istinād is a key Hanafi principle in suspended contracts.
4
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: if the buyer frees the slave before approval and the owner later approves, the slave is free in the Hanafi view. In the opposing view, the slave remains enslaved even after approval.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: emancipation is effective upon approval. This is from the zahir al-riwayah.
Al-Marghinani adopts this view.

(وجاز إعتاق المشتري من الغاضب لا بيعُهُ إن أجيز بيع الغاصب) : أي إذا " باع الغاصب العبد المغصوب فأعتقه المشتري ، فأجاز المالك البيع ينفد الإعتاق ، وعند محمد الله لا ينفذ ؛ لقولهِ (٣) الله : « لا عتق ) [٤] [1] قوله : أي للبائع حق الفسخ ...الخ ؛ بخلافِ الفُضُولي في النكاح، حيث لا يكون الفسح له قبل الإجازة ؛ لأن الحقوق لا ترجع إليه. (٢) قوله : أي إذا ... الخ ؛ أي إذا غصب رجل عبداً، فباع الغاصب ذلك العبد المغصوب، ثم أعتقه المشتري، ثم أجاز المولى البيع ينفذ الإعتاق عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف ، وقال محمد وزفر له وهو رواية عن أبي يوسف له لا يجوز العتق ، وهو القياس، وهذا قول الشافعي ومالك . وهذه ثانية المسألتين اللتين جرتِ المحاورة فيهما بين أبي يوسف ومحمد ، فقال أبو يوسف المحمد : رويتُ لك أنَّ العتق باطل عند أبي حنيفة ، وقال محمد : رويت لي أنَّ العتق جائز عنده، وحاصل الخلافِ راجع إلى أن إعتاق المشتري موقوف عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف له على أن ينفذ الشراء بإجازة المالك، وباطل عند محمد ده [٣] قوله : لقوله ... الخ ؛ حاصلُهُ : إنَّ العتق لا يتحقق إلا بالملك لقوله : «لا عتق فيما لا يملكه ابن آدم (۱) ، وهذا نص مطلق ، والمطلق ينصرف إلى الكامل، والبيع الذي توقف نفاده على إجازة المالكِ لا يفيد الملك ، ولو ثبت الملك حين إجازة المالك لثبت مستنداً إلى السبب السابق، وهو بيع الغاصب. والملك الثابت بطريق الاستناد ثابت من وجه دون وجه، والمصحح للإعتاق هو الملك الكامل ؛ لكون النَّص مطلقاً، وانصراف المطلق إلى الكامل، وصار كإعتاق المشتري من الغاصب إذا أدى الغاصب. [٤]قوله : لا عتق ؛ روى أبو داود والترمذي وقال : حديث حسن صحيح : عن
(And the emancipation of the buyer from the usurper is valid, but not his sale, if the usurper's sale is approved): That is, when the usurper sells the usurped slave and the buyer emancipates him, then the owner approves the sale, the emancipation is effective. According to Muhammad, it is not effective because of his saying: 'No emancipation' [4]. [1] His statement: meaning the seller has the right of rescission... etc., unlike the fuduli in marriage, where there is no rescission for him before approval because the rights do not revert to him. (2) His statement: meaning if a man usurps a slave, then the usurper sells that usurped slave, then the buyer emancipates him, then the owner approves the sale, the emancipation is effective according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad, Zufar, and a narration from Abu Yusuf say it is not valid, and that is the analogy, and this is the view of al-Shafi'i and Malik. This is the second of the two issues in which discussion occurred between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said to Muhammad: I have narrated to you that emancipation is void according to Abu Hanifa. Muhammad said: You have narrated to me that emancipation is valid with him. The essence of the dispute goes back to the fact that the buyer's emancipation is suspended according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf upon the execution of the purchase by the owner's approval, and void according to Muhammad. [3] His statement: 'Because of his saying...' The summary: Emancipation is only realized through ownership because of his saying: 'No emancipation in what the son of Adam does not own.' This is an absolute text, and the absolute is applied to the complete. The sale whose effect is suspended on the owner's approval does not confer ownership. If ownership is established at the time of the owner's approval, it is established retrospectively based on the previous cause, which is the usurper's sale. Ownership established by way of istinad is established in one sense but not another, and what validates emancipation is complete ownership because the text is absolute, and the absolute is applied to the complete. It becomes like the emancipation of the buyer from the usurper when the usurper pays. [4] His statement: 'No emancipation'; Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi narrated and said: a good and authentic hadith, from
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the validity of a buyer's emancipation of a slave purchased from a usurper (ghasib) when the owner later approves the sale. The text presents the matn stating that the emancipation is valid, and then elaborates on the intra-Hanafi difference and the views of other schools. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, if a usurper sells a slave, the buyer frees him, and then the owner approves, the emancipation takes effect (nāfidh). Muhammad, Zufar, and a narration from Abu Yusuf say it is void (bāṭil), which is also the position of al-Shafi'i and Malik. This is the second of two famous issues debated between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. The hadith 'La 'itq fīmā lā yamliku ibn Ādam' (No emancipation in what the son of Adam does not own) is cited by the opponents. The commentary explains that the Hanafi response relies on the concept of istinād (retroactive effect): upon approval, ownership relates back to the time of the sale, so the buyer had a form of ownership at the time of emancipation, albeit incomplete. The absolute text requires complete ownership, but the Hanafis argue that the retroactive ownership suffices for the validity of emancipation because the subsequent approval perfects it. The text also distinguishes this case from the fuduli in marriage, where the rights of rescission are different.

Key Terms
الغاصب (al-ghasib): The usurper who takes property without the owner's consent and sells it without permission.
الإعتاق (al-i'taq): Emancipation; the act of freeing a slave.
الاستناد (al-istinad): Retroactive validation; the owner's approval relates back to the time of the sale, giving the buyer ownership from that moment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, why is the buyer's emancipation of a slave purchased from a usurper valid when the owner later approves?
A) Because the buyer owned the slave at the time of purchase.
B) Because the owner's approval gives the buyer ownership from the start of the sale (istinad).
C) Because the buyer acted as the owner's agent.
D) Because the usurper's sale is automatically valid.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle of istinād validates a buyer's emancipation of a usurped slave upon the owner's subsequent approval, diverging from schools that require immediate ownership.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate

Manumission by Buyer from Usurper After Ratification
Intra-Hanafi dispute on validity of manumission after ratification of usurper's sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani on the validity of manumission by a buyer from a usurper after ratification, and explain the hadith and qiyas arguments for each.

Core Idea
Manumission by a non-owner can become valid if ownership is later confirmed through ratification, per Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

This passage presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute where a buyer purchases a usurped slave and frees him, then the owner ratifies the sale. Two views emerge: one side holds the manumission valid upon ratification, the other deems it entirely void. The disagreement turns on the nature of ownership at the time of manumission and the interpretation of the Prophetic hadith 'No manumission for what one does not own.'

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their ruling
First, distinguish the two positions: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say the manumission is valid upon ratification; Muhammad al-Shaybani and Zufar say it is void. Note that a narration from Abu Yusuf aligns with Muhammad.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; the opposing school (Shafi'i, Maliki) agrees with Muhammad.
2
Analyze the hadith evidence
Both sides rely on the hadith 'لا عتق له فيما لا يملك' (no manumission for what one does not own). Examine how each interprets 'what one does not own'—Muhammad requires complete ownership at time of utterance; Abu Hanifa allows suspended ownership through a valid contract.
The hadith is ahad and its meaning is zanni; this allows for interpretive flexibility.
3
Examine the qiyas arguments
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf analogize to cases like manumission by a buyer from a pawnbroker or from an heir of an indebted estate. In those cases, manumission is suspended pending ratification. Identify the common illa (effective cause): ownership through a contract that can be ratified.
Muhammad rejects this qiyas because he considers the absence of complete ownership at the time of manumission to be a fatal flaw.
4
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If the owner ratifies, the manumission is valid according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, freeing the slave absolutely. If the owner does not ratify, the manumission is void. Under Muhammad's view, the manumission remains void regardless of ratification.
This affects the slave's status and potential liability for the usurper and buyer.

[4] قوله : أي للبائع حق الفسخ ...الخ ؛ بخلافِ الفُضُولي في النكاح، حيث لا يكون الفسح له قبل الإجازة ؛ لأن الحقوق لا ترجع إليه. (٢) قوله : أي إذا ... الخ ؛ أي إذا غصب رجل عبداً، فباع الغاصب ذلك العبد المغصوب، ثم أعتقه المشتري، ثم أجاز المولى البيع ينفذ الإعتاق عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف ، وقال محمد وزفر له وهو رواية عن أبي يوسف له لا يجوز العتق ، وهو القياس، وهذا قول الشافعي ومالك . وهذه ثانية المسألتين اللتين جرتِ المحاورة فيهما بين أبي يوسف ومحمد ، فقال أبو يوسف المحمد : رويتُ لك أنَّ العتق باطل عند أبي حنيفة ، وقال محمد : رويت لي أنَّ العتق جائز عنده، وحاصل الخلافِ راجع إلى أن إعتاق المشتري موقوف عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف له على أن ينفذ الشراء بإجازة المالك، وباطل عند محمد ده
[٣] قوله : لقوله ... الخ ؛ حاصلُهُ : إنَّ العتق لا يتحقق إلا بالملك لقوله : «لا عتق فيما لا يملكه ابن آدم (۱) ، وهذا نص مطلق ، والمطلق ينصرف إلى الكامل، والبيع الذي توقف نفاده على إجازة المالكِ لا يفيد الملك ، ولو ثبت الملك حين إجازة المالك لثبت مستنداً إلى السبب السابق، وهو بيع الغاصب. والملك الثابت بطريق الاستناد ثابت من وجه دون وجه، والمصحح للإعتاق هو الملك الكامل ؛ لكون النَّص مطلقاً، وانصراف المطلق إلى الكامل، وصار كإعتاق المشتري من الغاصب إذا أدى الغاصب.
[٤] قوله : لا عتق ؛ روى أبو داود والترمذي وقال : حديث حسن صحيح : عن عمرو بن شعيب عن أبيه عن جده الله قال : قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «لا نذر لابن آدم ما لا يملك، ولا عتق له فيما لا يملك ، ولا طلاق له فيما لا يملك» (٢).
[۲] قوله : ولهما ...الخ ؛ حاصله : إنَّ ملك المشتري من الغاصب ثبت موقوفاً بتصرف مطلق موضوع ؛ لإفادة الملك، فيتوقف الإعتاق بتوقفه، وينفذ بنفاذه ؛ لأنه من حقوقه والشيء إذا نفذ نفذ بحقوقه، وإذا توقف توقف بحقوقه.
وصار كإعتاق المشتري من الراهن العبد ،المرهون، فإنه يتوقف وينفذ بإجازة المرتهن بيع الراهن، وكإعتاق المشتري من الوارث عبداً من التركةِ المستغرقة بالدين، فإنَّ نفاذه يتوقف على إجازة الغرماء، كإعتاق الوارث عبداً من التركةِ المستغرقة بالدين ، فإنَّه ينفذ إذا قضى الدين بعده أو أبرأ الغرماء منه.
بخلاف ما إذا باع المشتري من الغاصب ثم أجاز المولى البيع حيث لم يجز البيع الثاني ؛ لأنَّ الإجازة يثبتُ بها للبائع الثاني، وهو المشتري ملك بات ؛ لأنَّ الشراء سبب مطلق موضوع لإفادة الملك ، فإذا أجاز المالك البيع الأول ثبت الملك للمشتري باناً مطلقاً ؛ لوجود السبب مطلقاً. والملك الباتُ إذا طَرَاً على ملك موقوف أبطله، فيبطل الملك الموقــوف الـذي للمشتري الثاني ضرورة أنه لا يجوز اجتماع عقدين نافذين في محلّ واحدٍ على الكمال. [٢] قوله : كإعتاق المشتري ؛ أي اشتراه من الرَّاهن بلا إجازة المرتهن ، فأعتقه ثمَّ أجازه المرتهن ينفذ إعتاقه.
Book of Sale, Chapter on Rights and Entitlement and Sale by an Unauthorized Person. And it is permissible for the buyer from the usurper to manumit, but not to sell, if the sale of the usurper is ratified. That is, the seller has the right of rescission before the owner's ratification to prevent harm to himself, for the rights of the contract revert to him... (The text continues with commentary on each part.)
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the validity of manumission by a buyer who purchased a usurped slave from the usurper, after the owner ratifies the sale. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the manumission is valid and takes effect upon ratification. Muhammad al-Shaybani and Zufar (and a narration from Abu Yusuf) hold that the manumission is void, which is the position of al-Shafi'i and Malik. The dispute hinges on whether the buyer acquires ownership at the time of the contract (suspended) or only upon ratification. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf argue that the buyer's ownership is established as a suspended ownership (milk mawquf) through a contract designed to transfer ownership, so manumission, being a right of ownership, also becomes suspended and takes effect when ownership becomes definitive. Muhammad argues that the hadith 'No manumission for what one does not own' requires complete ownership at the time of manumission; since the buyer did not have full ownership at the time of manumission, it is invalid. The commentary provides analogies and reconciles with other cases.

Key Terms
غاصب (ghāṣib): A person who takes property wrongfully by force or stealth; a usurper.
إعتاق (iʿtāq): The act of freeing a slave; manumission.
إجازة (ijāzah): Ratification; the owner's consent to a contract made by an unauthorized person (fudūlī or ghāṣib).
ملك موقوف (milk mawqūf): Suspended ownership; ownership that exists provisionally and becomes definitive upon fulfillment of a condition (e.g., ratification).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the dispute discussed, what is the position of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding the manumission by a buyer from a usurper after the owner ratifies the sale?
A) The manumission is valid and takes effect immediately upon ratification.
B) The manumission is void because the buyer did not have complete ownership at the time of manumission.
C) The manumission is valid only if the buyer pays the usurper's debt.
D) The manumission is valid but requires a new contract after ratification.

Key Takeaway
The validity of manumission by a buyer from a usurper depends on whether the law recognizes suspended ownership (milk mawquf) — and this remains a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.

Acts Concerning Unowned Property: Suspended Ownership and Ratification
Expansion of ruling on acts concerning unowned property with proofs and cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on manumission of a slave not owned at the time, and explain how ratification by the true owner makes the act valid retroactively.

Core Idea
An act on unowned property is suspended, not void; ratification by the owner gives it retroactive effect.

This lesson explores a key Hanafi principle: when a person performs an act like freeing a slave they do not own, the act is not automatically void. Instead, it is suspended (mawquf) and can become valid if the true owner later ratifies the transaction. We will examine the primary evidence from the hadith and see how the school applies it to real cases involving usurpers, pawnbrokers, and heirs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its initial ruling
Determine what act is being discussed (manumission, divorce, vow) and the default ruling: it is invalid if the person does not own the property at the time.
The hadith clearly states 'no manumission for what he does not own'.
2
Introduce the concept of suspended ownership (milk mawquf)
Understand that Hanafis distinguish between absolute invalidity and suspended invalidity. If the act is performed by someone who later acquires ownership (via ratification), it becomes valid retroactively.
This is based on the principle 'al-ijaza fi al-intiha' ka al-idhn fi al-ibtida'.
3
Analyze the hadith proof
Examine the hadith of Amr ibn Shu'ayb: the negative 'la' indicates prohibition and nullity, but the condition 'ma la yamlik' leaves room for ownership retroactively.
The hadith is ahad but its meaning is clear (qati' al-dalala).
4
Apply to the case of a purchaser from a usurper
Work through the scenario: a person buys a slave from a usurper, frees him, then the true owner ratifies the sale. The Hanafi position (Sahibayn) holds the manumission takes effect because ownership is established from the time of purchase.
This is analogized to pawnbroker and heir cases.
5
Contrast with the second sale scenario
If the first purchaser resells the slave before ratification, and then the true owner ratifies the first sale, the second sale is invalidated. Understand why definitive ownership overrides suspended ownership.
Two conflicting ownerships cannot coexist.

(ولو قُطِعَ يدُهُ، ثُمَّ أُجيز فارشُهُ للمشتري) : أي قُطِعَتْ يد العبدِ فأخذ إِرْشَها ، ثُمَّ أجاز المالك البيعَ ، فأرشُهُ للمشتري ؛ لأنَّ الملك تَـم لـه مـن وقتِ الشراء، فتبيَّنَ أنَّ القطعَ وَقَعَ على ملك المشتري، فالأرسُ له، وتصدق بما زاد على نصف ثمنه) : أي إن كان الأرسُ زائداً على نصف الثَّمن، فالزيادة لا تطيب له ، فوجب تصدقه ؛ إذ في الزيادة شبهة عدم الملك.

[1] قوله : فإذا طَرَا...الخ ؛ فإن قيل : يشكل على هذا الأصل ما إذا باع الغاصب ثم أدى الضمان ينقلب بيع الغاصب جائزاً ، وإنَّ طراً الملك البات الذي يثبت للغاصب بأداء الضمان على ملك المشتري الذي اشتراه منه، وهو موقوف. قلنا : إنَّ ثبوت الملكِ للغاصب ضروري ؛ لأنَّ الملك يثبت له ضرورة وجوب الضمان عليه، فلم يظهر في حقٌّ إبطال ملك المشتري ، كذا في «الكفاية).

Regarding what the son of Adam does not own: even if it is established in the Hereafter, it is established as based, and it is established from one aspect but not another. And for them (the two): ownership is established as suspended by an absolute transaction (tasarruf mutlaq) that is intended to confer ownership, so manumission is suspended dependent on it, like the manumission by the purchaser from the pawnbroker. Amr ibn Shu'ayb from his father from his grandfather reported that the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: 'There is no vow for the son of Adam regarding what he does not own, and no manumission for him regarding what he does not own, and no divorce for him regarding what he does not own.' His saying: 'And for them...' etc.; its summary: The ownership of the purchaser from the usurper is established as suspended by an absolute transaction intended to confer ownership, so manumission is suspended by its suspension and takes effect upon its taking effect, because it is among its rights, and when a thing takes effect, its rights take effect, and when it is suspended, its rights are suspended. And it becomes like the manumission by the purchaser from the pawnbroker of the pawned slave: it is suspended and takes effect upon the ratification of the mortgagee's sale by the pawnbroker, and like the manumission by the purchaser from the heir of a slave from an estate overburdened with debt: its effectiveness is suspended upon ratification by the creditors, like the manumission by the heir of a slave from an estate overburdened with debt: it takes effect if he pays the debt afterwards or the creditors release him. Contrary to if the purchaser from the usurper sells and then the owner ratifies the (first) sale: the second sale does not take effect, because by ratification a definitive ownership is established for the first purchaser, so when it overrides the suspended ownership of the second purchaser, it invalidates it. His saying: 'like the manumission by the purchaser' meaning he bought from the pawnbroker without the mortgagee's ratification, then manumitted, then the mortgagee ratified: his manumission takes effect. And if his (the slave's) hand is cut off, then (the sale) is ratified: the compensation (arsh) belongs to the purchaser, and he must give in charity what exceeds half its price. And if the purchaser from the usurper sells, then the first sale is ratified: the second does not take effect, because by ratification definitive ownership is established for the first purchaser, and when it overrides the suspended ownership of the second purchaser, it invalidates it. (And if his hand is cut off, then ratified: the compensation is for the purchaser): meaning the slave's hand was cut off, so he took its compensation, then the owner ratified the sale: his compensation is for the purchaser, because ownership became complete for him from the time of purchase, so it becomes clear that the cutting occurred while the slave was the purchaser's property, so the compensation is his; and he gives charity for what exceeds half its price: meaning if the compensation exceeds half the price, the excess is not lawful for him, so he must give it in charity, because in the excess there is a semblance of non-ownership. [0] [1] His saying: 'then when it overrides...' etc.: If it is said: this principle is problematic in the case where the usurper sells and then pays the guarantee, making the usurper's sale valid, and the definitive ownership that accrues to the usurper by paying the guarantee overrides the ownership of the purchaser who bought from him, which is suspended. We say: the establishment of ownership for the usurper is necessary, because ownership is established for him necessarily due to the obligation of guarantee on him, so it does not appear in the right of invalidating the purchaser's ownership, as in al-Kifaya. [2] His saying: 'invalidates it'; meaning the definitive ownership invalidates the suspended ownership, because it is not conceivable for the definitive and the suspended to coexist in one place, and once the sale is invalidated, ratification does not affect it. [3] His saying: 'then for him'; because the cause of ownership, which is the sale, was definitive itself, but the establishment of ownership was prevented by a hindrance, which is the right of the person from whom the property was usurped; so when it is removed, ownership is established from the time of the cause, because ratification at the end is like permission at the beginning, as in al-Nihaya. [4] His saying: 'what exceeds'; he restricted it, because he does not give all of it in charity, even though there is a semblance of non-ownership in it because it is guaranteed against him, unlike what exceeds. And in al-Kafi he elaborated: if it is not in his possession, then the excess is profit not yet guaranteed; if it is in his possession, then there is a semblance of non-ownership in it, as in al-Bahr. [0] His saying: 'because in the excess...' etc.; because ownership did not actually exist at the time of cutting, and the compensation...
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the ruling on acts (such as manumission, divorce, vow) concerning property that a person does not own. The author begins by stating the general principle: such acts are invalid because the person lacks ownership. He then cites the hadith of Amr ibn Shu'ayb, which explicitly states that there is no valid vow, manumission, or divorce for what one does not own. This is the primary proof (dalil naqli). The commentary then addresses a specific scenario: a person buys a slave from a usurper (ghasib) and then manumits him. According to Abu Hanifa's companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the manumission is suspended (mawquf) because the purchaser's ownership itself is suspended, dependent on ratification by the true owner. If the true owner ratifies the sale, the ownership becomes definitive from the time of purchase, and the manumission takes effect. This is analogized to the case of a purchaser from a pawnbroker (rahn) or from an heir of an estate overburdened with debt, where ratification by the mortgagee or creditors is needed. The passage also discusses a case where the purchaser from the usurper sells the slave to a second buyer, and then the true owner ratifies the first sale. In this situation, the second sale is invalidated because ratification gives the first purchaser definitive ownership from the start, which overrides the suspended ownership of the second purchaser. Another scenario: if the slave's hand is cut off and compensation (arsh) is taken, then the sale is ratified, the compensation belongs to the first purchaser (since ownership is retroactive), but any amount exceeding half the price must be given in charity due to a semblance of non-ownership. The commentary then addresses objections: e.g., if a usurper sells and then pays the guarantee, his ownership becomes definitive and overrides the purchaser's suspended ownership; but the response is that this is a necessary outcome, not a contradiction. The passage provides a detailed analysis of the interplay between suspended and definitive ownership in the context of third-party rights.

Key Terms
عتق (itq): Manumission; freeing a slave. The act of emancipating a slave, which requires ownership of the slave at the time of the act or valid ratification.
ملك موقوف (milk mawquf): Suspended ownership; ownership that is not fully effective but can become definitive upon fulfillment of a condition (e.g., ratification by the true owner).
إجازة (ijaza): Ratification; post-hoc approval of a transaction by the person who has the right to it (e.g., the true owner of property sold by a usurper).
غاصب (ghasib): Usurper; one who wrongfully takes and possesses another's property without permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person buys a slave from a usurper and then frees him, and later the true owner ratifies the sale, what is the status of the manumission?
A) It is absolutely invalid because the freed slave was not owned at the time of manumission.
B) It is suspended and becomes valid upon ratification because ownership is retroactive.
C) It is valid only if the freed slave was a male.
D) It is valid only if the purchaser pays compensation to the usurper.

Key Takeaway
Acts on unowned property are initially invalid, but in the Hanafi school they can become valid retroactively if the true owner ratifies the transaction, due to the principle of suspended ownership.

Compensation for Slave's Hand After Ratification
Ruling on compensation for slave's hand cut before ratification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on who receives compensation (arsh) for a slave's injury when the sale is ratified after the injury, and explain why the excess above half the price must be given in charity.

Core Idea
Ratification retroactively vests ownership; compensation belongs to the buyer, but excess above half price is tainted and must be given in charity.

In this lesson, we examine a nuanced scenario from the Hanafi school: a slave is sold but the owner has not yet ratified the sale. Before ratification, the slave's hand is cut off. After the injury, the owner ratifies. Who gets the compensation? The answer hinges on the powerful principle that ratification ('ijaza) is retroactive—it transfers ownership to the buyer from the moment of the contract. However, if the compensation exceeds half the price, the excess is considered problematic due to a semblance of non-ownership and must be given in charity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and core ruling
The scenario: a slave is sold, then his hand is cut off before ratification, then the owner ratifies. The ruling: the compensation (arsh) goes to the buyer, and any excess above half the price is given in charity.
The ruling is conditional on ratification occurring.
2
Understand the principle of retroactive ratification
Ratification ('ijaza) is akin to permission from the start. When the owner ratifies, ownership is established from the time of the original contract, not from the moment of ratification. This means the cutting occurred while the slave was effectively the buyer's property.
This is a key Hanafi principle: 'al-ijaza fi al-intiha' k'al-idhn fi al-ibtida'.
3
Apply the principle to determine compensation ownership
Since the buyer is considered the owner from the contract date, the injury happened on his property. Therefore, the compensation (arsh) is his right. The seller has no claim to it.
The compensation is assessed as the reduction in the slave's value.
4
Explain the charity rule for excess compensation
If the compensation exceeds half the sale price, the excess is deemed to have a semblance (shubha) of non-ownership. Even though the buyer owns the slave, the excess is not considered wholly permissible (tayyib) because at the time of cutting, the sale was not yet ratified and ownership was not fully established. Hence, the excess must be given in charity.
Al-Kafi distinguishes based on whether the excess is in the buyer's possession or not.
5
Examine the subsidiary case of resale to the usurper
If the buyer (first purchaser) then sells the slave to the usurper (the person who cut the hand), and then the first sale is ratified, the second sale is void. The first buyer's absolute ownership (mulk bat) retroactively nullifies the suspended ownership (mulk mawquf) that was transferred to the usurper. This is because absolute and suspended ownership cannot coexist in the same object.
An objection regarding the usurper's own subsequent payment of compensation is answered: the usurper's ownership is necessary due to liability, so it does not nullify the buyer's pre-existing right.

Source Text

Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage deals with the scenario where a slave is sold but the sale is not yet ratified by the owner, and before ratification, the slave's hand is cut off. The ruling: if the owner subsequently ratifies the sale, the compensation (arsh) for the hand belongs to the buyer, not the seller. This is because ratification retroactively establishes the buyer's ownership from the time of the original contract. Therefore, the cutting occurred while the slave was effectively the buyer's property, making the compensation his right. However, if the compensation exceeds half the price, the excess must be given in charity due to a semblance of non-ownership. The passage also addresses a subsidiary issue: if the buyer (first purchaser) sells the slave to the usurper, and then the first sale is ratified, the second sale is void. This is because the first buyer's ownership becomes absolute and retroactive, nullifying the suspended ownership that was transferred to the usurper. The text then provides responses to potential objections and nuanced explanations from various commentators.

Key Terms
أرش (arsh): Compensation paid for injury to a slave, assessed as the reduction in value.
إجازة (ijaza): Ratification of a contract by the owner, which retroactively validates the contract from its inception.
ملك موقوف (mulk mawquf): Suspended ownership that is not final until ratification, vulnerable to being nullified by a prior absolute right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Ratification retroactively vests ownership, making the buyer entitled to compensation for pre-ratification injury, but any excess above half the price must be purified through charity due to a semblance of non-ownership.

Invalidity of Evidence Due to Contradiction in Sales
Ruling on buyer's evidence to prove seller lacked authorization

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a buyer's evidence of lack of authorization is rejected under Hanafi law due to contradiction, and distinguish the case where the seller confesses before a judge.

Core Idea
Actions contradicting claims invalidate evidence—entering a sale admits its validity.

When a buyer purchases an item from a seller who is not the owner, the buyer later may claim the seller lacked authorization. But the law looks at the parties' actions: by voluntarily entering the contract, both buyer and seller implicitly confirm its validity. Therefore, any later evidence brought by the buyer to prove unauthorized is rejected as contradictory. However, if the seller himself confesses before a judge, the sale can be rescinded because a confession is binding on the confessor alone. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind these rules and their practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the buyer's attempt to bring evidence (bayyinah) that the seller lacked the owner's permission, intending to return the purchased slave. The ruling is that the evidence is not accepted (la tuqbal).
The buyer's claim is that the sale was invalid due to lack of authorization.
2
Locate the rational proof (dalil aqli)
The proof is the principle of tanaqud (contradiction): by entering the contract, both parties have already admitted its validity. The apparent state of a Muslim and sane person is to perform only valid contracts. Thus, a later contradictory claim is inherently flawed.
This is a qiyas-like reasoning but based on logical consistency, not a transmitted text.
3
Apply the principle of tanaqud
Because the buyer's purchase implies admission of the sale's validity, his later claim of invalidity contradicts his own prior action. The claim is therefore invalid (batil), and evidence supporting it cannot be accepted.
The same applies if the seller tries to bring evidence against the buyer—both are bound by their initial action.
4
Distinguish the confession scenario
If the seller himself confesses before a judge that he had no authority, the sale is rescinded. Confession (iqrar) is a restrictive proof (hujjah qasirah) binding only on the confessor, and it does not suffer from contradiction because a confession can be true even if earlier actions suggested otherwise.
The buyer can assist the seller in this confession, and they can mutually cancel the contract.
5
Note the practical outcome
The ruling prevents buyers from undermining contracts by later claiming defects they implicitly accepted. It upholds the stability of transactions based on apparent authority.
Other schools may have different rules; this is the established Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن اشترى عبداً من غيرِ سيّده فأقامَ بيِّنةً على إقرار بائعه أو سيده بعدم أمرِهِ مُريداً رده لا تقبل. ولو أقر بائعهُ به : أي بعدم أمر المالك بالبيع عند قاض به وطلب مشتريه رده رد بيعه) ، الفرق بين الصُّورتين : أنَّ البيِّنةَ لا تقبل إلا عند صحة الدعوى ، وفي المسألة الأولى لا تصحُ الدَّعوى للتناقض، وفي الصورة الثانيةِ : التَّناقض لا يمنعُ صحة الإقرار فللمشتري أن يساعد البائع في ذلك، فيتحقق الاتفاق بينهما. اليد الواحدة في «البحر» (۱) : نصفُ الدية، وفي العبدِ نصفُ القيمة، والذي دخل في ضمانه هو ما كان بمقابلة الثمن فيما زاد على نصف الثمن شبهة عدم الملك ، فيتصدق به وجوباً. قوله : فأقام ؛ أي بعد ما ادعى على البائع أنه أقر قبل البيع بأني أبيعُ بغير أمر مولاه، أو بعد البيع : بأني بعث بغير أمره، أو على المولى أنه أقر بعدم أمر البيع. قوله : لا تقبل بينته لبطلان دعواه بالتناقض، فإنَّ إقدامهما على العقدِ اعتراف منهما بصحته ونفاذه ؛ لأنَّ الظاهر من حال المسلم العاقل مباشرة العقد الصحيح النافذ، والبينة لا تبنى إلا على دعوى صحيحة، فإذا بطلت الدعوى لا تقبل، كما لو قام البائع البينة أنه باع بلا أمرٍ أو برهن على إقرار المشتري بذلك، فإنه لا يقبل. قوله : لا يمنع صحة الإقرار ؛ لأنَّ الإقرار حجّةٌ قاصرة، فلا تتعدى إلى الغير.
And whoever buys a slave from other than his master and brings evidence of the confession of his seller or the master that there was no authorization, intending to return it, it is not accepted. And if his seller acknowledges it: meaning the lack of authorization of the owner for the sale, before a judge, and his buyer requests its return, his sale is returned. The difference between the two scenarios: that evidence is only accepted when the claim is valid, and in the first case the claim is invalid due to contradiction, and in the second case, contradiction does not prevent the validity of the confession, so the buyer may assist the seller in that, thus agreement is realized between them. The single hand in al-Bahr: half the blood money, and for the slave half the value, and what enters into his guarantee is what was in exchange for the price, regarding what exceeds half the price, a semblance of non-ownership, so he must give it in charity. His saying: 'he brings' meaning after he claims against the seller that he confessed before the sale 'I am selling without the permission of my master', or after the sale: 'I sold without his permission'; or against the master that he confessed the lack of authorization for the sale. His saying: 'his evidence is not accepted because his claim is invalid by contradiction'; because their undertaking of the contract is an admission from them of its validity and execution; because the apparent condition of a Muslim and sane person is to undertake a valid and executed contract, and evidence is not built except on a valid claim, so if the claim is invalid, it is not accepted, just as if the seller brings evidence that he sold without authorization or mortgage based on the buyer's confession to that, it is not accepted. His saying: 'it does not prevent the validity of the confession'; because the confession is a restrictive proof, so it does not extend to others.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where a person buys a slave from someone other than the owner (i.e., a seller who claims to be authorized). After the sale, the buyer wants to return the slave by proving that the seller did not have the owner's authorization. The ruling states that if the buyer brings evidence (beyond a mere claim) that the seller or the owner confessed to a lack of authorization, that evidence is not accepted. Why? Because by entering into the contract, both parties have already acted in a way that implies the contract is valid; their own actions contradict any later claim that it was unauthorized. Thus, the buyer's claim is contradictory and invalid, so evidence in support is rejected. However, if the seller himself confesses (before a judge) that he acted without authorization, then the sale can be rescinded. This is because a confession is binding on the confessor alone and does not require a valid underlying claim; the buyer can assist the seller in this confession, and they can mutually agree to cancel the sale. The passage also briefly returns to the previous topic, linking it: For a single hand (in a free person), the compensation is half the blood money; for a slave, it is half the value; the excess over half the price must be given in charity due to the semblance of non-ownership.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically testimony of witnesses, required to prove a claim in court.
إقرار (iqrar): Confession or acknowledgment of a fact, binding on the confessor alone.
تناقض (tanaqud): Contradiction between one's actions and later claims, invalidating the claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is evidence of lack of authorization not accepted when brought by the buyer after the sale?
A) Because the seller did not confess
B) Because the buyer's claim is contradictory to his own act of purchasing
C) Because evidence is only accepted from the seller
D) Because the slave is not present

Key Takeaway
Evidence of lack of authorization is rejected if brought by the buyer because it contradicts his own act of purchase; only the seller's confession before a judge can invalidate the sale.

When Can a Buyer Use Admission of Unauthorized Sale to Return the Goods?
Ruling on buyer's evidence of admission that sale was unauthorized

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between when a buyer's evidence of unauthorized sale is accepted and when it is not, and explain the Hanafi rationale using the concepts of claim validity (da'wa sahiha) and limited proof (hujjah qasirah).

Core Idea
Evidence is only as strong as the claim it supports; a contradictory claim collapses its own foundation.

In Hanafi commercial law, the validity of evidence (bayyinah) hinges on the consistency of the underlying claim. This lesson examines a subtle distinction: when a buyer brings witnesses to prove that the seller admitted selling without the owner's permission, the evidence is rejected if it contradicts the apparent consent implied by the contract. However, if the seller himself admits this before a judge, the buyer can act on that admission. Understanding this distinction illuminates the interplay between da'wa (claim), bayyinah (evidence), and iqrār (admission) in Hanafi judicial reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage describes two cases: (1) the buyer brings witness evidence (bayyinah) that the seller or owner admitted the sale was unauthorized, and (2) the seller himself admits unauthorized sale before a judge. Note the different outcomes: evidence rejected in the first, admission accepted in the second.
Pay attention to who makes the statement: a third-party witness vs. the seller himself.
2
Analyze why the first scenario fails
The buyer's claim is deemed invalid due to contradiction (tanāquḍ). Why? Because both parties voluntarily engaged in the contract, which implies consent. The apparent state (zāhir al-ḥāl) of a Muslim is to contract validly. Since the claim contradicts this apparent reality, the claim is unsound, and evidence built on it is rejected.
This is a Hanafi application of istidlāl bi'l-ḥāl (inference from the apparent state).
3
Understand why the second scenario succeeds
When the seller admits (iqrār) that he sold without permission, the admission is a hujjah qāṣirah (limited proof) binding only on himself. Unlike bayyinah, iqrār does not require a valid external claim; it is an acknowledgement against one's own interest. The buyer can then support this admission, leading to agreement and rescission.
Iqrār is effective even if it contradicts the apparent state because it is self-implicating.
4
Contrast the two evidentiary mechanisms
Bayyinah operates on claims affecting others and requires consistency between the claim and observed reality. Iqrār operates only on the admitter's rights and does not require external consistency; contradiction does not invalidate it.
This difference explains why the same contradiction undercuts evidence but not admission.
5
Apply the rule to a similar case
Consider a buyer of a car from a non-owner. If the buyer brings two witnesses that the seller admitted the owner never permitted the sale, the court rejects this evidence because the contract itself suggests consent. But if the seller admits it in court, the buyer can return the car.
The principle is general for all sales of non-owned goods, not just slaves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن اشترى عبداً من غير سيده فأقام بينة على إقرار بائعه أو سيده بعدم أمره مريداً رده لا تقبل. ولو أقر بائعه به : أي بعدم أمر المالك بالبيع عند قاض به وطلب مشتريه رده رد بيعه) ، الفرق بين الصورتين : أن البينة لا تقبل إلا عند صحة الدعوى ، وفي المسألة الأولى لا تصح الدعوى للتناقض، وفي الصورة الثانية : التناقض لا يمنع صحة الإقرار" فللمشتري أن يساعد البائع في ذلك، فيتحقق الاتفاق بينهما. اليد الواحدة في «البحر» : نصف الدية، وفي العبد نصف القيمة، والذي دخل في ضمانه هو ما كان بمقابلة الثمن فيما زاد على نصف الثمن شبهة عدم الملك ، فيتصدق به وجوباً. [٢] قوله : لا تقبل بينته لبطلان دعواه بالتناقض، فإن إقدامهما على العقد اعتراف منهما بصحته ونفاذه ؛ لأن الظاهر من حال المسلم العاقل مباشرة العقد الصحيح النافذ، والبينة لا تبنى إلا على دعوى صحيحة، فإذا بطلت الدعوى لا تقبل، كما لو قام البائع البينة أنه باع بلا أمرٍ أو برهن على إقرار المشتري بذلك، فإنه لا يقبل. (٣) قوله : لا يمنع صحة الإقرار ؛ لأن الإقرار حجة قاصرة، فلا تتعدى إلى الغير.
And whoever buys a slave from other than his owner, then brings evidence upon the admission of his seller or the owner that there was no command (from the owner), intending to return (the slave), it is not accepted. And if his seller admits to that – meaning the lack of the owner's command for the sale – before a judge, and the buyer requests its return, his sale is returned. The difference between the two scenarios: evidence is only accepted when the claim is valid, and in the first case the claim is invalid due to contradiction, and in the second case contradiction does not prevent the validity of admission, so the buyer may assist the seller in that, thus agreement between them is realized. The single hand in al-Bahr: half the blood-money, and for a slave half the value; and what enters his guarantee is that which corresponds to the price in what exceeds half the price due to the suspicion of non-ownership, so he must give it in charity as an obligation. [1] His saying: 'so he brings' – meaning after he claimed against the seller that he admitted before the sale: 'I am selling without the command of his master', or after the sale: 'I sold without his command', or against the master that he admitted the lack of command for the sale. [2] His saying: 'his evidence is not accepted because his claim is invalid due to contradiction, for their proceeding with the contract is an admission from them of its validity and effectiveness; because the apparent state of a rational Muslim is direct engagement in a valid and effective contract, and evidence is only built upon a valid claim, so if the claim is invalid, it is not accepted, just as if the seller brought evidence that he sold without command or presented evidence of the buyer's admission thereof, it is not accepted. Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hanafi school regarding the acceptance of evidence (bayyinah) in a sale of a slave by someone other than the owner. The author distinguishes between two scenarios: (1) the buyer brings evidence (testimony) that either the seller or the owner admitted that the sale was without the owner's permission, intending to return the slave; such evidence is not accepted. (2) However, if the seller himself admits before a judge that he sold without permission, and the buyer requests return, the sale is rescinded. The commentator explains the rationale: in the first scenario, the buyer's claim is self-contradictory because both parties engaged in the contract, which implies consent; the apparent state of Muslims is to engage in valid contracts, so the claim contradicts that appearance. Evidence is only accepted when the underlying claim is valid. In the second scenario, the seller's admission is a limited proof (hujjah qasirah) that binds only himself, not others, so the buyer can act on it to return the slave. The passage also briefly mentions that if a slave loses a hand, the blood-money is half, and anything beyond half the price received by the buyer due to the suspicion of non-ownership must be given in charity as an obligation.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, usually testimony of witnesses, presented to prove a claim in court.
إقرار (iqrār): Admission or confession by a party against oneself, which is a binding proof limited to that party.
دعوى (da‘wā): A legal claim made by a plaintiff seeking a remedy.
تناقض (tanāquḍ): Contradiction, here referring to the inconsistency between the buyer's claim of unauthorized sale and the apparent consent implied by both parties engaging in the contract.
الظاهر من حال المسلم (al-zāhir min ḥāl al-muslim): The apparent state of a Muslim is to engage in valid, effective contracts; a legal principle used to assess the consistency of claims.
حجة قاصرة (hujjah qāṣirah): Limited proof that binds only the party making the statement (e.g., iqrār), not others.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The seller's admission was not in writing.
D) The slave was not present in court.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, bayyinah (witness evidence) requires a valid, non-contradictory claim, while iqrār (admission) is effective even if contradictory because it is a limited proof binding only the admitter.

The Precise Definition of Salam (Forward Sale) in Hanafi Fiqh
Definition and correction of Salam contract terminology

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the correct Hanafi definition of Salam (bay' ajil bi 'ajil) and explain why the phrase 'akhdh 'ajil bi ajil' is erroneous.

Core Idea
Salam is a sale of deferred goods for immediate payment — the price must be spot, the object deferred.

Salam (forward sale) is a vital contract in Islamic finance, enabling pre-payment for future delivery. However, its exact definition is often confused. This lesson clarifies the precise fiqh definition from the Hanafi commentary, correcting a common terminological error and setting the foundation for understanding its legal conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions presented in the text.
The commentator presents two definitions: one rejected ('taking immediate for deferred') and one accepted ('sale of deferred for immediate'). You must recognize both and understand their differences.
2
Analyze why the first definition is rejected.
The rejected definition (akhdh 'ajil bi ajil) is too broad — it would include a credit sale where the price is also deferred. The commentator labels it a corruption from copyists.
The critique is based on the principle that a definition must be exclusive and precise.
3
Recognize the correct definition.
The correct definition is 'bay' ajil bi 'ajil' — the sale of deferred goods for immediate payment. This uniquely identifies Salam, as the price is spot and the subject matter is deferred.
Note the semantic inversion: the object (ajil) is sold for the price ('ajil).
4
Understand the placement of the Salam chapter.
The commentator explains why the chapter on Salam is placed after general sales but before Sarf (currency exchange): because Salam requires only one spot receipt (the price), while Sarf requires two, making Salam simpler.
This structural placement reflects fiqh logic about required conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب السلم [١] قوله : باب السلم ؛ لما كان السلم من أنواع البيوع، ولكن شرط فيه القبض، كالصرف أخرهما وقدمه على الصرف ؛ لأن الشرط في الصرف قبضهما، وفي السلم قبض أحدهما ، فهو بمنزلة المفرد من المركب، والسلم بفتحتين : بيش وادن بها، ومنه : بيع السلم، كذا في «الصراح». وفي «المصباح المنير : السلم في البيع : مثل السلف وزناً ومعني، وأسلمت إليه : بمعنى أسلفت انتهى . والسلف درگذشتن ویدرامان درگذ شته ، اسلاف سلاف جمع ونوعي از بيع که بها بيشي وهندجون سلم، كذا في «الصراح». وعرفوه بعبارات : ١. منها : أخذ عاجل بآجل، وهذا فاسد ؛ لأن السلعة إذا بيعت بثمن مؤجل يوجد هذا المعنى، وليس بسلم ، ولو قيل : بيع أجل بعاجل، لكان أصوب، وقال في «البحر» : الظاهر أن قولهم : أخذ عاجل بأجل، تحريف من النساخ الجهلة ، فاستمر النقل على هذا التحريف. انتهى. قلت: هذه العبارة فيها تبديل مخل. فإن عبارة «البحر » ) هي : والظاهر أن قولهم أخذ عاجل بأجل من باب القلب، والأصل أخذ أجل بعاجل وهو أولى مما في «البناية» من أن قولهم أخذ عاجل بأجل تحريف من الناسخ الجاهل، فاستمر النقل على هذا التحريف. ٢. ومنها : هو بيع أجل بعاجل، ذكره في «الملتقى».
Chapter of Salam. [1] His saying: 'Chapter of Salam' – since Salam is one of the types of sales, but it includes the condition of (spot) receipt – similar to Sarf, he delayed it (i.e., placed it after other sales) and advanced it over Sarf because the condition in Sarf is receipt of both, and in Salam receipt of one, so it is like the simple from the compound. And Salam (with two fathahs) is a type of sale, from it: sale of Salam. So it is in al-Sirah. And in al-Misbah al-Munir: Salam in sale is like Salaf in weight and meaning, and 'aslamtu ilayhi' means 'aslaftu' (I gave as a loan). End. And Salaf is ... (Persian gloss). And they defined it with expressions: 1. Among them: taking immediate for deferred, and this is invalid because if a commodity is sold with deferred payment, this meaning exists, and it is not Salam. If it were said: sale of deferred for immediate, it would be more correct. And he said in al-Bahr: apparently their saying 'taking immediate for deferred' is a corruption by ignorant copyists, and the transmission continued on this corruption. End. I say: this expression contains harmful substitution. For the expression of al-Bahr is: apparently their saying 'taking immediate for deferred' is by way of inversion, and the original is 'taking deferred for immediate', and this is better than what is in al-Binayah that their saying 'taking immediate for deferred' is a corruption by an ignorant copyist, and the transmission continued on this corruption. 2. And among them: it is a sale of deferred for immediate, mentioned in al-Multaqa.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of the chapter on Salam (forward sale) in the commentary. The author first explains why the chapter on Salam is placed after other types of sales but before Sarf (currency exchange): like Sarf, Salam requires spot receipt (qabd) of one party, but Sarf requires receipt of both, so Salam is simpler and placed first. The lexical meaning of Salam is discussed, equating it with Salaf (loan/advance). Then the commentator critiques two definitions of Salam. The first definition, 'taking immediate for deferred' (akhdh 'ajil bi ajil), is rejected because it would also apply to a credit sale (where the price is deferred). The correct definition is 'sale of deferred for immediate' (bay' ajil bi 'ajil) i.e., the buyer pays the price immediately and the goods are delivered later. The commentator notes that the prevalent phrase 'taking immediate for deferred' is a copyist's error or an inversion, citing al-Bahr and personally critiquing the wording. The second definition from al-Multaqa is given as correct: 'sale of deferred for immediate'. This sets the precise legal definition of Salam as a sale where the price is paid on the spot and the subject matter is deferred.

Key Terms
سلم (salam): A sale where the price is paid immediately and the goods are delivered at a future date; a forward sale.
سلف (salaf): Synonym for Salam, both in weight and meaning; also means a loan or advance.
أخذ عاجل بآجل (akhdh 'ājil bi ājil): Taking immediate (price) for deferred (goods); an incorrect definition rejected by the author.
بيع أجل بعاجل (bay' ajil bi 'ājil): Sale of deferred (goods) for immediate (price); the correct definition of Salam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Taking immediate for deferred.
B) Sale of deferred for immediate.
C) A loan with interest.
D) Any transaction with delayed delivery.

Key Takeaway
Salam is defined as bay' ajil bi 'ajil: the sale of deferred goods for immediate payment, distinguishing it from credit sales where both sides may be deferred.
