Defining Salam: Forward Sale with Immediate Payment
Listing and critiquing six definitions of the Salam contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct definition of Salam and explain why the first definition is a corruption.

Core Idea
Salam is a sale of deferred goods for immediate payment; the common inversion of terms leads to fundamental misunderstanding.

The passage presents several definitions of Salam (forward sale) and critically examines the first, which had been corrupted by copyists. We will work through these definitions to extract the precise legal concept and understand the correction made by the commentators.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
Salam is a type of sale recognized as valid in the Hanafi school despite the general prohibition of selling what one does not possess, due to the need for farmers and traders.
It is a concession (rukhsah) with specific conditions.
2
Locate the Definition and the Critique
The author lists six definitions. The first ('taking immediate for deferred') is criticized as a corruption that inverts the correct order. Identify the error: the price is given immediately for the commodity which is deferred, not vice versa.
The correction is that the immediate payment is for the deferred commodity.
3
Trace the Correction
The author cites 'al-Bahr' and 'al-Binaya' to show that the original definition was 'taking deferred for immediate' or 'selling deferred for immediate'. The corrupted version mistakenly suggests the commodity is given immediately.
This correction is crucial for the proper understanding of the contract's temporal asymmetry.
4
Extract the Essential Elements
From the corrected definitions, the core elements are: immediate transfer of full payment at the contract session, and deferred delivery of a specified commodity. The seller owns the price instantly, the buyer owns the commodity only upon future delivery.
These elements distinguish Salam from other sales and loans.

Source Text

٤. ومنها : هو عقد يثبت الملك في الثمن عاجلاً، وفي المثمن آجلاً، ذكره العلامة العيني.

Chapter of Salam. [1] His saying: Chapter of Salam; since Salam is one of the types of sales, but the condition of possession is stipulated in it, like currency exchange, he delayed them (the chapters of exchange and Salam) and placed it before exchange, because the condition in exchange is possession of both, whereas in Salam possession of one of them is required, so it is like a singular from a composite. As-Salam (with two fathas): selling with advance payment, from which is 'bay' as-salam' (sale of Salam). In 'as-Sirah'. In 'al-Misbah al-Munir': as-Salam in sale is like as-Salaf in weight and meaning, and 'aslamtu ilayhi' means 'aslaftu' (I gave as a loan). End. Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on Salam (forward sale) in the fiqh manual. The author first explains the placement of this chapter after exchange (sarf) due to a common condition: in both, possession of at least one countervalue is required at the contract session, although in sarf both must change hands immediately while in Salam only the price must be handed over at the outset. He then presents two lexicographical definitions of Salam from 'as-Sirah' and 'al-Misbah al-Munir', equating it with Salaf. Six technical definitions follow, each attempting to capture the essence of Salam. The first definition ('taking immediate for deferred') is criticized as a corruption that inverts the correct understanding—the immediate payment is actually given for the deferred commodity, not vice versa. The author endorses the corrected version ('sale of deferred for immediate') and notes the confusion among copyists. The remaining definitions, cited from reliable authorities like al-Multaqa, al-Birjandi, al-Ayni, and ash-Shami, consistently emphasize the immediate transfer of price and deferred delivery of the commodity, with the seller owning the price instantly and the buyer owning the commodity only at the future date. The author does not single out one definition as definitive, implying that all are acceptable as long as they reflect this core temporal asymmetry.

Key Terms
السَّلَم (as-salam): A contract of sale where the price is paid in advance at the contract session and the goods are to be delivered at a specified future date.
السَّلَف (as-salaf): Synonym for salam, meaning forward sale or advance payment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best defines Salam according to the corrected definition in the text?
A) Taking something immediate for something deferred
B) Selling something deferred for something immediate
C) Exchanging a commodity for another on the spot
D) A loan without interest

Key Takeaway
The correct definition of Salam is a sale of deferred goods for immediate payment; the inverted version results from copyist error and must be avoided.

The Rukn of Salam and the Dispute over Using the Word 'Bay'
Establishing the pillar of Salam and the dispute over using 'bay' for its validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on using the word 'bay' for Salam, explain the opposing argument, and evaluate the Hanafi qiyas response.

Core Idea
Salam is valid with the word 'bay' because both are tamlik mal bi mal.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya' clarifies the pillar (rukn) of Salam and addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute: can Salam be concluded using the generic word 'bay' (sale)? The author affirms the most sound view (al-asahh) that it is valid, citing a narration from al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa. The opposing view, held by Zufar and Isa ibn Aban and also found in the Shafi'i school, restricts Salam to its specific wording because it is a contract contrary to qiyas.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The most sound view in the Hanafi school is that Salam can be validly concluded using the word 'bay' (sale) or 'ishtara' (buy).
This is a narration from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Hanifa, labelled 'al-asahh'.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Zufar and Isa ibn Aban (and the Shafi'i school) hold that Salam is not valid with the word 'bay' because it requires a specific wording such as 'aslamtu' or 'aslaftu'.
The text mentions 'Zufar and Isa ibn Aban said: It is not valid'.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
They argue that Salam is a contract established contrary to qiyas (as an exception to the prohibition of selling non-existent goods) and thus must be restricted to its specific terminology.
Qiyas here refers to extending the ruling of ordinary sale to salam, which they reject.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafis respond that both sale and Salam are tamlik mal bi mal (exchange of property for property). Sale is the genus, Salam is a species, so using the genus name is permissible by qiyas.
The analogy is likened to calling a specific human by the genus name 'insan'.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if someone uses 'bay' without specific Salam wording, the contract is valid according to the Hanafi asahh view, but invalid according to the opponents.
This affects everyday contracting when ordinary Muslims use common sale language for salam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وركنه : الإيجاب والقبول، بأن يقول المشتري : أسلمتُ اليك عشرة دراهم في كُرّ حنطة، وأسلفت، فيقول البائع : قبلت، وينعقد بلفظ البيع في الأصح، وهو رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة . وصورته : أن يقول : اشتريتُ أو بعتُ منك كُرَّبُرّ صفته كذا، بكذا إلى كذا ، على أن توفيه في مكان، وقال زفر وعيسى بن أبان : لا يصح، وهو وجة في مذهب الشافعي له ؛ لأنَّ السلم عقد ثبت على خلاف القياس بلفظ خاص، فلا يعدل عنه. ولنا : أنَّ كلاً من البيع والسَّلَم تمليك مال بمال، فيكون البيع اسم جنسه فيصاب به كما يصاب باسمه، كذا في كمال الدراية.
Its pillar: offer and acceptance, such that the buyer says, 'I have advanced to you ten dirhams for a kurr of wheat' or 'I have given as a salaf,' and the seller says, 'I accept.' And it is concluded with the word of sale (bay') in the most sound view, which is a narration of al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa. Its form is that he says, 'I have bought or sold from you a kurr of wheat with such-and-such description, for such-and-such price until such-and-such time, on condition that you deliver it in such-and-such place.' And Zufar and Isa ibn Aban said: It is not valid, and it is a position in the Shafi'i school, because Salam is a contract established contrary to analogy (qiyas) with a specific wording, so one should not deviate from it. And our proof: Each of sale and Salam is an exchange of property for property, so 'sale' is the name of its genus, and one may refer to it with that name just as one may refer to it with its specific name. As stated in Kamal al-Diraya.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage defines the pillar (rukn) of the Salam contract and discusses a key issue: whether Salam can be validly contracted using the word 'bai' (sale) or 'ishtara' (buy) instead of the specific terms 'aslama' or 'aslafa'. The author states that the most sound view (al-asahh) is that it is valid, and this is a narration from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Hanifa. The form of the contract is given as an example: the buyer says 'I have bought a kurr of wheat with such description for such price payable at such time, on condition of delivery at such place.' Two Hanafi authorities, Zufar and Isa ibn Aban, disagreed, holding that Salam is not valid with the word 'bai' because Salam is a contract established on the basis of a concession (rukhsah) and is contrary to qiyas; therefore it must be restricted to its specific wording. The Shafi'i school also held this view. The Hanafi response (lana) is based on qiyas: both sale and Salam are 'tamlik mal bi mal' (transferring ownership of property in exchange for property), meaning that sale is the generic name and Salam is a specific type. Just as one may refer to the genus by its name, one may also refer to the specific by the generic name. Therefore, using the word 'bai' is acceptable. This reasoning is cited from Kamal al-Diraya.

Key Terms
ركن (rukn): The essential pillar of a contract; here, offer and acceptance (ijab wa qabul) for Salam.
الإيجاب والقبول (al-ijab wa al-qabul): Offer and acceptance; the two components that constitute the rukn of any contract in Hanafi fiqh.
تمليك مال بمال (tamlik mal bi mal): Transferring ownership of property in exchange for property; the common genus of sale and salam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The most sound Hanafi view allows Salam to be concluded with the word 'bay' because both are fundamentally exchanges of property for property.

Legitimacy of Salam from Quran and Sunnah
Citing the Quranic verse and a hadith as proof for Salam's legitimacy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two textual proofs (Quran and hadith) for the permissibility of Salam and explain the legal significance of each proof.

Core Idea
Salam is legislated by both the Quran (Ayat al-Mudayanah) and the hadith of Ibn Abbas.

Some may assume that Salam is merely a customary practice with no scriptural basis. This lesson examines the explicit textual evidence that establishes Salam as a legitimate contract in Islamic law, as cited by the Hanafi tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling being proven
The passage states that Salam is 'mashru'' (legislated). Determine what this means for the legal status of the contract.
Mashru' indicates that the contract is permissible and recognized by the Lawgiver.
2
Locate the first proof: the Quran
The author cites 'Ayat al-Mudayanah' (Surah al-Baqarah 2:282). This verse deals with deferred debts and documentation.
The verse is general but includes Salam because Salam involves a deferred delivery.
3
Understand the Quranic indication
How does the 'verse of debt' support Salam? The verse commands writing down debts with a fixed term, which applies to Salam as a forward sale.
The Quranic proof is indirect but valid through general applicability (umum).
4
Locate the second proof: the Sunnah
Al-Hakim narrates from Ibn Abbas that he testifies Allah has permitted a guaranteed loan (salaf) to a specified term. This hadith directly mentions the permissibility of Salam.
This is a direct proof (nass) from the Prophet's statement.
5
Analyze the hadith's content
The hadith uses the word 'salaf' which is synonymous with Salam, and explicitly states it is 'made lawful by Allah'. This is a clear textual ruling.
The hadith is ahad but acceptable for establishing permissibility (mashru'iyyah).

Second, he quotes a hadith narrated by al-Hakim in his Mustadrak from Ibn Abbas, who testifies that Allah has permitted a guaranteed advance payment (salaf) for a specified term. This hadith supports the permissibility of Salam directly. The author does not elaborate further, but the implication is that Salam is a legitimate contract based on clear textual evidence.

Key Terms

مشروع (mashru'): Legislated; meaning the contract is established by Shariah as permissible and valid.

Salam Contract: Validity of Using 'Bay'' as a Wording
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the wording of salam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on using the word 'bay'' for salam and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute with its proofs and thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Salam can be validly concluded with the word 'bay'' because both are property exchanges; the generic term suffices.

In this passage, we encounter the classical Hanafi definitions of salam (deferred delivery sale), its pillar (offer and acceptance), and an important intra-Hanafi dispute: can salam be validly concluded using the word 'bay'' (sale) instead of 'aslamtu'? The source presents the majority view that it is valid, along with the dissenting opinions of Zufar and Isa ibn Aban. Understanding this dispute reveals the Hanafi emphasis on contractual meaning over literal wording.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
Recognize that the passage discusses the salam contract and specifically the ruling when it is concluded with the word 'bay'' instead of the specific salam formula.
The dispute is about validity, not about the contract's existence.
2
State the Hanafi majority position and its proof
The relied-upon view (al-asahh) from Abu Hanifa via al-Hasan holds that salam concluded with 'bay'' is valid. The proof is that both bay' and salam involve tamlik mal bi mal (transfer of property for property), so 'bay'' is a generic term that includes salam.
This is a qiyas jaliy argument.
3
State the opposing view and its proof
Zufar and Isa ibn Aban argue it is invalid because salam is a contract contrary to analogy (ghayr al-qiyas) and thus requires a specific word; deviating from that word invalidates it. This is also a view in the Shafi'i school.
They emphasize the exceptional nature of salam compared to regular sales.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response (lana) uses the common illa of property exchange to argue that 'bay'' is a generic name that can be applied to salam, just as it is applied to other sales. The contract's essence, not the wording, determines validity.
This reflects the Hanafi principle of classifying contracts by meaning (ma'na).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
The practical consequence: if a party uses 'bay'' instead of 'salam', the Hanafi position upholds its validity, while Zufar/Isa would invalidate it. The mufta bihi is validity, as indicated by the text's 'al-asahh'.
This shows how intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is resolved through tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها : بيع الشيء على وجه يوجب الملك للبائع في الثمن عاجلاً، وللمشتري في المثمن آجلاً، مسمَّى به ؛ لما فيه من وجوب تقدم الثمن، ذكره البرجندِي. و منها : هو عقد يثبت الملك في الثمن عاجلاً، وفي المثمن آجلاً، ذكره العلامة العيني. و منها : شراء أجل بعاجل ؛ لأنَّ السلم اسمٌ من الإسلام ذكره العلامةُ الشامي نقلاً عن القُهستاني. و منها : ما قال الشارح البارع . وركنه : الإيجاب والقبول، بأن يقول المشتري : أسلمتُ اليك عشرة دراهم في كُرّ حنطة، وأسلفت، فيقول البائع : قبلت، وينعقد بلفظ البيع في الأصح، وهو رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة . وصورته : أن يقول : اشتريتُ أو بعتُ منك كُرَّبُرّ صفته كذا، بكذا إلى كذا ، على أن توفيه في مكان، وقال زفر وعيسى بن أبان : لا يصح، وهو وجة في مذهب الشافعي له ؛ لأنَّ السلم عقد ثبت على خلاف القياس بلفظ خاص، فلا يعدل عنه. ولنا : أنَّ كلاً من البيع والسَّلَم تمليك مال بمال، فيكون البيع اسم جنسه فيصاب به كما يصاب باسمه، كذا في كمال الدراية.
Among them: Sale of a thing in a manner that obligates ownership for the seller in the price immediately, and for the buyer in the sold item deferred, named thereby; because of the requirement that the price be paid in advance, mentioned by al-Barjandi. And among them: It is a contract that establishes ownership in the price immediately, and in the sold item deferred, mentioned by al-Allamah al-Ayni. And among them: Purchase of deferred for immediate; because salam is derived from Islam, mentioned by al-Allamah al-Shami quoting al-Quhistani. And among them: What the excellent commentator said. Its pillar: offer and acceptance, such that the buyer says: I have salam to you ten dirhams for a kurr of wheat, and I have advanced, and the seller says: I accept. And it is concluded with the word 'bay'' in the most correct view, which is the narration of al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa. And for us: Both bay' and salam are transferring ownership of property for property, so bay' is a generic name and it is achieved with it just as it is achieved with its specific name, as in Kamal al-Dirayah.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the validity of using the word 'bay'' (sale) to conclude a salam contract. The text first presents several definitions of salam from different Hanafi scholars: Barjandi defines it as a sale where the price is immediate and the commodity is deferred; al-Ayni defines it as a contract with immediate price and deferred commodity; al-Shami defines it as buying deferred for immediate; and the excellent commentator (likely al-Marghinani) states its pillar is offer and acceptance. The accepted view (al-ashahh) is that salam can be concluded with the word 'bay'' or other words implying sale, which is reported from al-Hasan (al-Shaybani) from Abu Hanifa. Zufar and Isa ibn Aban, however, argue that salam is a contract established contrary to analogy (ghayr al-qiyas) and thus must use a specific word; using 'bay'' would be invalid. The Hanafi response (lana) is that both sale and salam involve exchange of property for property, so 'bay'' is a generic term that encompasses salam, just as it is used for other types of sale. This reasoning is based on qiyas jaliy (clear analogy). The dispute highlights the Hanafi principle that contracts are classified by their meaning (ma'na) not just by verbal formulas.

Key Terms
سلم (salam): A sale where the price is paid immediately and the commodity is delivered after a specified term (deferred delivery).
إيجاب وقبول (ijab wa qabul): The offer and acceptance that constitute the pillar (rukn) of the salam contract. The buyer makes the offer by saying 'Aslamtu ilayka...' and the seller accepts by saying 'Qabiltu'.
لفظ البيع (lafz al-bay'): Using the word 'sale' (bay') to conclude a salam contract, e.g., 'I bought from you...' instead of 'I gave salam...'

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because the Prophet used the word 'bay'' for salam.
C) Because all scholars agree that any word suffices.
D) Because salam is not a real sale.

Key Takeaway
Salam can be concluded with the word 'bay'' because the contract's essence is property exchange, and 'bay'' is a generic term that covers it.

Proofs for Salam: Quran, Sunnah, and Consensus
Proofs for the legality of salam contract.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three proofs for salam (Quran, Sunnah, consensus) and explain why a known term (ajal ma'lum) is a condition for its validity according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Salam is valid by istihsan despite qiyas, based on explicit texts and consensus, with a known term as an essential condition.

This lesson presents the classical Hanafi proofs for the legitimacy of the salam (forward sale) contract. The author cites the Quranic verse on debt (ayat al-mudayanah), two authentic hadiths specifying conditions, and the consensus of the Companions. The key point of dispute with the Shafi'i school—whether a known term is obligatory—is addressed, showing how the Hanafi school relies on explicit textual commands.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three sources of proof
The text explicitly mentions three sources: the Quran (ayat al-mudayanah), the Sunnah (hadiths from Ibn Abbas and Ibn Abi Awfa), and consensus (ijma). Note that the Quranic verse is interpreted by Ibn Abbas to include salam.
2
Examine the Quranic evidence
The verse on debt (2:282) is cited, and Ibn Abbas's testimony that it covers 'guaranteed lending to a specified term' is used as evidence. This indicates that salam, as a form of deferred debt, is included in the general command.
The hadith from al-Hakim is considered authentic but not recorded by Bukhari and Muslim.
3
Analyze the key hadith
The hadith from Ibn Abbas states: 'Whoever gives salaf, let him give salaf in a known measure, a known weight, and to a known term.' The Hanafis interpret the command 'to a known term' as a condition (shart) for validity, making salam without a term invalid.
The hadith is from Sunan al-Nasa'i, classified as ahad (zanni al-thubut) but clear in meaning (qati al-dalala).
4
Understand the qiyas objection and istihsan
Qiyas (analogy) would render salam invalid because it sells a non-existent item (ma'dum). However, the Hanafis set aside qiyas using istihsan (juristic preference) based on the explicit texts and consensus. This is a classic example of overriding qiyas with nass.
The text says 'but we have abandoned it due to the existence of explicit texts' – this is istihsan.
5
Recognize the inter-madhhab dispute
The Shafi'i school does not consider a known term obligatory; they view it as recommended or not required. The Hanafis argue that the hadith is explicit, and opposing it contradicts the clear text. The practical consequence (thamarah) is that a salam contract without a term is batil (void) in the Hanafi school.
The dispute is about the condition of ajal; Shafi'is may interpret the term as referring to the payment deadline or use qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو مشروع : 1. بالكتاب : وهو آية المداينة ، روى الحاكم في «المستدرك» في تفسير سورة البقرة ، عن ابن عباس قال : أشهد أنّ سَلَفَ المضمونِ إلى أجل مسمى قد أحلّه الله في الكتاب وأذن فيه ، قال الله : يَتَأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا إِذَا تَدَايَنتُم بِدَيْنٍ إِلَى أَجَلٍ مُسَمَّى فَاكْتُبُوهُ الآية ، وقال : حديث صحيح على شرط الشيخين، ولم يخرجاه، كذا في نصب الراية. 2. وبالسنة : وهو ما أخرجَهُ النَّسَائيّ في «سننه»: أخبرنا عبيد الله بن سعيد ، ثنا يحيى عن شعبة، عن عبد الله بن أبي المجالد قال: سألت ابن أبي أوفى عن السلف، فقال: «كنا نسلف على عهد رسولِ اللهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم وأبي بكر وعمر في البرِّ والشعير والتمر إلى قوم لا أدري أعندهم أم لا. وابن أبزى قال : مثل ؛ يعني ذلك السلم في الزبيب. وعن أبي المنهال قال : سمعت ابن عباس قال : «قدم رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم المدينة وهم يسلفون في التمر السنتين والثلاث فنهاهم وقال : من أسلف سلفاً فيلسلف في كيل معلوم ووزن معلوم إلى أجل معلوم. انتهى. أخرجه النسائي في «سننه»)، فقوله : إلى أجل معلوم من جملة شروطه صحة السلم، والحديث حجّةٌ على الشَّافِعِي ومن معه في عدم اشتراط الأجل، وهو مخالفةٌ للنص الصريح. وبالإجماع لأنهم أجمعوا على جوازه لحاجة الناس إليه، والقياس يأباه ؛ لأنَّ المسلم فيه يبيع وهو معدوم ، ولما كان بيع موجود غير مملوك، أو مملوك غير مقدور التسليم ليس بصحيح ، فبيع المعدوم أولى ، لكنَّا تركناه لوجود النصوص الصريحة على جواز ما نحن فيه من الكتاب والسنة.
And it is legislated: 1. By the Book: and it is the verse of debt. Al-Hakim narrated in al-Mustadrak in the exegesis of Surat al-Baqarah from Ibn Abbas: I bear witness that guaranteed lending to a specified term has been made permissible by Allah in the Book and He permitted it. Allah said: O you who believe, when you contract a debt for a specified term, write it down... the verse. And he said: a hadith authentic according to the condition of the two shaykhs (Bukhari and Muslim) but they did not record it, as in Nasb al-Rayah. 2. By the Sunnah: and it is what al-Nasa'i narrated in his Sunan: Ubaydullah ibn Sa'id told us, Yahya narrated to us from Shu'bah, from Abdullah ibn Abi al-Mujalid: I asked Ibn Abi Awfa about salaf, and he said: We used to give salaf in the time of the Messenger of Allah, Abu Bakr, and Umar for wheat, barley, and dates for people, I do not know if they had it or not. And Ibn Abza said: similar, meaning that salam for raisins. And from Abu al-Minhal: I heard Ibn Abbas: The Messenger of Allah came to Madinah while they were giving salaf for dates for two or three years, so he forbade them and said: Whoever gives salaf, let him give salaf in a known measure, a known weight, and a known term. End. Al-Nasa'i recorded it in his Sunan. So his statement 'to a known term' is among the conditions of the validity of salam, and the hadith is an argument against al-Shafi'i and those with him regarding not stipulating the term, and it is opposition to the explicit text. And by consensus: because they all agreed on its permissibility due to people's need for it, while analogy (qiyas) rejects it because the commodity sold in salam is non-existent. And since selling something existent that is not owned, or owned but not deliverable is not valid, then selling non-existent is even less valid, but we have abandoned (istihsan) it due to the existence of explicit texts on the permissibility of what we are discussing from the Book and Sunnah.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is a standard Hanafi proof for the legitimacy of salam (forward sale). It first cites the Quranic verse on debt (ayat al-mudayanah) and the interpretation of Ibn Abbas that it includes salam. Then it provides two hadiths: one from Ibn Abi Awfa describing the practice of salaf in early Islam, and a longer hadith from Ibn Abbas where the Prophet specified conditions for salaf (known measure, weight, and term). The text emphasizes that the condition of a known term is derived from this hadith, which serves as a refutation of the Shafi'i position that does not require a term (or views it as recommended). The passage also notes consensus on permissibility despite qiyas (analogy) because selling something nonexistent seems problematic. The Hanafi method is istihsan (juristic preference) based on clear texts and public need. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that nass (explicit text) overrides qiyas, and that exceptional contracts like salam are valid by specific evidence.

Key Terms
آية المداينة (ayat al-mudayanah): The verse on debt (Surah al-Baqarah 2:282) which is used as evidence for the permissibility of salam since salam is a form of deferred debt.
سلم (salam): A forward sale where the price is paid in advance for a commodity to be delivered later, with specific conditions of measure, weight, and term.
أجل معلوم (ajal ma'lum): A known, fixed term (time period) that must be specified in a salam contract for its validity.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning; here, qiyas would indicate that salam is invalid because it involves selling a non-existent item, but it is overridden by explicit texts (istihsan).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi position on the condition of a known term (ajal) in a salam contract?
A) It is a condition (shart) without which salam is invalid.
B) It is recommended but not obligatory.
C) It is not required at all.
D) It is only required for some commodities.

Key Takeaway
Salam is a valid contract by Quran, Sunnah, and consensus, but its validity hinges on specifying the measure, weight, and term; without a known term, it is invalid.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Conditions for Salam: Subject Matter Must Be Known in Quantity and Quality
Establishes the ruling that Salam is valid only for items whose quantity and quality are known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid Salam contract and identify which items are suitable for Salam according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Salam is valid only when the subject matter is known in quantity and quality.

Salam is a forward sale where the buyer pays the full price upfront, and the seller delivers a specified commodity later. The validity of this contract hinges on precise specification of the subject matter (muslam fih). This lesson examines the condition that the muslam fih must be known in quantity and quality, supported by the hadith of Ibn Abbas, and explores exceptions such as dirhams and dinars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions for Salam validity
From the text, the core condition: the muslam fih must be known in its quantity (kayl or wazn) and quality (sifah). This ensures determinacy and avoids dispute.
Quality can include type, grade, and other attributes.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The hadith 'Whoever gives a salaf, then let him give salaf in a known measure, a known weight, for a known term' is cited. In Hanafi usul, the imperative indicates obligation, so specifying quantity and weight is mandatory.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut), but accepted in muamalat.
3
Apply to examples of measured and weighed items
The text lists measured items (e.g., wheat, barley) and weighed items (e.g., saffron, musk). It also discusses cases of cross-specification (e.g., Salam in a measured item by weight) where the relied-upon view is permissible due to precision.
The permissibility is based on the possibility of accurate determination.
4
Distinguish exceptions: dirhams and dinars
Dirhams and dinars are athman (prices) and cannot be muslam fih because they are not determinate commodities. The contract is invalid if structured as Salam in these, though an intra-Hanafi dispute exists.
Consensus exists that one currency cannot be sold for another on Salam.
5
Resolve intra-Hanafi disputes on fulus and price
For fulus (copper coins), the two shaykhs permit Salam by number; Muhammad prohibits. For Salam in the price itself, 'Isa ibn Aban holds it batil; Abu Bakr al-A'mash reinterprets as a valid deferred sale. The relied-upon view follows 'Isa ibn Aban.
The tarjih authority is al-Marghinani and others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السَّلَمُ : بيع الشيء على أن يكون ديناً على البائع بالشرائط المعتبرة شرعاً، فالمبيع يسمَّى مُسَلَّماً فيه والثَّمَنُ رأس المال، والبائع مُسَلَّمَاً إليه، والمشتري ربُّ السَّلَم. (صح) فيما يُعْلَمُ قدره وصفتُهُ : كالمكيل، والموزونِ مُثمَّناً) : إِنَّما قال : مثمناً احترازاً عن الموزون الذي يكون ثمناً : كالدَّراهم والدنانير فإنَّهما أثمان فلا يجوز فيهما السلم. قوله : صح...الخ ؛ لقوله : «من أسلف سلفاً فليسلف في كـيـل مـعـلـوم، ووزن معلوم إلى أجل معلوم»، كما تقدَّم في حديث ابن عباس ؛ ولأنَّ ما لم يعلم قدره ووصفه فهو مجهول جهالة تفضي إلى المنازعة، فلا يجوز فيه السَّلم كما في سائر الديون. قوله : كالمكيل ؛ كالحنطة والشعير والسمسم والزيت والملح والتمر، وأما الزبيب فكيلي عند بعض المشايخ، ووزني عند آخرين، والموزون كالزّعفران والمسكِ والثوم والبص وغيرها ، فلو أسلم في المكيل وزناً ، كما إذا أسلم في البر والشعير بالميزان، فيه روايتان والمعتمد الجواز ؛ لوجودِ الضبط، وعلى هذا الخلاف لو أسلم في الموزون كيلاً ، كما صرح به في «البحر». قوله : فلا يجوز فيهما السَّلَم ؛ لأنَّ المُسَلَّم فيه لا بد له أن يكون مبيعاً متعيناً بالتعين، والدراهم والدنانير ليست كذلك ، ولو أسلم في الثمن يكون السلم باطلاً عند عيسى بن أبان ، وبيعاً صحيحاً بثمن مؤجل عند أبي بكر الأعمش له حملاً لكلامهما على الصحة بقدر الإمكان. وإن شئت التفصيل فارجع إليه وإلى «البحر » ) ، وهذا الخلافُ فيما إذا أسلم غير شيء من النقدين في أحدهما، وأمَّا إذا أسلم أحدهما في الآخر، فإنّه لا يجوز بالإجماع ؛ لأنَّ النقد بانفراده يحرِّمُ النَّسأ كذا في كمال الدراية. وأما الفلوس فلم تخلق للثمنية، فلو أسلم في الفلوس عدداً جاز، وهو ظاهرُ الرواية عن الشيخين، وعن محمد الله : أنه لا يجوز ، كذا في «الفتاوى المنصورية». وفي «الذخيرة»: منهم من قال جوازُ السَّلم في الفلوس، ذكره البرجندي.
Salam is a sale of a thing with the condition that it is a debt upon the seller according to the conditions considered legally. So the sold item is called muslam fih, the price is called ra's al-mal, the seller is called muslam ilayh, and the buyer is called rabb al-salam. (It is valid) in what is known in its quantity and quality: like measured and weighed items, but he said 'as a price (muthammanan)' as a precaution against weighed items that are prices: like dirhams and dinars, for they are prices, so Salam in them is not permissible. His saying: 'It is valid... etc.'; due to his saying: 'Whoever gives a salaf, then let him give salaf in a known measure, and a known weight, for a known term', as mentioned in the hadith of Ibn Abbas; and because what is not known in its quantity and quality is unknown with an ignorance that leads to dispute, so Salam is not permissible in it as in other debts. His saying: 'Like measured'; like wheat, barley, sesame, oil, salt, and dates. As for raisins, it is measured according to some mashayikh, and weighed according to others. And weighed items like saffron, musk, garlic, onions, and others. So if one makes Salam in a measured item by weight, as when one makes Salam in wheat and barley by the scale, there are two narrations, and the relied-upon is permissibility due to the existence of precision; and likewise the difference if one makes Salam in a weighed item by measure, as clarified in 'al-Bahr'. His saying: 'So Salam in them is not permissible'; because the muslam fih must necessarily be a sold item determinate by determination, and dirhams and dinars are not like that. And if one makes Salam in the price, the Salam is invalid according to 'Isa ibn Aban, and a valid sale with a deferred price according to Abu Bakr al-A'mash, carrying both statements to validity as much as possible. And if you want the detail, refer to it and to 'al-Bahr'. And this difference is when one makes Salam in something other than the two currencies in one of them. As for when one makes Salam of one of them in the other, then it is not permissible by consensus; because currency by itself prohibits delay, as in Kamal al-Diraya. As for fulus, they were not created for being a price (thimaniyya), so if one makes Salam in fulus by number, it is permissible, and that is the apparent narration from the two Shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf); and from Muhammad that it is not permissible, as in al-Fatawa al-Mansuriyya. And in al-Dhakhira: some of them said the permissibility of Salam in fulus, mentioned by al-Barjandi.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the conditions of validity for Salam (forward sale). The author first defines Salam: it is a sale where the sold item (muslam fih) is a debt upon the seller, with conditions set by Shari'ah. The buyer is called rabb al-salam, the seller muslam ilayh, and the price ra's al-mal. The core ruling: Salam is valid only when the quantity and quality of the subject matter are known. The proof is the hadith of Ibn Abbas: 'Whoever gives a salaf, then let him give salaf in a known measure, a known weight, for a known term.' The author explains that unknown items lead to dispute and are invalid, similar to other debts. Examples of measured items include wheat, barley, etc., and weighed items include saffron, musk, etc. There is a nuance: if one makes Salam in a measured item by weight, or a weighed item by measure, there are two narrations; the relied-upon view is permissibility due to precision, as per al-Bahr. An important exception: Salam is not valid in dirhams and dinars (gold and silver coins) because they are themselves prices (athman), and the muslam fih must be a determinate commodity. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: if one makes Salam in such a price, 'Isa ibn Aban says the contract is invalid, while Abu Bakr al-A'mash considers it a valid sale with a deferred price. The author notes that the first view (invalid) is more correct, as upheld in al-Hidayah and others. If one currency is sold for the other on Salam, it is invalid by consensus because currency itself prohibits delay. As for fulus (copper coins), they are not originally created as prices, so Salam in them by number is permissible according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), but Muhammad disagrees. The author refers to other sources for details.

Key Terms
السَّلَم (as-salam): A forward sale where the price is paid in advance and the goods are delivered later as a debt.
المُسَلَّم فيه (al-muslam fih): The commodity being sold on Salam, which must be specified in quantity and quality.
رأس المال (ra's al-mal): The price paid in advance by the buyer in a Salam contract.
مثمَّناً (muthammanan): A commodity intended for sale, not a price (thaman). Used to exclude dirhams and dinars which are prices.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is Salam not permissible in dirhams and dinars according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because they are not measurable or weighable.
B) Because they are themselves prices (athman) and cannot be determinate commodities.
C) Because they are valuable items.
D) Because Salam requires a known term, and money has no term.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires that the subject matter of Salam be known in quantity (measure or weight) and quality; dirhams and dinars are excepted because they are prices, not commodities.
